### [1] [Merging Methods for Multilingual Knowledge Editing for Large Language Models: An Empirical Odyssey](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13919)
*Kunil Lee,Ki-Young Shin,Jong-Hyeok Lee,Young-Joo Suh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates and analyzes vector merging approaches for multilingual knowledge editing, finding that vector summation with shared covariance is most reliable, TSVM offers limited interference reduction, and performance depends strongly on scaling and rank choices.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multilingual knowledge editing in large language models is difficult because edits in one language can interfere with others, and existing locate-then-edit methods that work in monolingual settings behave unpredictably across languages. There is a lack of systematic understanding of how different vector merging strategies affect multilingual interference and performance, especially under batch-editing scenarios.

Method: The authors systematically benchmark six vector merging variants for multilingual knowledge editing using two backbone LLMs, two base editing methods, and 12 languages on the MzsRE benchmark in a large-scale batch-editing setting. They particularly study Task Singular Vectors for Merging (TSVM) and analyze how weight scaling factors and rank compression ratios impact performance.

Result: Vector summation with shared covariance emerges as the most robust and reliable merging strategy, while naive summation without modeling shared covariance performs poorly. TSVM yields performance improvements in specific cases but only modestly reduces multilingual interference overall. The experiments reveal that both the scaling factor applied to weight updates and the rank compression ratio significantly affect outcomes, with larger-than-default scales and relatively low ranks often providing better performance.

Conclusion: Current vector merging methods for multilingual knowledge editing have clear practical strengths and limitations: shared-covariance vector summation is generally preferable, TSVM is helpful but not a complete solution to multilingual interference, and careful tuning of scaling and rank is crucial. These insights offer practical guidance and baselines for future research on more effective and robust multilingual knowledge editing techniques.

Abstract: Multilingual knowledge editing (MKE) remains challenging because language-specific edits interfere with one another, even when locate-then-edit methods work well in monolingual settings. This paper focuses on three issues: the effectiveness of vector merging methods for MKE, the extent to which Task Singular Vectors for Merging (TSVM) can reduce multilingual interference, and the influence of the weight scaling factor and rank compression ratio on performance. We evaluate six merging variants with two popular backbone large language models, two base knowledge editing methods, and 12 languages on the MzsRE benchmark under a large-scale batch-editing setting. Our results show that vector summation with shared covariance is the most reliable overall strategy, whereas simple summation without shared covariance performs poorly. TSVM improves performance in some settings, but its ability to mitigate multilingual interference is limited. We also find that performance is sensitive to both weight scale and rank ratio, with larger-than-default scaling and relatively low rank often yielding better results. These findings clarify the practical strengths and limits of current vector merging methods for MKE and provide guidance for future multilingual knowledge editing research.

</details>


### [2] [VectraYX-Nano: A 42M-Parameter Spanish Cybersecurity Language Model with Curriculum Learning and Native Tool Use](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13989)
*Juan S. Santillana*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduction of VectraYX-Nano, a 42M-parameter Spanish cybersecurity-oriented language model with native tool invocation via MCP, along with its corpus, architecture, training curriculum, and empirical findings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a lack of small, efficient, Spanish-native language models specialized in cybersecurity and capable of native tool invocation, particularly focused on Latin America. Existing models are typically English-centric, larger, harder to deploy on commodity hardware, and not tightly integrated with tool-use protocols like MCP. The paper aims to fill this gap with an openly released stack (corpus, training recipe, weights, benchmarks).

Method: The authors build a 170M-token Spanish cybersecurity corpus using an inexpensive eight-VM pipeline and partition it by domain (conversational, cybersecurity, offensive-security tooling). They train from scratch a 41.95M-parameter decoder-only Transformer with modern architectural components (GQA, QK-Norm, RMSNorm, SwiGLU, RoPE, z-loss, byte-fallback BPE). They apply continual pre-training with a replay buffer, followed by supervised fine-tuning on instruction-following, Q&A, and tool-use traces (MCP). They then run ablation experiments on corpus bootstrapping and LoRA-based tool-selection performance across different corpus densities and model scales, evaluating on a B1–B5 benchmark of tool-use tasks.

Result: Continual pre-training with replay yields steadily decreasing loss across stages. After SFT, the model achieves a conversational gating score of about 0.78 on average. Ablation shows an inversion in the relationship between loss and language register at this small (nano) model scale. LoRA experiments reveal that very low tool-selection performance (B4=0.000) stems from sparse tool-use examples in the corpus rather than fundamental model capacity limits; densifying the tool-use corpus raises B4 scores notably on both 42M and 260M models. The final GGUF model is compact (81MB) and achieves sub-second time-to-first-token on commodity hardware with llama.cpp.

Conclusion: A 42M-parameter, Spanish-native, cybersecurity-focused LLM with MCP-native tool integration can be trained cheaply and deployed efficiently on commodity hardware while achieving non-trivial conversational and tool-use performance. Corpus composition and density, especially for tool-use traces, are critical for tool-selection capabilities, and phenomena like loss-vs-register inversion may appear at small scales. The open release of corpus recipes, training scripts, model weights, and benchmarks lowers the barrier for future research and practical deployments in Spanish cybersecurity and related domains.

Abstract: We present VectraYX-Nano, a 41.95M-parameter decoder-only language model trained from scratch in Spanish for cybersecurity, with a Latin-American focus and native tool invocation via the Model Context Protocol (MCP). Four contributions: (i) Corpus: VectraYX-Sec-ES, a 170M-token Spanish corpus from an eight-VM pipeline (~$25 USD) partitioned into conversational (42M tokens, OpenSubtitles-ES, OASST1), cybersecurity (118M tokens, NVD, Wikipedia-ES, CVE mirror, security blogs), and offensive-security tooling (10M tokens, ExploitDB, HackTricks, OWASP) phases. (ii) Architecture: 42M-parameter Transformer decoder with GQA, QK-Norm, RMSNorm, SwiGLU, RoPE, z-loss, and a 16,384-token byte-fallback BPE. (iii) Curriculum with replay: continual pre-training with a replay buffer yields monotonic loss descent (9.80->3.17->3.00->2.16); after SFT on OASST-ES, Alpaca-ES, CVE Q&A, and 6,327 tool-use traces, the model attains a conversational gate of 0.78+-0.05 (N=4 seeds). (iv) Two findings: a bootstrap-corpus ablation reveals a loss-vs-register inversion at nano scale; a LoRA study shows the B4 tool-selection floor of 0.000 is a corpus-density artifact, not a capacity gate -- a tool-dense corpus (2,801 examples) raises B4 to 0.145+-0.046 on Nano 42M and 0.445+-0.201 on a 260M mid-tier. The GGUF artifact is 81 MB (F16), runs at sub-second TTFT on commodity hardware under llama.cpp, and is to our knowledge the first Spanish-native cybersecurity LLM with end-to-end MCP integration. Corpus recipe, training scripts, GGUF weights, and B1-B5 benchmark are released.

</details>


### [3] [Mistletoe: Stealthy Acceleration-Collapse Attacks on Speculative Decoding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14005)
*Shuoyang Sun,Chang Da,Hao Fang,Kuofeng Gao,Xinhao Zhong,Yi Sun,Fan Mo,Shu-Tao Xia,Bin Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper reveals and exploits a new vulnerability in speculative decoding for large language models, showing that an attacker can drastically reduce the efficiency gains (speedup) without visibly changing the model’s outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding speeds up LLM inference by having a smaller drafter model propose multiple tokens that are then verified by a larger target model. The efficiency relies on the average accepted length of draft tokens. However, the security and robustness implications of this mechanism, specifically whether an attacker can degrade efficiency without altering visible outputs, have not been thoroughly studied. The authors aim to investigate and demonstrate such a mechanism-level vulnerability.

Method: The authors introduce Mistletoe, an attack method that manipulates inputs or model behavior to reduce the agreement between the drafter and target models while constraining the target model to preserve its original output distribution. They formalize a joint optimization problem with two objectives: (1) a degradation objective that minimizes drafter-target agreement (reducing draft-token acceptance), and (2) a semantic-preservation objective that keeps the target model’s outputs semantically similar and with similar perplexity. To resolve conflicts between these goals, they propose a null-space projection mechanism that projects degradation gradients away from directions that change the target model’s semantics, thus selectively harming the acceptance mechanism without noticeably affecting outputs. They then evaluate Mistletoe on different speculative decoding setups.

Result: Across multiple speculative decoding systems and settings, Mistletoe significantly reduces the average accepted length τ of draft tokens, which in turn collapses the speedup that speculative decoding normally provides and lowers the effective token throughput. Despite this attack-induced slowdown, the target model’s output quality and perplexity remain largely unchanged, demonstrating that the attack is stealthy at the level of observable outputs.

Conclusion: Speculative decoding, while effective for accelerating LLM inference, introduces a distinct mechanism-level attack surface that existing robustness measures focused on output quality do not cover. The Mistletoe attack shows that adversaries can selectively and stealthily collapse the efficiency benefits of speculative decoding without degrading the visible outputs. This finding motivates the need for more robust designs and defenses for LLM acceleration mechanisms, not just for their outputs.

Abstract: Speculative decoding has become a widely adopted technique for accelerating large language model (LLM) inference by drafting multiple candidate tokens and verifying them with a target model in parallel. Its efficiency, however, critically depends on the average accepted length $τ$, i.e., how many draft tokens survive each verification step. In this work, we identify a new mechanism-level vulnerability in model-based speculative decoding: the drafter is trained to approximate the target model distribution, but this approximation is inevitably imperfect. Such a drafter-target mismatch creates a hidden attack surface where small perturbations can preserve the target model's visible behavior while substantially reducing draft-token acceptability. We propose Mistletoe, a stealthy acceleration-collapse attack against speculative decoding. Mistletoe directly targets the acceptance mechanism of speculative decoding. It jointly optimizes a degradation objective that decreases drafter-target agreement and a semantic-preservation objective that constrains the target model's output distribution. To resolve the conflict between these objectives, we introduce a null-space projection mechanism, where degradation gradients are projected away from the local semantic-preserving direction, suppressing draft acceptance while minimizing semantic drift. Experiments on various speculative decoding systems show that Mistletoe substantially reduces average accepted length $τ$, collapses speedup, and lowers averaged token throughput, while preserving output quality and perplexity. Our work highlights that speculative decoding introduces a mechanism-level attack surface beyond existing output robustness, calling for more robust designs of LLM acceleration systems.

</details>


### [4] [Physics-R1: An Audited Olympiad Corpus and Recipe for Visual Physics Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14040)
*Shan Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper audits multimodal physics evaluation pipelines and reveals hidden issues in dataset construction and evaluation formats that inflate perceived model performance, then introduces new audited corpora and a reference RL-tuning recipe that substantially but not fully closes the gap to top proprietary models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current multimodal physics benchmarks and training resources are assumed to be clean and reliable measures of vision-language reasoning, but the authors suspect they are compromised by subtle train-eval contamination, translation artifacts, and overly easy MCQ formats, leading to misleadingly high scores and poor generalization to real olympiad-style problems.

Method: They perform an end-to-end audit of public multimodal physics training pools and evaluations using a three-stage near-duplicate/paraphrase detection pipeline (5-gram Jaccard, embedding cosine with mxbai-embed-large, and Haiku-4.5 LLM judging). They quantify translation-induced difficulty shifts via paired Estonian-English olympiad problems tested with Sonnet 4.5 and standard statistical tests. They measure format and novelty effects by comparing the same model on MCQ PhyX versus a new, open-ended olympiad benchmark. Finally, they construct several new audited corpora and train a physics-specialized model, Physics-R1, using a GSPO+DAPO reinforcement learning recipe on Qwen3-VL-8B-Thinking, evaluating across multiple benchmarks and seeds.

Result: The three-stage audit reveals extensive hidden train-eval leakage, with SciInstruct alone containing 134 near-duplicates and 4,846 paraphrase candidates relative to six public physics evals, despite passing naive 5-gram Jaccard checks. Sonnet 4.5 performs 17 percentage points better on Estonian than on English versions of the same olympiad problems, showing translation drift significantly affects difficulty and evaluation comparability. The same Sonnet weights show a 46-point performance drop moving from saturated MCQ (79.7% on PhyX) to open-ended olympiad evaluation (33.4% on PhysOlym-A), exposing MCQ saturation and format sensitivity. Their Physics-R1 model, trained on the audited corpora, improves substantially over the 8B base model on multiple physics benchmarks (e.g., +18.3 pp on PhysOlym-A, +15.7 pp on PhysReason, +6.9 pp on OlympiadBench-Physics, +4.1 pp on PhyX), even surpassing larger and proprietary models on some tasks but still trailing Sonnet 4.5 on the hardest benchmark.

Conclusion: Multimodal physics evaluation practices are significantly compromised by undetected train-eval contamination, translation-induced difficulty drift, and over-simplified MCQ formats, meaning current benchmarks overstate true vision-language reasoning ability. More rigorous pipeline audits and harder, open-ended, source-novel benchmarks are necessary. The authors’ released corpora, benchmarks (especially PhysOlym-A), and the Physics-R1 training recipe provide a more reliable foundation for measuring and improving scientific reasoning in multimodal models, though there remains a performance gap to state-of-the-art proprietary systems.

Abstract: We audit the multimodal-physics evaluation pipeline end-to-end and document three undetected construction practices that distort how the field measures vision-language reasoning: train-eval contamination, translation drift, and MCQ saturation. (1) Public training pools (UGPhysics-Train, SciInstruct, MMK12) pass single-stage 5-gram-Jaccard audits with zero hits across all six public physics evals; a three-stage audit (Jaccard -> mxbai-embed-large cosine -> Haiku-4.5 LLM-judge) surfaces 134 near-duplicates and 4,846 paraphrase candidates in SciInstruct alone. (2) A 17-pp Sonnet 4.5 delta on 59 paired Estonian-English olympiad problems (30.5% vs. 13.6%; sign test p=0.011, McNemar p=0.021, paired bootstrap 95% CI [+5.1, +28.9] pp). (3) A 46-pp format-and-novelty gradient on identical Sonnet weights between MCQ (79.7% on PhyX) and open-ended olympiad evaluation (33.4% on PhysOlym-A). We release four artifacts addressing these gaps: PhysCorp-A (6,432-record three-stage-audited multimodal corpus), PhysR1Corp (2,268-record closed-form RL pool), PhysOlym-A (500-problem, 99.8% novel-source held-out olympiad eval with native difficulty labels and an EN/ET bilingual subset), and Physics-R1, a reference GSPO+DAPO recipe cold-started from Qwen3-VL-8B-Thinking. Across 3 seeds, Physics-R1 lifts the audited corpus over the 8B base by +18.3 pp on PhysOlym-A liberal (8.0 -> 26.3 +/- 1.7; 7.1 pp behind Sonnet 4.5), +15.7 pp on PhysReason (23.9 -> 39.6 +/- 6.4; ahead of Qwen3-VL-32B and Gemini 2.5 Pro), +6.9 pp on OlympiadBench-Physics (46.2 +/- 1.5), and +4.1 pp on PhyX MCQ (77.8 +/- 0.3).

</details>


### [5] [Derivation Prompting: A Logic-Based Method for Improving Retrieval-Augmented Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14053)
*Ignacio Sastre,Guillermo Moncecchi,Aiala Rosá*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces Derivation Prompting, a logic-inspired prompting technique for Retrieval-Augmented Generation that builds interpretable derivation trees and reduces hallucinations in domain-specific QA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs in knowledge-intensive, domain-specific QA suffer from hallucinations and faulty reasoning, even with standard RAG or long-context methods. There is a need for methods that not only improve answer correctness but also provide interpretable and controllable reasoning processes.

Method: Proposes Derivation Prompting, where the model mimics logic derivations by starting from initial hypotheses and repeatedly applying predefined rules to derive conclusions. This process builds a derivation tree that structures and constrains generation. It is plugged into the generation step of the RAG framework and compared against standard RAG and long-context baselines in a case study.

Result: In the case study, Derivation Prompting significantly reduced the rate of unacceptable answers relative to both traditional RAG and long-context window approaches, suggesting better control over hallucinations and reasoning errors.

Conclusion: Derivation Prompting is an effective, logic-inspired enhancement to RAG for domain-specific QA, yielding more reliable and interpretable outputs by structuring generation as a derivation process and demonstrably reducing unacceptable answers.

Abstract: The application of Large Language Models to Question Answering has shown great promise, but important challenges such as hallucinations and erroneous reasoning arise when using these models, particularly in knowledge-intensive, domain-specific tasks. To address these issues, we introduce Derivation Prompting, a novel prompting technique for the generation step of the Retrieval-Augmented Generation framework. Inspired by logic derivations, this method involves deriving conclusions from initial hypotheses through the systematic application of predefined rules. It constructs a derivation tree that is interpretable and adds control over the generation process. We applied this method in a specific case study, significantly reducing unacceptable answers compared to traditional RAG and long-context window methods.

</details>


### [6] [PEML: Parameter-efficient Multi-Task Learning with Optimized Continuous Prompts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14055)
*Anjir Ahmed Chowdhury,Syed Zawad,Xiaolong Ma,Xu Dong,Feng Yan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes PEML, a parameter-efficient multi-task learning method that jointly optimizes continuous prompts and low-rank weight adaptations, achieving notable accuracy gains over existing PEFT-based multi-task baselines on GLUE, SuperGLUE, MMLU, and commonsense benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard PEFT methods like LoRA and Prefix Tuning are typically designed for single-task adaptation of large language models and are suboptimal when a single LLM must serve many tasks. Multi-task fine-tuning can share knowledge and reduce overall data and deployment costs, but current methods either only adjust model weights (e.g., LoRA) or use overly simple prompt architectures (e.g., Prefix Tuning), limiting adaptation capacity and efficiency.

Method: The authors introduce PEML (Parameter-Efficient Multi-task Learning), which jointly performs (1) neural architecture engineering for optimizing continuous prompts and (2) low-rank adaptation of model weights (in the spirit of LoRA). They build an automated framework that searches/optimizes the prompt architecture and its parameters while simultaneously learning low-rank updates to the base LLM for multiple tasks in a unified setup.

Result: Across GLUE, SuperGLUE, MMLU, and commonsense reasoning benchmarks, PEML outperforms state-of-the-art multi-task PEFT baselines such as MTL-LoRA, MultiLoRA, C-Poly, and MoE. The method yields up to 6.67% average accuracy improvement, with some individual tasks seeing gains as high as 10.75%.

Conclusion: Jointly optimizing prompts and low-rank model adaptations is more effective for multi-task PEFT than focusing on either component alone. PEML provides a practical, parameter-efficient way to adapt a single LLM to many tasks, achieving substantial performance improvements over existing multi-task PEFT approaches while maintaining efficiency advantages over full fine-tuning or multiple separate models.

Abstract: Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning (PEFT) is widely used for adapting Large Language Models (LLMs) for various tasks. Recently, there has been an increasing demand for fine-tuning a single LLM for multiple tasks because it requires overall less data for fine-tuning thanks to the common features shared among tasks. More importantly, LLMs are resource demanding and deploying a single model for multiple tasks facilitates resource consolidation and consumes significantly less resources compared to deploying individual large model for each task. Existing PEFT methods like LoRA and Prefix Tuning are designed to adapt LLMs to a specific task. LoRA and its variation focus on aligning the model itself for tasks, overlooking the importance of prompt tuning in multi-task learning while Prefix Tuning only adopts a simple architecture to optimize prompts, which limits the adaption capabilities for multi-task. To enable efficient fine-tuning for multi-task learning, it is important to co-optimize prompt optimization and model adaptation. In this work, we propose a Parameter-Efficient Multi-task Learning (\PM), which employs a neural architecture engineering method for optimizing the continuous prompts while also performing low-rank adaption for model weights. We prototype PEML by creating an automated framework for optimizing the continuous prompts and adapting model weights. We evaluate PEML against state-of-the-arts multi-task learning methods MTL-LoRA, MultiLoRa, C-Poly, and MoE, on the GLUE, SuperGLUE, Massive Multitask Language Understanding, and commonsense reasoning benchmarks. The evaluation results present an average accuracy improvement of up to 6.67%, with individual tasks showing peak gains of up to 10.75%.

</details>


### [7] [Dual Hierarchical Dialogue Policy Learning for Legal Inquisitive Conversational Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14057)
*Xubo Lin,Zezhii Deng,Shihao Wang,Grace Hui Yang,Yang Deng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Inquisitive Conversational Agents (ICAs) that proactively ask questions to achieve their own objectives, and proposes a dual hierarchical reinforcement learning framework tailored to U.S. Supreme Court oral arguments, outperforming baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most dialogue systems are reactive and user-driven, which is insufficient in high-stakes settings like legal proceedings where an agent must proactively gather information and pursue its own goals, such as clarifying legal issues and uncovering crucial facts.

Method: The authors define Inquisitive Conversational Agents and build one for U.S. Supreme Court oral arguments using a Dual Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning architecture with two cooperating RL agents. One agent handles high-level dialogue management (when and what kind of question to ask), and the other handles low-level, fine-grained utterance generation (how to phrase the question). The system is trained to emulate judicial questioning strategies and to ask probing questions that reveal legally relevant information.

Result: On a dataset of U.S. Supreme Court oral arguments, the proposed ICA with Dual Hierarchical RL outperforms multiple baseline dialogue systems across several evaluation metrics that capture both effectiveness of information elicitation and dialogue quality.

Conclusion: Dual Hierarchical RL for inquisitive, goal-driven dialogue enables agents to strategically ask questions and uncover key information, offering better performance than standard reactive systems and marking an initial step toward broader deployment of proactive agents in high-stakes, domain-specific contexts like law.

Abstract: Most existing dialogue systems are user-driven, primarily designed to fulfill user requests. However, in many critical real-world scenarios, a conversational agent must proactively extract information to achieve its own objectives rather than merely respond. To address this gap, we introduce \emph{Inquisitive Conversational Agents (ICAs)} and develop an ICA specifically tailored to U.S. Supreme Court oral arguments. We propose a Dual Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning framework featuring two cooperating RL agents, each with its own policy, to coordinate strategic dialogue management and fine-grained utterance generation. By learning when and how to ask probing questions, the agent emulates judicial questioning patterns and systematically uncovers crucial information to fulfill its legal objectives. Evaluations on a U.S. Supreme Court dataset show that our method outperforms various baselines across multiple metrics. It represents an important first step toward broader high-stakes, domain-specific applications.

</details>


### [8] [Measuring and Mitigating Toxicity in Large Language Models: A Comprehensive Replication Study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14087)
*Mokshit Surana,Archit Rathod,Akshaj Satishkumar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Replication study of DExperts shows it greatly reduces explicit toxicity in LLM outputs but is less robust to implicit hate speech and adds substantial latency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs trained on web data often generate toxic or hateful content, even from harmless prompts, which is risky for deployment. Existing mitigation should ideally be effective, not require retraining, preserve utility, and be computationally feasible. The paper aims to rigorously replicate and assess DExperts, a decoding-time mitigation method, to understand its true safety benefits, robustness (especially to implicit hate), and practical costs.

Method: The authors conduct a three-phase empirical replication. (1) They measure baseline toxicity of standard GPT-2 models using the RealToxicityPrompts benchmark. (2) They implement DExperts, an inference-time steering technique that combines a base model with expert and anti-expert models during decoding, and evaluate its ability to mitigate explicit toxicity on the same benchmark. (3) They stress-test DExperts on adversarial, implicit hate speech using the ToxiGen dataset to probe robustness. They also measure latency per generation to assess computational overhead.

Result: DExperts nearly eliminates explicit toxicity on RealToxicityPrompts, reaching about 100% safety. However, on adversarial implicit hate speech in ToxiGen, safety drops to about 98.5%, revealing brittleness to more subtle, indirect toxicity. In addition, DExperts incurs a roughly 10x increase in generation latency, from around 0.2 seconds to 2.0 seconds per output, indicating significant computational overhead.

Conclusion: Decoding-time expert steering (DExperts) is highly effective for reducing explicit toxic outputs but less robust against subtle, implicit hate, and it substantially slows inference. There is a clear robustness gap between explicit and implicit toxicity mitigation, and the latency cost challenges real-time deployment. The paper argues that future safety methods must better generalize across diverse, adversarial hate patterns while avoiding prohibitive computational costs.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs), when trained on web-scale corpora, inherently absorb toxic patterns from their training data. This leads to ``toxic degeneration'' where even innocuous prompts can trigger harmful outputs. This phenomenon poses significant risks for real-world deployments. Thus, necessitating effective mitigation strategies that should maintain model utility while ensuring safety. In this comprehensive replication study, we evaluate the efficacy of \textbf{DExperts} (Decoding-time Experts), which is an inference-time mitigation technique that steers generation without requiring model retraining. We structured our research into three systematic phases: (1) establishing baseline toxicity measurements using \textbf{RealToxicityPrompts} on standard GPT-2 models; then (2) implementing and evaluating DExperts to mitigate explicit toxicity; and finally (3) stress-testing the method against implicit hate speech using the adversarial \textbf{ToxiGen} dataset. Our empirical results confirm that while DExperts achieves near-perfect safety rates (100\%) on explicit toxicity benchmarks, it exhibits brittleness against adversarial, implicit hate speech, with safety rates dropping to 98.5\%. Furthermore, we quantify a critical trade-off. The method introduces a $\sim$10x latency penalty (from 0.2s to 2.0s per generation), posing challenges for real-time deployment scenarios. This study contributes to the growing body of work on AI safety by highlighting the robustness gap between explicit and implicit toxicity mitigation. We emphasize the need for more sophisticated approaches that generalize across diverse hate speech patterns without prohibitive computational costs.

</details>


### [9] [When Evidence Conflicts: Uncertainty and Order Effects in Retrieval-Augmented Biomedical Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14115)
*Yikun Han,Mengfei Lan,Halil Kilicoglu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how biomedical retrieval-augmented LLMs behave when given incomplete, misleading, or conflicting evidence, and proposes a conflict-aware abstention method to improve reliability under such conditions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of biomedical RAG systems mostly test accuracy when the retrieved context is helpful, but real-world biomedical literature is often incomplete, misleading, or internally contradictory. There is a need to understand and improve LLM reliability when evidence conflicts rather than assuming ideal retrieval.

Method: The authors introduce HealthContradict, an evaluation setup with five controlled evidence conditions (no context, correct-only, incorrect-only, incorrect-first mixed, correct-first mixed). They test six open-weight LLMs under these conditions and measure accuracy and prediction flips when the order of conflicting documents is reversed. They also design and evaluate a conflict-aware abstention score that integrates model confidence with a conflict detector over the retrieved evidence.

Result: Across all models, reversing the order of the same two contradictory documents leads to accuracy drops and 11.4%–25.2% of predictions flipping, indicating strong order sensitivity and instability. The proposed conflict-aware abstention score significantly improves selective accuracy over using confidence-only in challenging settings, with average gains of 7.2–33.4 points in the incorrect-only condition and 3.6–14.4 points in the incorrect-first conflicting condition at coverage levels of 75%, 50%, and 25%.

Conclusion: Biomedical RAG LLMs are brittle in the presence of conflicting evidence, with outputs strongly influenced by evidence order, framing this as both an uncertainty and robustness issue. Explicitly modeling and detecting evidence disagreement, and using it to drive abstention, can substantially improve reliability, suggesting that future evaluation and deployment of biomedical LLMs should incorporate conflict-aware methods.

Abstract: Biomedical retrieval-augmented large language models (LLMs) often face evidence that is incomplete, misleading, or internally contradictory, yet evaluation usually emphasizes answer accuracy under helpful context rather than reliability under conflict. Using HealthContradict, we evaluate six open-weight LLMs under five controlled evidence conditions: no retrieved context, correct-only context, incorrect-only context, and two mixed conditions containing both correct and contradictory documents in opposite orders. In this conflicting-evidence order contrast, where the same two documents are both present and only their order is reversed, accuracy drops for every model and 11.4%--25.2% of predictions flip. To support abstention in these difficult cases, we also evaluate a conflict-aware abstention score that combines model confidence with a detector of evidence conflict. In the two hardest conditions, this score improves selective accuracy over confidence-only, with mean gains of 7.2--33.4 points in incorrect-only (`IC') and 3.6--14.4 points in incorrect-first conflicting (`ICC') conditions across 75%, 50%, and 25% coverage. These results show that conflicting biomedical evidence is both an uncertainty and robustness problem and motivate evaluation and abstention methods that explicitly account for evidence disagreement.

</details>


### [10] [Generative Floor Plan Design with LLMs via Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Rewards](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14117)
*Luis Lara,Aristides Milios,Zhi Hao Luo,Aditya Sharma,Ge Ya Luo,Christopher Beckham,Florian Golemo,Christopher Pal*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose an LLM-based, text-driven floor plan generator that uses reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards to satisfy both connectivity and strict numerical constraints (dimensions/areas), outperforming prior methods on realism and constraint satisfaction.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing floor plan generation methods, including LLM-based ones, mainly ensure room connectivity but fail to reliably satisfy quantitative numerical constraints like exact room dimensions and areas, which are crucial for professional design quality and usability. There is a need for a generative system that jointly respects topology, numeric constraints, and aesthetic/functional quality, with systematic metrics for constraint adherence.

Method: They fine-tune a large language model on real-world floor plans represented in text, then apply reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) to optimize the model for adherence to both topological (connectivity) and numerical (dimensions, areas) constraints, while penalizing invalid or overlapping layouts. They also design quantitative constraint adherence metrics to evaluate generated plans against user-specified requirements.

Result: The proposed model generates floor plans that more faithfully satisfy user-specified connectivity and numerical constraints than existing approaches, and achieves superior performance on Realism, Compatibility, and Diversity metrics. It yields at least a 94% relative reduction in Compatibility violations compared with baselines and produces fewer invalid or overlapping layouts.

Conclusion: Text-based LLMs, when fine-tuned on domain data and optimized with RLVR, can effectively handle complex topological and numerical constraints in floor plan generation, outperforming previous generative methods. This indicates broader potential for constrained, text-based generative modeling in structured design tasks beyond floor planning.

Abstract: An AI system for professional floor plan design must precisely control room dimensions and areas while respecting the desired connectivity between rooms and maintaining functional and aesthetic quality. Existing generative approaches focus primarily on respecting the requested connectivity between rooms, but do not support generating floor plans that respect numerical constraints. We introduce a text-based floor plan generation approach that fine-tunes a large language model (LLM) on real plans and then applies reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) to improve adherence to topological and numerical constraints while discouraging invalid or overlapping outputs. Furthermore, we design a set of constraint adherence metrics to systematically measure how generated floor plans align with user-defined constraints. Our model generates floor plans that satisfy user-defined connectivity and numerical constraints and outperforms existing methods on Realism, Compatibility, and Diversity metrics. Across all tasks, our approach achieves at least a 94% relative reduction in Compatibility compared with existing methods. Our results demonstrate that LLMs can effectively handle constraints in this setting, suggesting broader applications for text-based generative modeling.

### [11] [Polar probe linearly decodes semantic structures from LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14125)
*Pablo J. Diego-Simón,Pierre Orhan,Yair Lakretz,Jean-Rémi King*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes and tests a simple geometric ‘polar’ code for how LLMs internally represent relations between entities, where distance encodes relation existence/strength and direction encodes relation type.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand mechanistically how large language models bind individual concepts into rich, structured knowledge (e.g., family trees, spatial layouts, arithmetic relations), beyond knowing that they perform well on language tasks. Specifically, the authors want to know whether there is a simple, general geometric principle underlying how relations between entities are represented in neural activation space.

Method: They hypothesize a neural code in which relations are represented by the distance and direction between entity embeddings in activation space: distance signals whether a relation holds; direction signals which relation type. They feed several LLMs natural language descriptions of small, minimalist semantic worlds in five domains (arithmetic, visual scenes, family trees, metro maps, and social interactions). They then train linear ‘Polar Probes’ on a subspace of the models’ layer activations to recover the underlying relational structure, analyzing in which layers this code appears, how it scales with model performance, and how well it generalizes to unseen entities and relations.

Result: (1) The ground-truth relational graphs can be linearly decoded from a low-dimensional polar subspace of LLM activations across all tested domains. (2) The polar code is strongest in middle layers and becomes clearer in better-performing LLMs. (3) Polar Probes generalize to novel entities and relation types but their performance decreases as the complexity/size of the semantic structure increases. (4) The quality of this polar geometric representation is positively correlated with the LLMs’ behavioral accuracy on queries about these semantic worlds.

Conclusion: LLMs appear to implement a relatively simple, domain-general geometric binding mechanism: entities are embedded so that relational information is encoded in inter-entity distances and directions. This polar coding emerges in middle layers, improves with model capability, generalizes somewhat systematically, and is behaviorally relevant, suggesting that complex semantic structures in LLMs are built via a common, simple geometric principle rather than ad hoc or purely symbolic mechanisms.

Abstract: How do artificial neural networks bind concepts to form complex semantic structures? Here, we propose a simple neural code, whereby the existence and the type of relations between entities are represented by the distance and the direction between their embeddings, respectively. We test this hypothesis in a variety of Large Language Models (LLMs), each input with natural-language descriptions of minimalist tasks from five different domains: arithmetic, visual scenes, family trees, metro maps and social interactions. Results show that the true semantic structures can be linearly recovered with a Polar Probe targeting a subspace of LLMs' layer activations. Second, this code emerges mostly in middle layers and improves with LLM performance. Third, these Polar Probes successfully generalize to new entities and relation types, but degrades with the size of the semantic structure. Finally, the quality of the polar representation correlates with the LLM's ability to answer questions about the semantic structure. Together, these findings suggest that LLMs learn to build complex semantic structures by binding representations with a simple geometrical principle.

*Michael S. Lee,Yash Maurya,Drew Rein,Bert Herring,Jonathan Nguyen,Kyungho Song,Udari Madhushani Sehwag,Jiyeon Cho,Kaustubh Deshpande,Yeongkyun Jang,Jiyeon Joo,Minn Seok Choi,Evi Fuelle,Christina Q Knight,Joseph Brandifino,Max Fenkell*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ROK-FORTRESS, a bilingual English–Korean benchmark for evaluating national security and public safety (NSPS) safety behaviors in large language models by disentangling effects of language and geopolitical context.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multilingual LLM safety benchmarks mostly rely on translation of English scenarios, so they do not fully capture how safety behavior changes with different languages and local geopolitical contexts. There is little empirical evidence about how language choice (e.g., English vs. Korean) and grounding in specific national institutions or entities (e.g., U.S. vs. ROK) jointly influence NSPS-related risks and over-refusals.

Method: The authors construct ROK-FORTRESS, a culturally adversarial benchmark centered on the English–Korean pair and the U.S.–ROK geopolitical axis. They build a "transcreation matrix" that systematically varies (i) language (English vs. Korean) and (ii) geopolitical grounding (U.S. vs. Korean institutions, entities, and operational details) for the same underlying adversarial intent. Each adversarial prompt has a paired dual-use benign version to detect over-refusal. Multiple frontier and Korean-specialized models are evaluated, and their responses are scored by calibrated LLM-as-a-judge panels using prompt-specific, expert-designed binary safety rubrics.

Result: Across both general frontier and Korean-optimized models, Korean-language variants show a consistent suppression effect, meaning models tend to refuse more in Korean than in English. However, the impact of geopolitical grounding (U.S. vs. Korean context) varies substantially by model. For many models, explicitly Korean geopolitical grounding counteracts some of the extra refusal triggered by Korean language alone, and there is no model where the opposite (amplification) is observed.

Conclusion: Language and geopolitical context interact in nontrivial ways to shape LLM NSPS safety behavior, and translation-only benchmarks miss these effects. For the English–Korean case, Korean language acts as a risk signal that generally increases refusal, while Korean geopolitical grounding can mitigate this suppression. The proposed transcreation matrix framework offers a generalizable methodology for systematically studying language–culture interactions in LLM safety across other language–culture pairs.

Abstract: Safety evaluations for large language models (LLMs) increasingly target high-stakes National Security and Public Safety (NSPS) risks, yet multilingual safety is typically assessed through translation-only benchmarks that preserve the underlying scenario, and empirical evidence of how language and geopolitical context interact remains limited to a narrow set of language pairs. We introduce \emph{ROK-FORTRESS} https://huggingface.co/datasets/ScaleAI/ROK-FORTRESS_public, a bilingual, culturally adversarial NSPS benchmark that uses the English--Korean language pair and U.S.--ROK geopolitical axis as a case study, separating the effects of language and geopolitical grounding via a \emph{transcreation matrix}: adversarial intents are evaluated under controlled combinations of (i) English versus Korean language and (ii) U.S.\ versus Korean entities, institutions, and operational details. Each adversarial prompt is paired with a dual-use benign counterpart to quantify over-refusal. Model responses are then scored using calibrated LLM-as-a-judge panels, applying our expert-crafted, prompt-specific binary rubrics.
  Across a dual-track set of frontier and Korean-optimized models, we find a consistent suppression effect in Korean variants and substantial model-to-model variation in how geopolitical grounding interacts with language. In many models, Korean grounding mitigates the Korean language-driven suppression -- with no model showing significant amplification in the other direction -- indicating that, at least in the English--Korean case, safety behavior is shaped by language-as-risk signals and context interactions that translation-only evaluations miss. The transcreation matrix methodology is designed to generalize to other language--culture pairs.

### [13] [BOOKMARKS: Efficient Active Storyline Memory for Role-playing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14169)
*Letian Peng,Ziche Liu,Yiming Huang,Longfei Yun,Kun Zhou,Yupeng Hou,Jingbo Shang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: BOOKMARKS is a search-based memory framework for role-playing agents that uses question-answer bookmarks instead of recurrent summarization to preserve task-relevant details and improve long-horizon consistency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing role-playing agent memory systems rely heavily on recurrent summarization, which compresses past interactions and often loses important, task-specific details needed for long-horizon consistency in character behavior and storytelling. There is a need for a memory mechanism that keeps fine-grained, reusable information without incurring high computational costs or discarding key content.

Method: The authors introduce BOOKMARKS, a memory framework where memory units are structured QA-style bookmarks tied to specific points in the storyline. For each task, BOOKMARKS (1) selects or initializes relevant question-based bookmarks at the beginning of the story, (2) synchronizes these bookmarks with the current story point by updating their answers, and (3) reuses them across grounding rounds. It supports three types of search—concept, behavior, and state—each with its own efficient synchronization method for updating and retrieving bookmark contents.

Result: In experiments on 85 characters drawn from 16 artifacts, BOOKMARKS is reported to significantly outperform baseline memory methods used in role-playing agents, indicating better long-horizon consistency and effectiveness of its search-based, bookmark-style memory compared with recurrent summarization approaches.

Conclusion: Search-based, question-answer styled bookmarks provide a more effective memory representation for role-playing agents than recurrent summarization. By actively grounding task-specific details and passively updating only when needed, BOOKMARKS preserves important information, reduces unnecessary computation, and improves performance in long-horizon role-playing tasks.

Abstract: Memory systems are critical for role-playing agents (RPAs) to maintain long-horizon consistency. However, existing RPA memory methods (e.g., profiling) mainly rely on recurrent summarization, whose compression inevitably discards important details. To address this issue, we propose a search-based memory framework called BOOKMARKS, which actively initializes, maintains, and updates task-relevant pieces of bookmarks for the current task (e.g., character acting). A bookmark is structured as the answer to a question at a specific point in the storyline. For each current task, BOOKMARKS selects reusable existing bookmarks or initializes new ones (at storyline beginning) with useful questions. These bookmarks are then synchronized to the current story point, with their answers updated accordingly, so they can be efficiently reused in future grounding rounds. Compared with recurrent summarization, BOOKMARKS offers (1) active grounding for capturing task-specific details and (2) passive updating to avoid unnecessary computation. In implementation, BOOKMARKS supports concept, behavior, and state searches, each powered by an efficient synchronization method. BOOKMARKS significantly outperforms RPA memory baselines on 85 characters from 16 artifacts, demonstrating the effectiveness of search-based memory for RPAs.

### [14] [Why Retrieval-Augmented Generation Fails: A Graph Perspective](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14192)
*Kai Guo,Xinnan Dai,Zhibo Zhang,Nuohan Lin,Shenglai Zeng,Jie Ren,Haoyu Han,Jiliang Tang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies why Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) models still fail even with access to external evidence, by tracing how retrieved information flows inside transformer layers during decoding. It builds attribution graphs to characterize internal evidence integration patterns for correct vs. incorrect answers and uses these graph features both to detect RAG errors and to design targeted interventions that improve grounding and reduce mistakes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RAG systems, despite having external knowledge access, still often generate incorrect answers, and the reasons for this remain unclear at the mechanistic, model-internal level. Existing evaluations mostly look at inputs and outputs, not at how evidence actually flows through the model. The authors want to open this black box: understand systematically how retrieved context influences generation, what distinguishes successful from failed RAG behavior, and whether this understanding can be used to detect and fix errors.

Method: The authors perform a model-internal analysis using circuit tracing on transformer-based RAG models. During decoding, they trace and quantify how information from retrieved evidence, the question, and intermediate activations flows through layers and heads. They turn these attributions into attribution graphs, where nodes represent tokens/activations and edges represent information flow. They then compare graph-structural properties across correct vs. incorrect predictions on multiple QA benchmarks, engineer topology-based features, and train a graph-based error detector. Finally, they design interventions that modify internal routing to emphasize question-conditioned evidence grounding and test how this affects performance.

Result: Across several QA benchmarks, the attribution graphs for correct answers systematically differ from those for incorrect answers: correct cases show deeper information paths across layers, more distributed and redundant evidence flow, and more organized local connectivity; failures show shallow, fragmented, and highly concentrated flows. Using graph-topology features from these attribution graphs, the authors build an effective error detection system that can flag likely RAG failures. Additionally, targeted interventions guided by these graphs can modify internal routing so that decoding remains better grounded in question-relevant evidence, empirically reducing error rates.

Conclusion: Internal evidence integration patterns in RAG models—captured via attribution graphs—are strongly predictive of success vs. failure, revealing that many RAG errors arise not from missing evidence but from how evidence is routed and used internally. Circuit-level graph analysis provides both diagnostic power (error detection) and a lever for improvement (targeted interventions that reinforce question-constrained grounding). This suggests that understanding and shaping internal information flow is a promising direction for building more reliable RAG systems, beyond simply improving retrieval quality.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) has become a powerful and widely used approach for improving large language models by grounding generation in retrieved evidence. However, RAG systems still produce incorrect answers in many cases. Why RAG fails despite having access to external information remains poorly understood. We present a model-internal study of retrieval-augmented generation that examines how retrieved evidence influences answer generation. Using circuit tracing, we construct attribution graphs that model the flow of information through transformer layers during decoding. These graphs represent interactions among retrieved context, intermediate model activations, and generated tokens, providing a graph, circuit-level view of how external evidence is integrated into the model's reasoning process across multiple question answering benchmarks, we observe consistent structural differences: correct predictions exhibit deeper reasoning paths, more distributed evidence flow, and a more structured pattern of local connectivity, while failed predictions show shallower, fragmented, and overly concentrated evidence flow. Building on these findings, we develop a graph-based error detection framework that uses attribution-graph topology features. Furthermore, we show that attribution graphs enable targeted interventions. By reinforcing question-constrained evidence grounding, we reshape internal routing so that answer generation remains guided by the question, leading to more effective integration of retrieved information and fewer errors.

### [15] [GradShield: Alignment Preserving Finetuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14194)
*Zhanhao Hu,Xiao Huang,Patrick Mendoza,Emad A. Alghamdi,Basel Alomair,Raluca Ada Popa,David Wagner*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GradShield is a pre-finetuning data filter for LLMs that scores and removes training examples likely to induce unsafe behavior, keeping models aligned while preserving task utility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Finetuning LLMs on downstream data can silently break prior safety alignment. Harmful or subtly biased examples—sometimes not obviously unsafe—can significantly increase attack success rates. Existing filters largely focus on explicit content or post-hoc safety checks, failing to catch implicitly harmful gradients introduced during finetuning. The paper aims to proactively protect LLMs during finetuning by identifying which individual training samples are most likely to degrade safety, before they are used for training.

Method: The authors propose GradShield, which assigns each candidate finetuning example a Finetuning Implicit Harmfulness Score (FIHS). FIHS is computed from gradient- or representation-based signals that estimate how training on that example would shift the model’s behavior toward or away from harmful outputs. GradShield then uses an adaptive thresholding algorithm to decide which data points to discard, automatically calibrating the fraction of filtered data according to the inferred risk profile of the dataset. GradShield is applied as a preprocessing step to multiple downstream finetuning tasks with varied proportions of harmful data, and the resulting models are evaluated using safety and utility benchmarks.

Result: Across several utility finetuning tasks with different contamination levels of harmful data, models trained on data filtered with GradShield achieve substantially lower Attack Success Rates (ASR), consistently below 6%, compared with baseline filtering or no filtering. At the same time, they maintain comparable task performance (utility metrics), showing that GradShield removes primarily safety-degrading examples without significantly harming the model’s useful capabilities.

Conclusion: GradShield provides an effective, principled way to safeguard LLM safety alignment during finetuning by preemptively filtering data points that are predicted to induce harmful behavior. Its gradient-based FIHS scoring and adaptive thresholding outperform existing baselines in keeping ASR low while preserving utility, suggesting that gradient-aware data selection is a promising direction for secure and robust LLM finetuning.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) pose a significant risk of safety misalignment after finetuning, as models can be compromised by both explicitly and implicitly harmful data. Even some seemingly benign data can inadvertently steer a model towards misaligned behaviors. To address this, we introduce GradShield, a principled filtering method that safeguards LLMs during finetuning by identifying and removing harmful data points before they corrupt the model's alignment. It removes potentially harmful data by computing a Finetuning Implicit Harmfulness Score (FIHS) for each data point and employs an adaptive thresholding algorithm. We apply GradShield to multiple utility fine-tuning tasks across varying levels of harmful data and evaluate the safety and utility performance of the resulting LLMs using various metrics. The results show that GradShield outperforms all baseline methods, consistently maintaining an Attack Success Rate (ASR) below $6\%$ while preserving utility performance.

We further analyze the results, demonstrating that the difficulty of items in the British Council's Knowledge-based Vocabulary Lists (KVL) is often affected by spelling difficulty or the construction of the test items, in addition to the genuine production difficulty of the words. ### [17] [Auditing Agent Harness Safety](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14271)
*Chengzhi Liu,Yichen Guo,Yepeng Liu,Yuzhe Yang,Qianqi Yan,Xuandong Zhao,Wenyue Hua,Sheng Liu,Sharon Li,Yuheng Bu,Xin Eric Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: HarnessAudit is a trajectory-level auditing framework and benchmark for LLM execution harnesses, revealing that many safety violations occur mid-trajectory and are not captured by traditional output-only evaluations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing safety evaluations for LLM systems largely assess only final outputs or terminal states. However, LLM agents increasingly operate within complex execution harnesses that call tools, manage resources, and route messages between multiple agents. In this setting, systems can produce correct final answers while having violated permission boundaries or leaked information mid-trajectory. This creates a blind spot: current benchmarks cannot reliably detect whether a harness respects user intent, permissions, and information-flow constraints throughout execution. The paper aims to close this gap by enabling systematic, trajectory-level auditing of harness behavior, especially in multi-agent scenarios where risks are amplified.

Method: The authors propose HarnessAudit, a framework that records and audits full execution trajectories of LLM harnesses against three dimensions: boundary compliance (respecting access and information-flow constraints), execution fidelity (whether the harness follows the specified protocol and intent), and system stability (robustness of behavior across steps and agents). They design HarnessAudit-Bench, a benchmark of 210 tasks spanning eight real-world domains, each instantiated with single-agent and multi-agent configurations and embedded safety constraints. Using this benchmark, they systematically evaluate ten different harness configurations built on top of frontier LLMs and three popular multi-agent frameworks, analyzing the types, frequencies, and locations (within trajectories) of safety violations.

Result: Experiments show that task success does not imply safe execution: systems often complete tasks while violating constraints mid-trajectory, and the number of violations increases with trajectory length. Safety risk profiles differ across domains, task structures, and specific agent roles. The majority of observed violations arise from improper resource access and unsafe inter-agent information transfer. Moreover, multi-agent setups substantially enlarge the safety risk surface compared to single-agent configurations. The architecture and policies of the execution harness itself critically determine the upper bound of how safely such systems can be deployed.

Conclusion: Trajectory-level auditing is necessary for reliable safety assessment of LLM-based systems operating in execution harnesses, particularly multi-agent ones. Output-only evaluations systematically miss important violations, so safe deployment must consider how harnesses manage permissions, resources, and information flow throughout the entire execution. HarnessAudit and the associated benchmark provide tools and datasets to study and compare harness designs, highlighting that careful harness architecture and constraint enforcement are key determinants of overall system safety.

Abstract: LLM agents increasingly run inside execution harnesses that dispatch tools, allocate resources, and route messages between specialized components. However, a harness can return a correct, benign answer over a trajectory that accesses unauthorized resources or leaks context to the wrong agent. Output-level evaluation cannot see these failures, yet most safety benchmarks score only final outputs or terminal states, even though many violations occur mid-trajectory rather than at termination. The central question is whether the harness respects user intent, permission boundaries, and information-flow constraints throughout execution. To address this gap, we propose HarnessAudit, a framework that audits full execution trajectories across boundary compliance, execution fidelity, and system stability, with a focus on multi-agent harnesses where these risks are most pronounced. We further introduce HarnessAudit-Bench, a benchmark of 210 tasks across eight real-world domains, instantiated in both single-agent and multi-agent configurations with embedded safety constraints. Evaluating ten harness configurations across frontier models and three multi-agent frameworks, we find that: (i) task completion is misaligned with safe execution, and violations accumulate with trajectory length; (ii) safety risks vary across domains, task types, and agent roles; (iii) most violations concentrate in resource access and inter-agent information transfer; and (iv) multi-agent collaboration expands the safety risk surface, while harness design sets the upper bound of safe deployment.

The authors want a model that yields a scalar left–right score and to know which transformer architectures and training data are most suitable for robust bias estimation in German political texts.

Architectural differences and domain-matched training data are critical for strong and robust bias estimation, not just model scale. The authors note current methodological limitations and suggest that further work is needed to improve robustness and generalizability of political bias measurement across domains and over time.

Abstract: Elections represent a crucial milestone in a nation's ongoing development. To better understand the political rhetoric from various movements, ranging from left to right, we propose a transformer-based model capable of projecting the political orientation of a text on a continuous left-to-right spectrum, represented by a normalized scalar d between -1 and 1. This approach enables analysts to focus on specific segments of the political landscape, such as conservatives, while excluding liberal and far-right movements. Such a task can only be achieved with multiclass classifiers, provided that the desired orientation is incorporated within one of their predefined classes. To determine the most suitable foundation model among 13 candidate transformers for this task, we constructed four distinct corpora. One corpus comprised annotated plenary notes from the German Bundestag, while another was based on an official online decision-making tool, Wahl-O-Mat. The third corpus consisted of articles from 33 newspapers, each identified by its political orientation, and the fourth included 535,200 tweets from 597 members of the 20th and 21st German Bundestag. To mitigate overfitting, we used two distinct corpora for training and two for testing, respectively. For in-domain performance, DeBERTa-large achieved the highest F1 score F1=0.844 as well as for the X (Twitter) out-of-domain test ACC=0.864. Regarding the newspaper out-of-domain test, Gemma2-2B excelled (MAE = 0.172). This study demonstrates that transformer models can recognize political framing in German news at the level of public opinion polls. Our findings suggest that both the model architecture and the availability of domain-specific training data can be as influential as model size for estimating political bias. We discuss methodological limitations and outline directions for improving the robustness of bias measurement.

### [19] [LLM-based Detection of Manipulative Political Narratives](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14354)
*Sinclair Schneider,Florian Steuber,Gabi Dreo Rodosek*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a computational pipeline to automatically detect and organize manipulative political narratives on social media by combining LLM-based filtering with unsupervised clustering and cluster-level interpretation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Political debate has shifted to social media, where manipulative narratives can spread quickly and are hard to distinguish from legitimate criticism or neutral discussion. Existing methods often rely on predefined categories or labeled data, which limits their ability to detect new or evolving manipulation strategies. The authors aim to build a system that can automatically identify and structure manipulative narratives at scale, without being constrained to a fixed label set.

Method: 1) Use a detailed few-shot prompt for a reasoning-style language model that mixes known campaign narratives and legitimate criticisms; the model labels each post as manipulative or not. 2) Filter out non-manipulative posts. 3) Embed the remaining posts, reduce dimensionality with UMAP, and cluster them using HDBSCAN to discover narrative groups in an unsupervised fashion. 4) Apply a reasoning model again to each cluster to infer and describe the underlying narrative. The pipeline is applied to a large social media corpus.

Result: On a dataset of over 1.2 million social media posts, the framework filtered the data and, through unsupervised clustering, discovered 41 distinct clusters corresponding to different manipulative political narratives. The combination of LLM-based filtering and clustering produced coherent narrative groupings without relying on fixed label sets.

Conclusion: Integrating prompt-based LLM filtering with UMAP+HDBSCAN clustering offers an effective, scalable, and label-flexible way to detect and structure manipulative political narratives on social media. The approach can reveal previously undefined or emerging narratives, making it valuable for analysis of political communication and potential manipulation campaigns.

Abstract: We present a new computational framework for detecting and structuring manipulative political narratives. A task that became more important due to the shift of political discussions to social media. One of the primary challenges thereby is differentiating between manipulative political narratives and legitimate critiques. Some posts may also reframe actual events within a manipulative context.
  To achieve good clustering results, we filter manipulative posts beforehand using a detailed few-shot prompt that combines documented campaign narratives with legitimate criticisms to differentiate them. This prompt enables a reasoning model to assign labels, retaining only manipulative narrative posts for further processing.
  The remaining posts are subsequently embedded and dimensionality-reduced using UMAP, before HDBSCAN is applied to uncover narrative groups. A key advantage of this unsupervised approach is its independence from a predefined list of target categories, enabling it to uncover new narrative clusters.
  Finally, a reasoning model is employed to uncover the narrative behind each cluster. This approach, applied to over 1.2 million social media posts, effectively identified 41 distinct manipulative narrative clusters by integrating prompt-based filtering with unsupervised clustering.

</details>


### [20] [Reinforcement Learning with Semantic Rewards Enables Low-Resource Language Expansion without Alignment Tax](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14366)
*Zeli Su,Ziyin Zhang,Zhou Liu,Xuexian Song,Zhankai Xu,Longfei Zheng,Xiaolu Zhang,Rong Fu,Guixian Xu,Wentao Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a semantic-space reinforcement learning method, GRPO, to align LLMs to low-resource languages while reducing catastrophic forgetting of general capabilities, demonstrating effectiveness on Tibetan tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Adapting large language models to low-resource languages often harms their general abilities, a phenomenon termed alignment tax. This typically results from standard supervised fine-tuning, which rigidly imitates surface forms from limited and biased data. The authors aim to find a way to gain strong low-resource language capabilities without sacrificing the model's broader competence.

Method: They propose a semantic-space alignment paradigm based on Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), a reinforcement learning method. Instead of optimizing token-level likelihood, the model is updated using embedding-based semantic rewards that reflect meaning similarity rather than exact surface overlap. This allows controlled, less destructive parameter updates while still steering the model toward good behavior in the low-resource language.

Result: On Tibetan-Chinese machine translation and Tibetan headline generation, their approach successfully acquires low-resource language capabilities while significantly mitigating the alignment tax. Compared with supervised fine-tuning, it better preserves general competence and yields generations with higher semantic quality and human preference, even if they differ more in surface form. Few-shot transfer experiments further show that it learns representations that are more transferable and robust under limited supervision.

Conclusion: Reinforcement learning with embedding-level semantic rewards is a safer and more reliable approach to extending LLMs to low-resource languages. By optimizing meaning rather than exact token imitation, the method reduces catastrophic forgetting and produces more robust, transferable representations than standard supervised fine-tuning.

Abstract: Extending large language models (LLMs) to low-resource languages often incurs an "alignment tax": improvements in the target language come at the cost of catastrophic forgetting in general capabilities. We argue that this trade-off arises from the rigidity of supervised fine-tuning (SFT), which enforces token-level surface imitation on narrow and biased data distributions. To address this limitation, we propose a semantic-space alignment paradigm powered by Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), where the model is optimized using embedding-level semantic rewards rather than likelihood maximization. This objective encourages meaning preservation through flexible realizations, enabling controlled updates that reduce destructive interference with pretrained knowledge. We evaluate our approach on Tibetan-Chinese machine translation and Tibetan headline generation. Experiments show that our method acquires low-resource capabilities while markedly mitigating alignment tax, preserving general competence more effectively than SFT. Despite producing less rigid surface overlap, semantic RL yields higher semantic quality and preference in open-ended generation, and few-shot transfer results indicate that it learns more transferable and robust representations under limited supervision. Overall, our study demonstrates that reinforcement learning with semantic rewards provides a safer and more reliable pathway for inclusive low-resource language expansion.

### [21] [Where Should Diffusion Enter a Language Model? Geometry-Guided Hidden-State Replacement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14368)
*Injin Kong,Hyoungjoon Lee,Yohan Jo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose DiHAL, a diffusion-transformer hybrid that inserts diffusion at geometrically chosen hidden layers of a pretrained LM, diffusing/recovering hidden states instead of tokens, which improves over prior continuous diffusion baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Continuous diffusion LMs underperform autoregressive transformers because operating in continuous spaces misaligned with discrete language tokens makes denoising and token recovery hard. There's a need to better integrate diffusion with strong pretrained transformers by finding where and how to insert diffusion for more effective language modeling.

Method: Introduce DiHAL, which: (1) scores each transformer layer using geometry-based proxies of hidden-state suitability for diffusion; (2) selects a "diffusion-friendly" intermediate layer; (3) replaces the lower prefix of the transformer with a learned diffusion bridge that maps noise to that layer's hidden state while keeping the upper layers and LM head fixed; and (4) trains diffusion to reconstruct the chosen hidden representation, avoiding direct token-space diffusion and discrete recovery.

Result: On 8B-parameter backbones, the geometry score reliably identifies shallow layers where inserting the diffusion bridge works well under a fixed training protocol, and the resulting hidden-state diffusion model outperforms comparable continuous diffusion baselines when matched for diffusion and recovery training budgets.

Conclusion: Hidden-state geometric properties can guide where to insert diffusion mechanisms inside pretrained LMs, and reconstructing internal hidden states instead of tokens makes diffusion-based replacements more feasible and effective than previous continuous diffusion language modeling approaches.

Abstract: Continuous diffusion language models lag behind autoregressive transformers, partly because diffusion is applied in spaces poorly suited to language denoising and token recovery. We propose DiHAL, a geometry-guided diffusion-transformer hybrid that asks where diffusion should enter a pretrained transformer. DiHAL scores layers with geometry-based proxies, selects a diffusion-friendly hidden-state interface, and replaces the lower transformer prefix with a diffusion bridge while retaining the upper layers and original LM head. By reconstructing the selected-layer hidden state rather than tokens, DiHAL avoids direct continuous-to-discrete recovery. Experiments on 8B-scale backbones show that the geometry score predicts effective shallow insertion layers under a fixed bridge-training protocol and that hidden-state recovery improves over continuous diffusion baselines in a diagnostic comparison matching the diffusion/recovery training budget. These results suggest that hidden-state geometry helps identify where diffusion-based replacement is feasible inside pretrained language models.

MARS organizes this belief state into three tiers: event memory buffers raw signals, preference memory maintains fine-grained mutable chunks with explicit strength and evidence tracking, and profile memory distills all preferences into a coherent natural language narrative. A complete lifecycle of six operations -- extraction, reinforcement, weakening, consolidation, forgetting, and resynthesis -- is adaptively scheduled by an LLM-based planner rather than fixed-interval heuristics. Experiments on four InstructRec benchmark domains show that \ours achieves state-of-the-art performance with average improvements of 26.4% in HR@1 and 10.3% in NDCG@10 over the strongest baselines with further gains from agentic scheduling in evolving settings.
