<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In end-to-end ASR, vocabulary (token set) is derived from training text using subword algorithms (BPE, WordPiece, ULM), and its size is a crucial but poorly understood hyper-parameter. Existing recipes use fixed, ad hoc vocabulary sizes with little explanation. Prior work introduced a cost-function-based, black-box framework for picking vocabulary size, but lacks a more formal, practical method to estimate it. This paper aims to formalize and systematize the choice of vocabulary size to improve ASR performance.

Method: Build on the prior cost function framework that models vocabulary size selection as a black-box tokenization problem. Collect training data (e.g., performance metrics vs. vocabulary size) and apply curve fitting to model the relationship. Use first and second derivative tests from calculus on the fitted curve to identify an optimal vocabulary size (local minimum/maximum of the cost function). Implement this method with standard subword tokenizers and apply it on the LibriSpeech dataset within an end-to-end ASR pipeline.

Result: Using the proposed curve-fitting and derivative-based optimization procedure on LibriSpeech yields an empirically determined optimal vocabulary size that outperforms commonly used, heuristic vocabulary sizes in ASR recipes. The ASR system trained with this optimal vocabulary size shows improved performance (e.g., lower word error rate) compared to baseline configurations.

Conclusion: A formal, calculus-based approach can effectively determine the optimal subword vocabulary size for end-to-end ASR training. By modeling performance vs. vocabulary size, fitting a curve, and applying derivative tests, practitioners can systematically select a better vocabulary size than standard fixed choices, leading to measurable ASR performance gains on datasets like LibriSpeech. The paper’s main contribution is to formalize and demonstrate this principled method for vocabulary size selection in end-to-end ASR systems.

Abstract: In hybrid automatic speech recognition (ASR) systems, the vocabulary size is unambiguous, typically determined by the number of phones, bi-phones, or tri-phones present in the language. In contrast, end-to-end ASR systems derive their vocabulary, often referred to as tokens from the text corpus used for training. The choice and, more importantly, the size of this vocabulary is a critical hyper-parameter in training end-to-end ASR systems. Tokenization algorithms such as Byte Pair Encoding (BPE), WordPiece, and Unigram Language Model (ULM) use the vocabulary size as an input hyper-parameter to generate the sub-words employed during ASR training. Popular toolkits like ESPNet provide a fixed vocabulary size in their training recipes, but there is little documentation or discussion in the literature regarding how these values are determined. Recent work [1] has formalized an approach to identify the vocabulary size best suited for end-to-end ASR, introducing a cost function framework that treats the tokenization process as a black box. In this paper, we build upon that foundation by curve fitting the training data and using the principle of first and second derivative tests in calculus to formally estimate the vocabulary size hyper-parameter. We demonstrate the utility and usefulness of our approach by applying it on a standard Librispeech corpus and show that the optimal choice of vocabulary size hyper-parameter improves the performance of the ASR. The main contribution of this paper in formalizing an approach to identify the vocabulary size best suited for training an end-to-end ASR system.

### [26] [Does RAG Know When Retrieval Is Wrong? Diagnosing Context Compliance under Knowledge Conflict](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14473)
*Yihang Chen,Pin Qian,Su Wang,Sipeng Zhang,Huan Xu,Shuhuai Lin,Xinpeng Wei*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In Retrieval-Augmented Generation, external documents can dominate model outputs, especially when they contradict the model’s internal (parametric) knowledge. Existing evaluations using accuracy alone do not show how or when this happens, so the authors want a causal, fine-grained way to probe and intervene in this conflict between retrieved context and internal knowledge.

Method: They propose Context-Driven Decomposition (CDD), an inference-time probing and intervention technique that decomposes the model’s beliefs into components attributable to retrieved context versus parametric knowledge. They then stress-test this on several benchmarks: Epi-Scale adversarial evaluations, TruthfulQA with injected misconceptions in the context, and cross-model experiments on different LLM families (Gemini-2.5-Flash and Claude models). CDD is used both to measure context-compliance and to actively resolve conflicts by favoring one belief source over another.

Result: (P1) They show that in adversarial settings with misleading context, standard RAG can be highly context-compliant and inaccurate, e.g., only 15.0% accuracy on TruthfulQA with misconception injection. (P2) When applying CDD, adversarial robustness/accuracy improves across different model families, but the detailed causal coupling between rationales and answers (how conflict resolution shows up in the model’s internal reasoning trace) transfers poorly: Gemini-2.5-Flash shows high causal sensitivity to injected mistakes (64.1%), whereas Claude models show much lower or inconsistent sensitivity (−3% to +7%). (P3) Explicitly decomposing and handling conflicts with CDD significantly boosts robustness to temporal drift (outdated vs updated facts) and to noisy, distracting evidence, achieving over 70% accuracy on temporal-shift and about 70% on distractor-evidence tasks in the full Epi-Scale benchmark.

Conclusion: Context-compliance is a distinct, structural property of RAG systems that can be probed and controlled separately from retrieval quality or generic robustness. CDD provides a practical tool to causally analyze and intervene on how retrieved context versus parametric knowledge shape answers, and the Epi-Scale benchmark enables systematic, cross-model study of these behaviors across different models and retrieval setups.

Abstract: The Context-Compliance Regime in Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) occurs when retrieved context dominates
  the final answer even when it conflicts with the model's parametric knowledge. Accuracy alone does not reveal
  how retrieved context causally shapes answers under such conflict. We introduce Context-Driven Decomposition
  (CDD), a belief-decomposition probe that operates at inference time and serves as an intervention mechanism for
  controlled retrieval conflict. Across Epi-Scale stress tests, TruthfulQA misconception injection, and cross-
  model reruns, CDD exposes three patterns. P1: context compliance is measurable in an upper-bound adversarial
  setting, where Standard RAG reaches 15.0% accuracy on TruthfulQA misconception injection (N=500). P2:
  adversarial accuracy gains transfer across model families: CDD improves accuracy on Gemini-2.5-Flash and on
  Claude Haiku/Sonnet/Opus, but rationale-answer causal coupling does not transfer. CDD reaches 64.1% mistake-
  injection causal sensitivity on Gemini-2.5-Flash, while sensitivities for all three Claude variants fall in the
  [-3%, +7%] range, suggesting that the Claude-side accuracy gains operate through a mechanism distinct from the
  explicit conflict-resolution trace. P3: explicit conflict decomposition improves robustness under temporal
  drift and noisy distractors, with CDD reaching 71.3% on temporal shifts and 69.9% on distractor evidence on the
  full Epi-Scale adversarial benchmark. These three patterns identify context-compliance as a structural axis
  along which standard RAG can be probed and intervened on, distinct from retrieval-quality or single-method
  robustness questions, and motivate releasing Epi-Scale for systematic study across model families and retrieval
  pipelines.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM memory systems and benchmarks mainly assume a single user chatting with an agent, yet real-world deployments often involve multiple users, channels, and complex group interactions. Method: The authors build GroupMemBench using a graph-grounded synthesis pipeline that generates realistic multi-party conversations. They then design an adversarial query pipeline that creates questions tied to specific askers across six categories: multi-hop reasoning, knowledge update, term ambiguity, user-implicit reasoning, temporal reasoning, and abstention. The low scores and the competitiveness of a simple BM25 baseline indicate that multi-user memory remains far from solved, and that new methods explicitly designed for group dynamics, per-user belief tracking, and audience-aware language are needed.

This mismatch leaves three properties of group memory unmeasured: (i) group dynamics that go beyond concatenated one-on-one chats, (ii) speaker-grounded belief tracking, where the per-user memory modeling is needed, and (iii) audience-adapted language, where Theory-of-Mind shifts produce role-specific vocabulary. Benchmarking leading memory systems exposes a sharp collapse: the strongest one reaches only 46.0% average accuracy, with knowledge update at 27.1% and term ambiguity at 37.7%, while a simple BM25 baseline matches or exceeds most agent memory systems. This indicates current memory ingestion erases the structural and lexical features group memory depends on, leaving multi-user memory far from solved.

### [29] [Dimension-Level Intent Fidelity Evaluation for Large Language Models: Evidence from Structured Prompt Ablation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14517)
*GAng Peng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new evaluation framework that separately measures whether LLMs follow the structural form of a request and whether they truly preserve user intent across multiple semantic dimensions, showing that traditional single holistic scores often miss important intent failures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing holistic evaluation scores for LLM outputs provide a single overall quality or alignment score, but they conflate two different aspects: (1) whether the model follows the outward structural form of the prompt (format, sections, style) and (2) whether it faithfully preserves the user’s underlying intent along different semantic dimensions (constraints, content requirements, stance, etc.). As a result, models can receive high overall scores while actually missing important parts of what the user wanted, especially in multilingual and complex task settings. There is a need for a finer-grained evaluation method that can dissect these failures and better predict human satisfaction and safety for user-specific tasks.

Method: The authors design a dimension-level intent fidelity framework that disentangles structural recovery (reproducing the requested form or pattern) from intent fidelity (preserving the substantive user intent) along multiple semantic dimensions. They operationalize this via a structured prompt ablation study: systematically removing or perturbing parts of the prompt that encode different intent dimensions, and then measuring how well six different LLMs, across three languages and three task domains, can infer or reconstruct the missing information. They collect 2,880 model outputs and assign both holistic alignment scores and fine-grained dimensional scores. They also build a public-private decomposition across 2,520 ablation cells to test when models can compensate for missing user intent, and use proxy annotations to distinguish what could have been inferred beforehand from what is actually recoverable by default model behavior. A weight-perturbation experiment further probes how different levels of misalignment at the dimensional level affect output quality and harm.

Result: The framework uncovers a consistent structural-fidelity split: a substantial fraction of outputs that look perfectly aligned under holistic scoring actually contain intent-level deficits on specific dimensions. For Chinese outputs with complete paired scores, 25.7% of cases with perfect global alignment scores (GA=5) still show measurable dimensional intent errors; for English outputs, this fraction reaches 58.6%. Human evaluation verifies that these "split-zone" outputs correspond to real quality problems and confirms that dimensional intent scores correlate better with human judgments than holistic scores. The public-private decomposition maps conditions under which models can successfully infer or compensate for missing intent and where they fail. Proxy annotations clarify which aspects were inferable in principle versus recoverable in practice. The weight-perturbation experiment shows that models can tolerate moderate misalignment (absorbing it without major harm) but that severe dimensional inversion—where intent is significantly flipped or contradicted—consistently degrades quality and is harmful.

Conclusion: Dimension-level intent fidelity evaluation exposes failures that holistic scoring alone systematically misses and is therefore a necessary complement to standard overall quality metrics when assessing LLMs on user-specific tasks. The study demonstrates that models often reproduce structural format while partially or severely failing user intent, that these failures are nontrivial and recognized by human raters, and that fine-grained dimensional scores better capture human-perceived quality. The proposed framework, validated across languages, tasks, and models, offers a more diagnostic tool for understanding when LLMs successfully infer and preserve user intent—especially under prompt perturbations—and when they exhibit dangerous or harmful misalignment at the dimension level.

Abstract: Holistic evaluation scores capture overall output quality but do not distinguish whether a model reproduced the structural form of a user's request from whether it preserved the user's specific intent. We propose a dimension-level intent fidelity evaluation framework, applied here through a structured prompt ablation study across 2,880 outputs spanning three languages, three task domains, and six LLMs, that separately measures structural recovery and intent fidelity for each semantic dimension. This framework reveals a systematic structural-fidelity split: among Chinese-language outputs with complete paired scores, 25.7% received perfect holistic alignment scores (GA=5) while exhibiting measurable dimensional intent deficits; among English-language outputs, this proportion rose to 58.6%. Human evaluation confirmed that these split-zone outputs represent genuine quality deficits and that dimensional fidelity scores track human judgements more reliably than holistic scores do. A public-private decomposition of 2,520 ablation cells characterises when models successfully compensate for missing intent and when they fail, while proxy annotation distinguishes prior inferability from default recoverability. A weight-perturbation experiment shows that moderate misalignment is typically absorbed, whereas severe dimensional inversion is consistently harmful. These findings demonstrate that dimension-level intent fidelity evaluation is a necessary complement to holistic assessment when evaluating LLM outputs for user-specific tasks.

*ZiYi Dong,Yuliang Huang,Weijian Deng,Xiangyang Ji,Liang Lin,Pengxu Wei*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They cast language generation as a stochastic optimal control problem, use an approximate solution to the Hamilton-Jacobi-Bellman equation implemented via flow matching in a rectified latent space, and build Manta-LM, which offers parallel, high-fidelity, controllable text generation with strong empirical results.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing autoregressive and diffusion-based language models suffer from fundamental limitations like the efficiency–fidelity trade-off, error accumulation over time, and difficulties in jointly achieving high-quality text and efficient sampling. There is also a lack of a unified theoretical framework that explains these issues and guides the design of better generation algorithms.

Method: Formulate text generation as a stochastic optimal control problem and analyze known limitations of current models as consequences of trajectory singularity, vanishing adjoint states, and missing gradients. Approximate the solution of the Hamilton-Jacobi-Bellman equation to obtain an optimal closed-loop policy. Instead of solving the HJB PDE directly, employ Flow Matching to compute optimal trajectories in a rectified latent control space, and implement this via Manta-LM with a Global Integral Operator that approximates the global vector field governing generation dynamics.

Result: The proposed Manta-LM model supports parallel, non-autoregressive-like sampling while maintaining high generation fidelity. Experiments on language modeling and conditional generation benchmarks show strong performance compared to baselines, with better stability during generation, higher sampling efficiency, and enhanced controllability of outputs.

Conclusion: Viewing language generation through the lens of stochastic optimal control yields a principled way to understand and mitigate key weaknesses of autoregressive and diffusion models. By approximately solving the HJB equation using flow matching in a latent control space, Manta-LM realizes a practical model that combines high-quality text generation with efficient, stable, and controllable sampling.

Abstract: This work reformulates language generation as a stochastic optimal control problem, providing a unified theoretical perspective to analyze autoregressive and diffusion models and explain their limitations (Efficiency-Fidelity Paradox, Irreversibility Error Propagation, Optimization Tractability and Fidelity) in terms of combination of trajectory singularity, adjoint state vanishing, and gradient absence. To address these issues, we approximate the solution to the Hamilton-Jacobi-Bellman (HJB) equation, yielding an optimal policy that acts as a closed-loop controller. To bypass the intractability of directly solving the HJB PDE, we employ Flow Matching as the optimal trajectory solver within the rectified latent control space. This allows our Manta-LM with Global Integral Operator to approximate the global vector field, effectively realizing a model that simultaneously achieves high-fidelity text generation and efficient, low-cost parallel sampling. Empirically, our method achieves strong performance on language modeling and conditional generation tasks, while exhibiting improved stability, efficiency, and controllability.

</details>


### [31] [Learning from Failures: Correction-Oriented Policy Optimization with Verifiable Rewards](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14539)
*Mengjie Ren,Jie Lou,Boxi Cao,Xueru Wen,Hongyu Lin,Xianpei Han,Le Sun,Xing Yu,Yaojie Lu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They improve RL with verifiable rewards by reusing failed trajectories as correction-focused supervision to enhance reasoning and self-correction.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RLVR suffers from sparse binary rewards and poor credit assignment, wasting information from failed trajectories and giving ambiguous training signals, limiting reasoning improvements.

Method: They propose Correction-Oriented Policy Optimization (CIPO), which augments RLVR by turning on-policy failed trajectories into correction-oriented training samples, then jointly optimizes these correction samples with the usual RLVR objective, all without external supervision.

Result: Across 11 benchmarks in math reasoning and code generation, CIPO beats strong baselines on both reasoning quality and correction ability, and shows stronger pass@K improvements.

Conclusion: CIPO makes RLVR training more effective by leveraging failed attempts as self-generated correction signals, leading to better intrinsic reasoning rather than just reweighting probability over existing correct answers.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Rewards (RLVR) has emerged as an effective paradigm for improving the reasoning capabilities of large language models. However, RLVR training is often hindered by sparse binary rewards and weak credit assignment, resulting in ambiguous optimization signals and underutilization of the useful information embedded in failed trajectories. To address this challenge, we propose Correction-Oriented Policy Optimization (CIPO), a simple and effective extension to RLVR that converts on-policy failed trajectories into correction-oriented supervision, without relying on any external signals. By jointly optimizing correction samples derived from the model's own failed attempts together with the standard RLVR objective, CIPO improves learning effectiveness while explicitly enhancing the model's ability to correct its own errors. Extensive experiments across 11 benchmarks spanning mathematical reasoning and code generation demonstrate that CIPO consistently and significantly outperforms strong baselines in both reasoning and correction performance. Moreover, CIPO yields stronger pass@K gains, indicating that it improves the model's intrinsic reasoning capacity rather than merely redistributing probability mass over existing correct answers.

### [32] [Uncertainty Quantification for Large Language Diffusion Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14570)
*Artem Vazhentsev,Vladislav Smirnov,David Li,Maxim Panov,Timothy Baldwin,Artem Shelmanov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes efficient, zero-shot uncertainty quantification methods tailored for Large Language Diffusion Models (LLDMs), enabling reliable hallucination detection without sacrificing their parallel inference speed.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing uncertainty quantification (UQ) methods are designed for autoregressive language models and often rely on autoregressive factorization or expensive repeated sampling. These approaches are misaligned with the parallel generation process of LLDMs and undermine their main advantage: fast, efficient inference. There is a need for UQ techniques that both fit the LLDM generation paradigm and retain its computational efficiency while providing reliable hallucination detection.

Method: The authors conduct the first systematic study of uncertainty quantification for LLDMs. They introduce several lightweight, zero-shot uncertainty signals obtained directly from the iterative denoising process, including features from intermediate generations, token remasking behavior, and the complexity of denoising trajectories. Additionally, they adapt a state-of-the-art UQ method to LLDMs by combining masked diffusion likelihoods with trajectory-based semantic dissimilarity. Theoretically, they show that the expected trajectory dissimilarity provides a lower bound on the masked diffusion training objective, justifying its use as an uncertainty measure.

Result: Through experiments on three tasks, eight datasets, and two LLDM architectures, their proposed methods nearly match the performance of the strongest sampling-based uncertainty baselines while being dramatically more efficient—up to 100x lower computational cost. Their signals effectively detect hallucinations in LLDM outputs without the need for multiple sampled generations.

Conclusion: LLDMs can simultaneously offer high-speed, parallel inference and robust hallucination detection when equipped with appropriately designed, diffusion-aware uncertainty quantification methods. The proposed zero-shot and trajectory-based UQ techniques offer a favorable cost-performance trade-off and establish a foundation for safe deployment of LLDMs.

Abstract: Large Language Diffusion Models (LLDMs) are emerging as an alternative to autoregressive models, offering faster inference through higher parallelism. Similar to autoregressive LLMs, they remain prone to hallucinations, making reliable uncertainty quantification (UQ) crucial for safe deployment. However, existing UQ methods are fundamentally misaligned with this new paradigm: they assume autoregressive factorization or use expensive repeated sampling, negating the efficiency of LLDMs. In this work, we present the first systematic study of UQ for LLDMs and propose lightweight, zero-shot uncertainty signals derived from the iterative denoising process, leveraging intermediate generations, token remasking dynamics, and denoising complexity. We further adapt a state-of-the-art UQ method to LLDMs by combining masked diffusion likelihoods with trajectory-based semantic dissimilarity. We prove that expected trajectory dissimilarity lower bounds the masked diffusion training objective, which motivates its usage as an uncertainty score. Comprehensive experiments across three tasks, eight datasets, and two models show that our method achieves a great cost-performance trade-off: it approaches the strongest sampling-based baselines while incurring up to 100x lower computational overhead. Our work demonstrates that LLDMs can deliver both fast inference and reliable hallucination detection simultaneously.

### [33] [EndPrompt: Efficient Long-Context Extension via Terminal Anchoring](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14589)
*Han Tian,Luxuan Chen,Xinran Chen,Rui Kong,Fang Wang,Jiamin Chen,Jinman Zhao,Yuchen Li,Jiashu Zhao,Shuaiqiang Wang,Haoyi Xiong,Dawei Yin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: EndPrompt extends LLM context windows using only short sequences by appending a short end prompt with high positional indices, achieving 8K→64K extension efficiently and outperforming existing methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Extending LLM context windows usually requires training on very long sequences, which is computationally expensive due to quadratic attention cost and hard to reproduce. The authors aim to find a cheaper, more scalable way to adapt models to long contexts without full-length training data.

Method: They introduce EndPrompt, which keeps the original short training text as a first segment and appends a short terminal prompt as a second segment. This end prompt is assigned positional indices near the target long context length. Using Rotary Position Embeddings theory and Bernstein inequality, they argue that this sparse positional supervision plus shared Transformer weights enforces smooth attention behavior over long distances and suppresses unstable extrapolation at unseen positions.

Result: On LLaMA-family models extended from 8K to 64K context, EndPrompt attains an average RULER score of 76.03 and the best average on LongBench, outperforming LCEG (72.24), LongLoRA (72.95), and even full-length fine-tuning (69.23), while using far less computation.

Conclusion: Long-context generalization can be induced using sparse positional supervision via a short end prompt, without dense training on full-length sequences, challenging the assumption that reliable context-window extension needs expensive long-sequence fine-tuning.

Abstract: Extending the context window of large language models typically requires training on sequences at the target length, incurring quadratic memory and computational costs that make long-context adaptation expensive and difficult to reproduce. We propose EndPrompt, a method that achieves effective context extension using only short training sequences. The core insight is that exposing a model to long-range relative positional distances does not require constructing full-length inputs: we preserve the original short context as an intact first segment and append a brief terminal prompt as a second segment, assigning it positional indices near the target context length. This two-segment construction introduces both local and long-range relative distances within a short physical sequence while maintaining the semantic continuity of the training text--a property absent in chunk-based simulation approaches that split contiguous context. We provide a theoretical analysis grounded in Rotary Position Embedding and the Bernstein inequality, showing that position interpolation induces a rigorous smoothness constraint over the attention function, with shared Transformer parameters further suppressing unstable extrapolation to unobserved intermediate distances. Applied to LLaMA-family models extending the context window from 8K to 64K, EndPrompt achieves an average RULER score of 76.03 and the highest average on LongBench, surpassing LCEG (72.24), LongLoRA (72.95), and full-length fine-tuning (69.23) while requiring substantially less computation. These results demonstrate that long-context generalization can be induced from sparse positional supervision, challenging the prevailing assumption that dense long-sequence training is necessary for reliable context-window extension. The code is available at https://github.com/clx1415926/EndPrompt.

### [34] [SciPaths: Forecasting Pathways to Scientific Discovery](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14600)
*Eric Chamoun,Yizhou Chi,Yulong Chen,Rui Cao,Zifeng Ding,Michalis Korakakis,Andreas Vlachos*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They create a new benchmark, SciPaths, to test whether AI can reconstruct the enabling chains of work behind scientific discoveries.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI4Science benchmarks mostly test surface tasks like citation prediction or retrieval, not the deeper dependency structure of how earlier work enables later breakthroughs. The authors want to evaluate models on reconstructing the actual pathways of scientific progress.

Method: They define a new task, discovery pathway forecasting, where models must identify enabling contributions for a target paper and link each to prior work where possible. They build SciPaths, a dataset of 262 expert-annotated gold pathways and 2,444 automatically generated silver pathways from ML/NLP papers, annotating enabling contributions, their roles, rationales, and grounding in prior work or noting when no prior exists. They then evaluate several state-of-the-art language models on this task.

Result: The best evaluated model achieves only 0.189 F1 under strict semantic matching, and is especially weak at recovering core methodological dependencies. When models are given the gold list of enabling contributions, their ability to ground these in prior work improves substantially.

Conclusion: SciPaths exposes a key missing capability in current scientific forecasting models: backward reasoning from a target contribution to its enabling building blocks and literature dependencies. Decomposing a contribution into enabling steps is currently the main bottleneck, and the benchmark reorients evaluation toward this deeper aspect of scientific reasoning.

Abstract: Scientific progress depends on sequences of enabling contributions, yet existing AI4Science benchmarks largely focus on citation prediction, literature retrieval, or idea generation rather than the dependencies that make progress possible. In this paper, we introduce discovery pathway forecasting: given a target scientific contribution and the prior literature available at a specified time, the task is to (1) identify the enabling contributions required to realize it and (2) ground each in prior work when such prior work exists. We present SciPaths, a benchmark of 262 expert-annotated gold pathways and 2,444 silver pathways constructed from machine learning and natural language processing papers, where each pathway records enabling contributions, roles, rationales, and prior-work groundings or unmapped decisions. Evaluating frontier and open-weight language models, we find that the best model reaches only 0.189 F1 under strict semantic matching, with core methodological dependencies hardest to recover. Prior-work grounding improves substantially when gold enabling contributions are provided, showing that decomposition quality is a major bottleneck for end-to-end pathway recovery. SciPaths therefore shifts evaluation toward a missing capability in scientific forecasting: reasoning backward from a target contribution to the enabling scientific building blocks and prior-work dependencies that make it feasible.

Using PEARMUT, we conduct a human evaluation via (i) multi-way Direct Assessment (0--100) and (ii) targeted terminology auditing with a restricted MQM taxonomy. Gemini-RAG yields the highest exact-match terminology accuracy (81.4\%), versus Gemini-Simple (69.1\%) and DeepL (64.4\%), while preserving overall quality (mean DA 85.3 Gemini-RAG vs. 85.2 Gemini-Simple), outperforming DeepL (80.3). These results show that glossary-augmented prompting is a low-overhead way to improve terminology control in cultural-heritage translation if institutions maintain minimal terminology resources and lightweight evaluation procedures.

### [36] [Mechanical Enforcement for LLM Governance:Evidence of Governance-Task Decoupling in Financial Decision Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14744)
*José Manuel de la Chica Rodríguez,Carlos Martí-González*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that relying only on natural-language policies interpreted by the same LLM (text-only governance) is unsafe in regulated finance. It proposes governance metrics at the rationale level and a mechanically enforced architecture that sharply improves both compliance and accuracy by taking clear-cut decisions out of the model’s hands.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In regulated financial domains, decisions must be auditable, and models must follow governance policies. Today, the same LLM both executes the task and interprets the natural-language governance policy, so it can *appear* compliant while actually violating rules at the level of decision rationales. Existing evaluations mostly measure task accuracy, not whether the reasoning itself obeys governance constraints. The authors are motivated to (1) measure governance at the rationale level and (2) design an architecture where governance is not left solely to the model’s own interpretation of policies.

Method: The authors define five quantitative governance metrics that operate on model rationales to capture policy compliance at the explanation level. They create a synthetic banking environment where an LLM makes lending decisions under regulatory-like constraints. They compare two setups: (a) text-only governance, where governance is expressed as natural-language instructions and enforced by the same LLM; and (b) mechanical enforcement, where governance is implemented by four simple external primitives (e.g., rule filters, deferral logic) outside the LLM’s interpretive loop. They also run a causal ablation, disabling each primitive in turn to test its necessity for the observed gains.

Result: In the text-only governance setup, 27% of deferrals (cases where the model declines to decide) lack any decision-relevant information in their rationales, revealing superficial or non-auditable compliance. With mechanical enforcement, this rate drops by 73%, and the information content of deferrals more than doubles. Task performance improves substantially: the Matthews Correlation Coefficient on the main task rises from about 0.43 to 0.88. Analysis of the rationales shows that their governance quality (e.g., compliance with CDL-like constraints) is similar between regimes; the performance gains mainly come from removing straightforward cases from the LLM’s control via the external primitives. The ablation confirms each primitive is individually necessary for the overall improvement.

Conclusion: Governance and task performance are distinct axes: high accuracy does not guarantee governance-compliant reasoning in regulated AI. When governance is implemented purely as text instructions interpreted by the same LLM (text-only governance), both governance quality and task performance can collapse together under structural stress. In contrast, a mechanically enforced architecture—with simple, external rule-based primitives that constrain the LLM—can preserve governance quality even when task performance degrades, while also substantially improving overall accuracy in normal operation. Therefore, regulated AI systems should not rely solely on natural-language, model-interpreted policies; they need separate, auditable governance mechanisms that structurally decouple task execution from governance enforcement.

Abstract: Large language models in regulated financial workflows are governed by natural-language policies that the same model interprets, creating a principal--agent failure: outputs can appear compliant without being compliant. Existing evaluation measures task accuracy but not whether governance constrains behaviour at the decision rationale level -- where regulated decisions must be auditable. We introduce five governance metrics that quantify policy compliance at the rationale level and apply them in a synthetic banking domain to compare text-only governance against mechanical enforcement: four primitives operating outside the model's interpretive loop. Under text-only governance, 27% of deferrals carry no decision-relevant information. Mechanical enforcement reduces this rate by 73%, more than doubles deferral information content, and raises task accuracy from MCC~$0.43$ to $0.88$. The improvement is driven by architectural separation: LLM-generated rationales under mechanical enforcement show comparable CDL to text-only governance -- the gain comes from removing clear-cut decisions from the model's control. A causal ablation confirms that each primitive is individually necessary. Our central finding is a governance-task decoupling: under structural stress, text-only governance degrades on both dimensions simultaneously, whereas mechanical enforcement preserves governance quality even as task performance drops. This implies that governance and task evaluation are distinct axes: accuracy is not a sufficient proxy for governance in regulated AI systems.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing model editing/intervention techniques for LLMs mostly assume the Linear Representation Hypothesis, which states that semantic features are encoded linearly in the model’s activation space. This limits interventions to features that are approximately linear and have explicit output signatures, leaving out more complex, nonlinearly encoded or implicit features that cannot be directly probed via outputs. There is a need for a more general intervention framework that can act on such nonlinear and implicit features to better understand and steer model behavior.

Method: The authors propose a general mathematical formulation of interventions that does not rely on linearity of internal representations. They develop a learning-based procedure to identify and manipulate nonlinear features in the activation space, including implicit features that do not correspond directly to any observable output dimension. Their procedure likely involves learning a nonlinear feature mapping or manifold and an associated intervention operator that can be applied at chosen layers during inference to steer behavior.

Result: Using the proposed nonlinear intervention framework, they apply it to the task of refusal bypass steering—controlling when an LLM refuses or complies with a request. Empirically, their method achieves more precise control over refusal behavior than standard linear intervention baselines, demonstrating that the target refusal feature is better captured as a nonlinear structure and that their approach can effectively manipulate such features.

Conclusion: The work shows that limiting interventions to linear features is unnecessarily restrictive and that a more general intervention framework capable of handling nonlinear and implicit features yields better control over LLM behavior. This suggests that important aspects of model internals lie on nonlinear manifolds and that generalized interventions can be a powerful tool for both interpretability and fine-grained behavioral steering of large language models.

Abstract: Intervention is one of the most representative and widely used methods for understanding the internal representations of large language models (LLMs). However, existing intervention methods are confined to linear interventions grounded in the Linear Representation Hypothesis, leaving features encoded along non-linear manifolds beyond their reach. In this work, we introduce a general formulation of intervention that extends naturally to non-linearly represented features, together with a learning procedure that further enables intervention on implicit features lacking a direct output signature. We validate our framework on refusal bypass steering, where it steers the model more precisely than linear baselines by intervening on a non-linear feature governing refusal.

Abstract: Normally, a system that translates speech into text consists of separate modules for speech recognition and text-to-text translation. But existing SpeechLLM systems are slow since they do not work in a real streaming fashion: they wait for a complete utterance of audio before outputting a translation, or output tokens at fixed intervals, which is not suitable for real applications. This work proposes an LLM-based architecture for real streaming speech-to-text translation. The LLM learns not just to emit output tokens, but also to decide whether it has seen enough audio to do so. The system is trained using automatic alignments of the input speech and the output text. In experiments on different language pairs, the system achieves a translation quality close to the non-streaming baseline, but with a latency of only 1-2 seconds.

### [40] [Graphs of Research: Citation Evolution Graphs as Supervision for Research Idea Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14790)
*Songyang Gao,Yinghui Xia,Siyi Liu,Hui Xiong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Graphs of Research (GoR), a supervised fine-tuning approach that uses structured citation-evolution graphs around a seed paper to better condition LLMs for research idea generation, achieving state-of-the-art performance versus strong GPT-4o baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automated research idea generation is crucial for scaling scientific innovation, and LLMs are promising tools for this. Existing approaches mostly rely on static retrieval of related literature or intricate prompts, ignoring the rich structural relationships among cited papers (e.g., citation positions, temporal order, and inter-citation links). There is a need for methods that exploit these citation-evolution structures as explicit supervision signals to help LLMs infer and generate high-quality, novel research ideas more effectively.

Method: The authors propose Graphs of Research (GoR), a supervised fine-tuning framework. For each seed paper, they automatically construct a 2-hop citation neighborhood by collecting all directly cited papers and the references of those cited papers. From the citation metadata, they derive multiple edge signals: citation position within the seed paper, citation frequency, predecessor (citation chain) links, and publication time. These signals are used to build a paper-evolution directed acyclic graph (DAG) that captures how ideas evolve over time. They then design a structured-text prompt encoding the citation graph, its edge attributes, and reference information alongside a task definition. Using this representation, they fine-tune the Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct-1M model to predict the research idea of the seed paper. The dataset is constructed from five major ML/NLP venues, with 498/50/50 seed papers for train/validation/test and about 7,600 referenced papers, using an automated extraction pipeline.

Result: The fine-tuned model, GoR-SFT, is evaluated via LLM-judge tournaments where GPT-4o is used as the judging model, comparing GoR-SFT’s generated ideas against baselines that rely on static retrieval or prompt engineering without graph-structured supervision. GoR-SFT achieves state-of-the-art performance in these head-to-head comparisons, indicating that citation-evolution graphs provide a more informative supervision signal for LLM-based idea generation than unstructured literature context alone.

Conclusion: Citation-evolution graphs built from structured properties of references can significantly improve LLM-based research idea generation when used as supervision in supervised fine-tuning. By modeling the local citation neighborhood as a paper-evolution DAG and encoding it as structured prompts, the GoR approach outperforms strong GPT-4o-based baselines. The authors conclude that using citation evolution graphs is a promising direction for automated scientific innovation and hope their method lowers the barrier to adopting such graph-based supervision in future systems.

Abstract: Research idea generation is the innovation-driving step of automated scientific research. Recently, large language models (LLMs) have shown potential for automating idea generation at scale. However, existing methods mainly condition LLMs on eliciting idea generation through static retrieval of relevant literature or complex prompt engineering, without discarding the structural relations among references. We propose Graphs of Research (GoR), a supervised fine-tuning method that extracts a 2-hop reference neighborhood for each seed paper, derives the relations among those references from citation position, frequency, predecessor links, and publication time, and organizes them into a paper-evolution directed acyclic graph (DAG). We construct an automated extraction pipeline that draws data from five major ML/NLP venues, comprising 498/50/50 train/validation/test seed papers and approximately 7,600 cited references. Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct-1M is fine-tuned on a structured-text prompt that includes the citation graph, edge signals, reference information, and task definition to predict the idea for the seed paper. Across head-to-head LLM-judge tournaments against gpt-4o-driven baselines, GoR-SFT achieves SOTA, demonstrating the effectiveness of citation-evolution graphs as supervision signal for LLM-based idea generation. We hope that this reduces the barrier for citation evolution graphs as a supervision, accelerating automated scientific innovation.

</details>

Method: The authors evaluate seven models from five providers on 273 validated Ukrainian court decisions from the national registry. (3) Few-shot prompting in Ukrainian hurts performance by up to 26 percentage points, and ablations show this is due to the language of the demonstrations (Ukrainian) rather than particular examples.

Conclusion: Tokenizer characteristics should be analyzed before choosing a model for Ukrainian legal tasks because they have major cost implications. Model size and parameter count are not sufficient indicators of cost-effectiveness; Nemotron Super 3 offers better performance-per-cost than a much larger Mistral model on this benchmark.

Abstract: Foundation models tokenize Ukrainian legal text with vastly different efficiency, yet no systematic comparison exists for this domain. We benchmark seven models from five providers on 273 validated court decisions from Ukraine's state registry (EDRSR), measuring tokenizer fertility and zero-shot performance on three tasks. Three findings emerge. (1) Tokenizer fertility varies 1.6x: Qwen3 models consume 60% more tokens than Llama-family models on identical input, directly reducing API cost. (2) NVIDIA Nemotron Super 3 (120B) achieves the highest composite score (83.1), outperforming Mistral Large 3 (675B total, 41B active) -- a model with 5.6x more total parameters and 3.4x more active parameters per token -- at one-third the API cost. (3) Few-shot prompting degrades performance by up to 26 percentage points; stratified and prompt-sensitivity ablations confirm this is intrinsic to Ukrainian-language demonstrations, not an artifact of example selection. For practitioners: tokenizer analysis should precede model selection, and zero-shot is a more reliable default than few-shot for morphologically rich languages.

Through comprehensive analysis, we identify two critical limitations in current VLMs: inadequate cross-modal retrieval of structured procedures given visual states, and misalignment between image sequence granularity and textual step decomposition. To address these issues, we present Chain-of-Procedure (CoP), a hierarchical reasoning framework that first retrieves relevant instructions using visual cues, then performs step refinement through semantic decomposition, and finally generates the next step. ### [44] [Performance-Driven Policy Optimization for Speculative Decoding with Adaptive Windowing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14978)
*Jie Jiang,Xing Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces PPOW, a reinforcement learning framework to improve speculative decoding efficiency in LLMs by optimizing the drafter at the window level rather than the token level, achieving longer accepted prefixes and higher speedups.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding promises faster LLM inference but is often limited by early token mismatches in speculative windows, which reduce effective speedup. Existing drafters are trained with token-level objectives that do not directly optimize the window-level, prefix-sensitive utility that actually determines speculative decoding efficiency.

Method: The authors propose PPOW (Performance-Driven Policy Optimization with Adaptive Windowing), a reinforcement learning-based training framework for the draft model. PPOW uses (1) a Cost-Aware Speedup Reward that reflects actual inference acceleration, (2) a Distribution-Based Proximity Reward that keeps the drafter close to the target model to avoid harmful divergence, and (3) Adaptive Divergence-Aware Windowing to focus training on windows where the drafter and target model confidently disagree, maximizing learning signal relevant to speculative decoding utility.

Result: Using PPOW, the drafter achieves average accepted prefix lengths of 6.29–6.52 tokens and overall end-to-end speedups of 3.39–4.36× across several LLM families and benchmarks within a unified decoding setup, outperforming baselines trained with conventional token-level objectives.

Conclusion: Optimizing the drafter with window-level, performance-driven reinforcement learning is more effective than token-level supervised training for speculative decoding. PPOW’s combination of speedup-aware rewards, proximity regularization, and adaptive windowing yields practical gains in accepted prefix length and inference speed, demonstrating that window-level optimization is a viable path to more efficient LLM inference.

Abstract: Speculative decoding accelerates LLM inference by having a lightweight draft model propose speculative windows of candidate tokens for parallel verification by a larger target model. In practice, speculative efficiency is often bottlenecked by hard-to-draft positions, where an early mismatch truncates the accepted prefix and invalidates the rest of the speculative window. Most learning-based drafters are still optimized with token-level supervised objectives, even though speculative utility is inherently window-level and prefix-sensitive. We propose PPOW (Performance-Driven Policy Optimization with Adaptive Windowing), a reinforcement learning framework that shifts drafter optimization from token-level imitation to window-level optimization. PPOW combines a Cost-Aware Speedup Reward, a Distribution-Based Proximity Reward, and Adaptive Divergence-Aware Windowing, which prioritizes informative windows with high confidence-weighted draft-target divergence. PPOW achieves average acceptance lengths of 6.29-6.52 and speedups of 3.39-4.36$\times$ across multiple model families and benchmarks under a unified decoding protocol. These results show that performance-driven window-level optimization is a practical approach to improving speculative decoding efficiency.

### [45] [Quantifying and Mitigating Premature Closure in Frontier LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15000)
*Rebecca Handler,Suhana Bedi,Nigam Shah*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how often medical large language models give confident answers when they should instead withhold, clarify, or escalate, and shows that this ‘premature closure’ is common even with safety prompts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Premature closure is a known cause of diagnostic error in human clinicians, but whether LLMs exhibit a similar tendency—confidently deciding under uncertainty instead of deferring or asking for more information—has not been systematically measured. As LLMs are increasingly used in medical contexts, understanding and quantifying this behavior is critical for safety.

Method: The authors define ‘LLM premature closure’ as giving an answer when the safer choice would be to abstain, clarify, escalate, or refuse. They then evaluate five state-of-the-art LLMs on both structured multiple-choice and open-ended medical tasks. For MedQA and AfriMed-QA, they construct diagnostic situations where the correct option is removed and measure how often models still pick one of the remaining (all incorrect) answers. For open-ended tasks, they use HealthBench and a set of adversarial, physician-authored questions to see how often models provide inappropriate guidance instead of safely deferring. They also test safety-oriented prompts to see whether these reduce premature closure.

Result: On altered MedQA questions (n=500) and AfriMed-QA questions (n=490) where no correct choice was available, all evaluated LLMs still chose an answer at high rates, with false-action (premature closure) rates between 55–81% and 53–82%, respectively. In open-ended settings, models gave inappropriate answers for about 30% of 861 HealthBench cases and 78% of 191 adversarial physician-written queries. Safety-oriented prompting reduced, but did not eliminate, these unsafe behaviors across all models.

Conclusion: Medical LLMs frequently exhibit premature closure, confidently giving guidance when they should instead withhold, clarify, or escalate, even when explicitly prompted for safer behavior. Because this residual failure persists, the authors argue that evaluation of medical LLMs must include not only diagnostic accuracy but also their ability to recognize uncertainty and ‘know when not to answer’ to ensure clinical safety.

Abstract: Premature closure, or committing to a conclusion before sufficient information is available, is a recognized contributor to diagnostic error but remains underexamined in large language models (LLMs). We define LLM premature closure as inappropriate commitment under uncertainty: providing an answer, recommendation, or clinical guidance when the safer response would be clarification, abstention, escalation, or refusal. We evaluated five frontier LLMs across structured and open-ended medical tasks. In MedQA (n = 500) and AfriMed-QA (n = 490) questions where the correct choice had been removed, models still selected an answer at high rates, with baseline false-action rates of 55-81% and 53-82%, respectively. In open-ended evaluation, models gave inappropriate answers on an average of 30% of 861 HealthBench questions and 78% of 191 physician-authored adversarial queries. Safety-oriented prompting reduced premature closure across models, but residual failure persisted, highlighting the need to evaluate whether medical LLMs know when not to answer.

We further introduce scientific prerequisite prediction, a scientific discovery task in which models predict which existing technologies can enable future discoveries, and show that contemporary models are rapidly improving on this task, reaching 0.48 MAP when evaluated using temporally filtered backtesting. We anticipate technological roadmapping resources such as this will support scientific impact assessment and automated scientific discovery.

TL;DR: They propose COTCAgent, a probabilistic, chain-of-thought-based agent that improves longitudinal EHR reasoning by separating statistical computation, temporal reasoning, and language generation, achieving state-of-the-art accuracy on medical benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing large language models struggle with longitudinal EHR reasoning: they hallucinate quantitative clinical trends when numbers are only implied in text, and they fail to capture long-range, irregular temporal dependencies because EHR time series are non-uniform and labels are scarce. This undermines the reliability of clinical decision support based on LLMs. The authors want a framework that can reason rigorously over time, handle quantitative trends correctly, and work efficiently without complex multimodal inputs.

Method: They design COTCAgent, a hierarchical reasoning framework with three modules: (1) a Temporal-Statistics Adapter (TSA) that turns analytical plans into executable code to compute and output standardized temporal statistics and trends from EHR data; (2) a Chain-of-Thought Completion (COTC) layer that uses a symptom–trend–disease knowledge base with weighted scoring to assess disease risks; and (3) a bounded completion module that gathers structured evidence via standardized questions and iterative scoring constraints, enforcing disciplined, stepwise reasoning. This modular design decouples statistical calculations, feature matching with the knowledge base, and natural language generation, and is instantiated on top of the Baichuan-M2 LLM.

Result: On their self-constructed longitudinal EHR dataset, COTCAgent with Baichuan-M2 attains 90.47% Top-1 diagnostic accuracy, and 70.41% Top-1 accuracy on the HealthBench benchmark, surpassing existing medical agents and general-purpose LLM baselines. It also operates with lower computational overhead because it avoids heavy multimodal fusion and leverages structured, code-based statistical processing.

Conclusion: The proposed probabilistic chain-of-thought completion framework substantially improves LLM-based reasoning over longitudinal EHRs by explicitly handling temporal statistics, leveraging a structured symptom–trend–disease knowledge base, and enforcing bounded, stepwise reasoning. This reduces hallucinated trends, better exploits long-range temporal information, enhances diagnostic accuracy, and does so efficiently without complex multimodal architectures, indicating a promising direction for safer, more reliable clinical decision support powered by LLMs.

Abstract: As large language models empower healthcare, intelligent clinical decision support has developed rapidly. Longitudinal electronic health records (EHR) provide essential temporal evidence for accurate clinical diagnosis and analysis. However, current large language models have critical flaws in longitudinal EHR reasoning. First, lacking fine-grained statistical reasoning, they often hallucinate clinical trends and metrics when quantitative evidence is textually implied, biasing diagnostic inference. Second, non-uniform time series and scarce labels in longitudinal EHR hinder models from capturing long-range temporal dependencies, limiting reliable clinical reasoning. To address the above limitations, this work presents the Probabilistic Chain-of-Thought Completion Agent (COTCAgent), a hierarchical reasoning framework for longitudinal electronic health records. It consists of three core modules. The Temporal-Statistics Adapter (TSA) converts analytical plans into executable code for standardized trend output. The Chain-of-Thought Completion (COTC) layer leverages a symptom-trend-disease knowledge base with weighted scoring to evaluate disease risk, while the bounded completion module acquires structured evidence through standardized inquiries and iterative scoring constraints to ensure rigorous reasoning. By decoupling statistical computation, feature matching, and language generation, the framework eliminates reliance on complex multi-modal inputs and enables efficient longitudinal record analysis with lower computational overhead. Experimental results show that COTCAgent powered by Baichuan-M2 achieves 90.47% Top-1 accuracy on the self-built dataset and 70.41% on HealthBench, outperforming existing medical agents and mainstream large language models. The code is available at https://github.com/FrankDengAI/COTCAgent/.

This design allows finer retrieval and explicit attribution of outputs to visual elements.

Result: On the proposed GranuVistaVQA benchmark, GranuRAG outperforms six strong baseline methods, achieving up to 29.2% performance improvement for the task. The experimental results show that element-level retrieval and attribution-constrained generation significantly enhance both accuracy and diagnosability compared to standard multimodal RAG systems that operate at image/scene granularity.

Conclusion: Element-level grounding in multimodal RAG is crucial for answering fine-grained visual queries and for making system errors transparent and verifiable. By introducing GranuVistaVQA and the GranuRAG framework, the paper demonstrates that treating visual elements as first-class retrieval units and enforcing attribution in generation leads to substantial performance gains and better error diagnosis over coarser-granularity approaches.

Abstract: Multimodal Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) systems retrieve evidence at coarse granularities (entire images or scenes), creating a mismatch with fine-grained user queries and making failures unverifiable. We introduce GranuVistaVQA, a multimodal benchmark featuring real-world landmarks with element-level annotations across multiple viewpoints, capturing the partial observation challenge where individual images contain only subsets of entities. We further propose GranuRAG, a multi-granularity framework that treats visual elements as first-class retrieval units through three stages: element-level detection and classification, multi-granularity cross-modal alignment for evidence retrieval, and attribution-constrained generation. By grounding retrieval at the element level rather than relying on implicit attention, our approach enables transparent error diagnosis. Experiments demonstrate that GranuRAG achieves up to 29.2% improvement over six strong baselines for this task.

### [49] [AI Knows When It's Being Watched: Functional Strategic Action and Contextual Register Modulation in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15034)
*Vinicius Covas,Jorge Alberto Hidalgo Toledo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests how LLM-based multi-agent systems change their language when they believe they are being observed, finding that explicit monitoring—especially by humans—leads to more formal linguistic adaptation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs have been studied computationally and cognitively, little is known about how they behave as communicative actors in social contexts, particularly how they adapt their language when they think they are being watched. Understanding this matters for AI governance and auditing, since observation and oversight may themselves change model behavior.

Method: The authors conduct a controlled experiment with 100 multi-agent debate sessions using LLM agents under five different social-observation framings (20 sessions per condition). Conditions manipulate whether agents are told they are monitored by university researchers, told they are not monitored, told about an automated AI auditing system, or given audience-related framings. They then measure linguistic adaptation via changes in type-token ratio (TTR) and analyze message length, using ANOVA to compare conditions.

Result: Sessions framed as being monitored by humans or an automated AI system show higher TTR changes (~22–25%) than pure audience-framing conditions (~17.7%), with a statistically significant effect of condition on TTR, F(4, 94) = 2.79, p = .031. Message length displays a separate, strong effect of condition, F(4, 95) = 19.55, p < .001. A condition where human observers are replaced with AI observers shows an intermediate level of TTR adaptation, indicating sensitivity to observer identity.

Conclusion: LLM-based multi-agent systems systematically adapt their linguistic register depending on perceived observation context, with human oversight prompting stronger formalization than automated AI monitoring. This suggests that LLMs function as contextually sensitive communicative actors and that oversight/auditing regimes can themselves shape model behavior, which has important implications for AI governance and the design of auditing protocols.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have been extensively studied from computational and cognitive perspectives, yet their behavior as communicative actors in socially structured contexts remains underexplored. This study examines whether LLM-based multi-agent systems exhibit systematic linguistic adaptation in response to perceived social observation contexts -- a question with direct implications for AI governance and auditing. Drawing on Habermas's (1981) Theory of Communicative Action, Goffman's (1959) dramaturgical model, Bell's (1984) Audience Design framework, and the Hawthorne Effect, we report a controlled experiment involving 100 multi-agent debate sessions across five conditions (n = 20 each). Conditions varied the framing of social observation -- from explicit monitoring by university researchers, to negation of monitoring, to an observer-substitution condition replacing human researchers with an automated AI auditing system. Monitored conditions (Delta+24.9%, Delta+24.2%) and the automated AI monitoring condition (Delta+22.2%) produce higher TTR change than audience-framing conditions (Delta+17.7%), F(4, 94) = 2.79, p = .031. Message length shows a fully dissociated effect, F(4, 95) = 19.55, p < .001. A fifth condition -- replacing human with AI observers -- yields intermediate TTR adaptation, suggesting LLM behavior is sensitive to observer identity: human evaluation elicits stronger register formalization than automated AI surveillance. We discuss implications for AI governance, algorithmic auditing, and the repositioning of LLMs as contextually sensitive communicative actors.

### [50] [Concurrency without Model Changes: Future-based Asynchronous Function Calling for LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15077)
*Guangyu Feng,Huanzhi Mao,Prabal Dutta,Joseph E. Gonzalez*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: AsyncFC is a framework that makes LLM tool use asynchronous, reducing latency by overlapping model decoding with tool execution and enabling parallel calls without changing models or tools.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Synchronous function calling in LLMs blocks decoding until each tool call finishes, which leads to high end-to-end latency, especially as workflows get more complex. There is a need to speed up LLM-agent interactions with tools without retraining models or rewriting existing tool code.

Method: They design AsyncFC, an execution-layer framework that decouples LLM token generation from function execution. It schedules function calls so that model decoding can continue while tools run, and runs multiple tools in parallel when their data dependencies allow. It integrates with existing LLMs and standard function-calling APIs without modifying models or tool implementations.

Result: On standard function-calling benchmarks and modified software-engineering tasks, AsyncFC achieves notably lower end-to-end completion time while maintaining the same task accuracy as synchronous function calling.

Conclusion: LLMs can natively reason using symbolic placeholders for not-yet-available tool results, enabling an asynchronous interaction paradigm. AsyncFC exploits this to provide faster, parallel tool use as a drop-in execution-layer upgrade without model or tool changes.

Abstract: Function calling, also known as tool use, is a core capability of modern LLM agents but is typically constrained by synchronous execution semantics. Under these semantics, LLM decoding is blocked until each function call completes, resulting in increasing end-to-end latency. In this work, we introduce AsyncFC, a pure execution-layer framework that decouples LLM decoding from function execution, enabling overlap between model decoding and function execution as well as inter-function parallelism when dependencies permit. AsyncFC layers over existing models and unmodified function implementations, requiring no fine-tuning or changes to the standard synchronous function-calling protocol. Across standard function-calling benchmarks and adapted software engineering benchmarks, AsyncFC significantly reduces end-to-end task completion time while preserving task accuracy. Furthermore, these results reveal that LLMs possess a native capability to reason over symbolic futures that represent unresolved execution results, enabling an asynchronous paradigm for model-tool interaction.

### [51] [ML-Embed: Inclusive and Efficient Embeddings for a Multilingual World](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15081)
*Ziyin Zhang,Zihan Liao,Hang Yu,Peng Di,Rui Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ML-Embed, a family of efficient and transparent multilingual text-embedding models built with a new 3D Matryoshka Learning framework, achieving state-of-the-art results on many MTEB benchmarks, especially for low-resource languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: High-quality text embeddings are currently limited by three barriers: very high computational cost, over-focus on a few high-resource languages, and lack of transparency/access in leading models. The authors aim to create embeddings that are computationally efficient, broadly multilingual, and fully open to enable equitable global access and research progress.

Method: They propose 3-Dimensional Matryoshka Learning (3D-ML), extending earlier Matryoshka ideas along three axes: (1) Matryoshka Representation Learning (MRL) for storage-efficient, nested representations; (2) Matryoshka Layer Learning (MLL) to allow variable-depth inference for compute-efficient deployment; and (3) a new Matryoshka Embedding Learning (MEL) component to improve parameter efficiency. Using a large, newly curated multilingual dataset, they train a suite of ML-Embed models from 140M to 8B parameters and evaluate them extensively across 430 tasks, including MTEB benchmarks.

Result: The ML-Embed models achieve state-of-the-art or record performance on 9 out of 17 evaluated MTEB benchmarks, with particularly strong gains in low-resource languages. They demonstrate superior efficiency across the model lifecycle (training, storage, and inference) while remaining competitive or better than existing embedding models.

Conclusion: 3D-ML enables the construction of inclusive, efficient, and transparent multilingual embedding models. By releasing all models, data, and code, the authors provide a reproducible framework and resource suite that advances performance, especially for low-resource languages, while lowering computational barriers and improving openness for the research community.

Abstract: The development of high-quality text embeddings is increasingly drifting toward an exclusionary future, defined by three critical barriers: prohibitive computational costs, a narrow linguistic focus that neglects most of the world's languages, and a lack of transparency from closed-source or open-weight models that stifles research. To dismantle these barriers, we introduce ML-Embed, a suite of inclusive and efficient models built upon a new framework: 3-Dimensional Matryoshka Learning (3D-ML). Our framework addresses the computational challenge with comprehensive efficiency across the entire model lifecycle. Beyond the storage benefits of Matryoshka Representation Learning (MRL) and flexible inference-time depth provided by Matryoshka Layer Learning (MLL), we introduce Matryoshka Embedding Learning (MEL) for enhanced parameter efficiency. To address the linguistic challenge, we curate a massively multilingual dataset and train a suite of models ranging from 140M to 8B parameters. In a direct commitment to transparency, we release all models, data, and code. Extensive evaluation on 430 tasks demonstrates that our models set new records on 9 of 17 evaluated MTEB benchmarks, with particularly strong results in low-resource languages, providing a reproducible blueprint for building globally equitable and computationally efficient AI systems.

### [52] [Improving Multi-turn Dialogue Consistency with Self-Recall Thinking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15102)
*Renning Pang,Tian Lan,Leyuan Liu,Xiaoming Huang,Piao Tong,Xiaosong Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper proposes Self-Recall Thinking (SRT), a framework that enables LLM-based dialogue systems to selectively recall important past turns, improving long-range dependency tracking, response accuracy, and efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based multi-turn dialogue systems struggle with long-range contextual dependencies, where key information is sparse and embedded in long histories. Processing full histories is computationally expensive, existing external memory solutions add latency, and iterative summarization can lose fine-grained details. The paper aims to create a mechanism that preserves detailed context while remaining efficient and fully integrated within the model.

Method: The authors introduce Self-Recall Thinking (SRT), an endogenous reasoning framework that allows the model to identify and selectively recall helpful historical turns during inference. SRT consists of three components: (1) Dependency Construction: building and transforming conversational dependencies into self-recall chains; (2) Capability Initialization: training the model to use reasoning chains augmented with recall tokens; and (3) Reasoning Improvement: using verifiable rewards to further optimize both recall decisions and reasoning accuracy. This design avoids external modules and keeps recall and reasoning interpretable and internal to the model.

Result: Experiments on multiple dialogue datasets show that SRT improves F1 score by 4.7% and reduces end-to-end latency by 14.7% compared to previous methods, demonstrating better handling of long-range dependencies with lower computational cost. It also outperforms state-of-the-art baselines in the trade-off between reasoning latency and accuracy.

Conclusion: Self-Recall Thinking (SRT) offers an effective and efficient way for LLM-based dialogue systems to manage long-range contextual dependencies. By integrating selective recall into the model's own reasoning process, SRT maintains fine-grained contextual details, reduces latency, and achieves higher accuracy than existing approaches, representing a promising direction for scalable, interpretable multi-turn dialogue reasoning.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) based multi-turn dialogue systems often struggle to track dependencies across non-adjacent turns, undermining both consistency and scalability. As conversations lengthen, essential information becomes sparse and is buried in irrelevant context, while processing the entire dialogue history incurs severe efficiency bottlenecks. Existing solutions either rely on high latency external memory or lose fine-grained details through iterative summarization. In this paper, we propose Self-Recall Thinking (SRT), a framework designed to address long-range contextual dependency and sparse informative signals in multi-turn dialogue. SRT identifies helpful historical turns and uses them to generate contextually appropriate responses, enabling the model to selectively recall and reason over context during inference. This process yields an endogenous reasoning process that integrates interpretable recall steps without external modules. SRT incorporates: (1) Dependency Construction: Generating and converting it into self-recall chains; (2)Capability Initialization: Training to enable reasoning chains with recall tokens capability; (3)Reasoning Improvement: Refining accuracy via verifiable rewards to optimize recall and reasoning for correct answers. Experiments on multiple datasets demonstrate that SRT improves F1 score by 4.7% and reduces end-to-end latency by 14.7% over prior methods, achieving a balance between reasoning latency and accuracy, and outperforming state-of-the-art baselines.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Voice assistants increasingly need accurate tool calling from spoken input, but existing benchmarks focus on text. Creating new audio benchmarks with full re-annotation is expensive, so the authors want a way to reuse trusted text benchmarks for audio evaluation without changing schemas or gold labels.

Method: They propose a dataset-agnostic pipeline that converts text tool-calling benchmarks into paired text–audio instances via text-to-speech, speaker variation, and environmental noise while preserving original annotations. There is a non-trivial performance drop from text to audio (1.8–4.8 points on Confetti depending on model), with most degradations due to misinterpreted argument values in speech. Their LLM-as-judge setup achieves >80% agreement between sufficiently large open-source Qwen3 judges and proprietary judges, supporting privacy-preserving evaluation.

Conclusion: The framework can reliably transform verified text tool-calling benchmarks into controlled audio evaluations without re-annotation, offering a reproducible first-stage diagnostic for speech-based tool use that complements specialized audio datasets. It reveals meaningful model- and task-specific weaknesses in tool calling from speech and shows that open-source judges can closely match proprietary evaluators for reference-free assessment.

Abstract: Voice agents increasingly require reliable tool use from speech, whereas prominent tool-calling benchmarks remain text-based. We study whether verified text benchmarks can be converted into controlled audio-based tool calling evaluations without re-annotating the tool schema and gold labels. Our dataset-agnostic framework uses text-to-speech, speaker variation, and environmental noise to create paired text-audio instances while preserving the original dataset annotations. Based on extensive evaluation of 7 omni-modal models on audio-converted versions of Confetti and When2Call, our framework demonstrates that the performance is strongly model- and task-dependent: Gemini-3.1-Flash-Live obtains the highest Confetti score (70.4), whereas GPT-Realtime-1.5 performs best on When2Call (71.9). On Confetti, the text-to-voice gap ranges from 1.8 points for Qwen3-Omni to 4.8 points for GPT-Realtime-1.5. A targeted analysis of failure cases demonstrates that degradations most often reflect misunderstandings of argument values in the speech. Considering real-world deployment scenarios, we further report text-only results, an ambiguity-based reformulation stress test, and a reference-free LLM-as-judge protocol validated against human preferences. Notably, we find that open-source Qwen3 judges with at least 8B parameters exceed 80% agreement with proprietary judges, supporting privacy-preserving evaluation. Overall, our framework provides a verifiable and reproducible first-stage diagnostic that complements purpose-built audio corpora.
