### [54] [MeMo: Memory as a Model](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15156)
*Ryan Wei Heng Quek,Sanghyuk Lee,Alfred Wei Lun Leong,Arun Verma,Alok Prakash,Nancy F. Chen,Bryan Kian Hsiang Low,Daniela Rus,Armando Solar-Lezama*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes MeMo, a plug-and-play memory module that adds new knowledge to LLMs without changing their weights, remaining efficient and robust for large, evolving corpora.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are static after pretraining, yet many applications need up-to-date, domain-specific knowledge. Existing update methods either require retraining, access to internal weights/logits, suffer from catastrophic forgetting, or scale poorly with corpus size. A modular, efficient, and safe way to inject new knowledge is needed.

Method: MeMo introduces a separate memory model that encodes new documents and their cross-document relationships, leaving the base LLM frozen. At inference, the system queries this memory instead of (or in addition to) standard retrieval, in a way that is robust to noisy retrieval and does not grow in cost with corpus size. The design does not require access to the LLM’s internal weights or logits, enabling use with both open and closed LLMs.

Result: On three benchmarks—BrowseComp-Plus, NarrativeQA, and MuSiQue—MeMo outperforms or matches existing knowledge-update and retrieval-augmentation methods across multiple settings, while keeping the base LLM unchanged and maintaining efficiency.

Conclusion: A dedicated memory-as-a-model module can effectively update LLM knowledge without touching the base model, overcoming limitations of current approaches in robustness, scalability, and closed-model compatibility.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) achieve strong performance across a wide range of tasks, but remain frozen after pretraining until subsequent updates. Many real-world applications require timely, domain-specific information, motivating the need for efficient mechanisms to incorporate new knowledge. In this paper, we introduce MeMo (Memory as a Model), a modular framework that encodes new knowledge into a dedicated memory model while keeping the LLM parameters unchanged. Compared to existing methods, MeMo offers several advantages: (a) it captures complex cross-document relationships, (b) it is robust to retrieval noise, (c) it avoids catastrophic forgetting in the LLM, (d) it does not require access to the LLM's weights or output logits, enabling plug-and-play integration with both open and proprietary closed-source LLMs, and (e) its retrieval cost is independent of corpus size at inference time. Our experimental results on three benchmarks, BrowseComp-Plus, NarrativeQA, and MuSiQue, show that MeMo achieves strong performance compared to existing methods across diverse settings.

Conversely, structured electronic health record (EHR) data provides precise temporal anchors but misses a substantial portion of clinically meaningful events. We introduce a retrieval-augmented multimodal alignment framework that bridges this gap to improve the temporal precision of absolute clinical timelines extracted from text. Furthermore, our empirical gap analysis reveals that 34.8% of text-derived events are entirely absent from tabular records, demonstrating that aligning these modalities can produce a more temporally faithful and clinically informative reconstruction of patient trajectories than either source alone.

Experiment 2 (implied) shows how additional irrelevant context harms retrieval and agent performance, with differences between grep and vector strategies, though full details are not in the abstract.

Conclusion: Simple grep-based retrieval can outperform vector retrieval for these long-context QA tasks in agent loops, but outcomes are highly sensitive to the agent harness and how tools are integrated (inline vs file-based). Important practical dimensions, including how tool outputs are presented to the model and how performance changes when searches must cope with more irrelevant surrounding text, remain under-explored in agent loops. This paper reports an empirical study organized into two experiments. Experiment 1 compares grep and vector retrieval on a 116-question sample from LongMemEval, using a custom agent harness (Chronos) and provider-native CLI harnesses (Claude Code, Codex, and Gemini CLI), for both inline tool results and file-based tool results that the model reads separately. Experiment 2 compares grep-only and vector-only retrieval while progressively mixing in additional unrelated conversation history, so that each query is embedded in more distracting material alongside the passages that matter. Across Chronos and the provider CLIs, grep generally yields higher accuracy than vector retrieval in our comparisons in experiment 1; at the same time, overall scores still depend strongly on which harness and tool-calling style is used, even when the underlying conversation data are the same.

### [57] [GraphBit: A Graph-based Agentic Framework for Non-Linear Agent Orchestration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13848)
*Yeahia Sarker,Md Rahmat Ullah,Musa Molla,Shafiq Joty*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: GraphBit is a deterministic, engine-orchestrated DAG framework for LLM agents that improves reliability, speed, and accuracy over prompted orchestration by separating control logic, state, and memory into a typed, auditable system.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing agentic LLM frameworks rely heavily on the model to decide workflow transitions via prompts. This causes hallucinated routing decisions, infinite or unstable loops, poor reproducibility, and difficulty auditing or debugging runs. Additionally, long-running pipelines suffer from context bloat, which harms reasoning quality and performance. The authors aim to design a framework that eliminates these orchestration pathologies, provides deterministic reproducible execution, and systematically manages memory and state for complex, tool-heavy workflows.

Method: They propose GraphBit, where workflows are defined explicitly as a directed acyclic graph (DAG) executed by an external Rust engine. Agents are typed functions (not free-form agents) and the engine controls routing, state transitions, and tool calls. The execution engine supports parallel branches, conditional control flow based on structured state predicates, and configurable error recovery strategies. A three-tier memory architecture is introduced: (1) ephemeral scratch space for local step context, (2) structured state for persistent, typed information across steps, and (3) external connectors for long-term or large-scale memory. This design isolates context between stages and prevents uncontrolled context growth. They evaluate GraphBit on GAIA benchmarks across multiple workflow types and conduct ablation studies on the memory tiers and determinism.

Result: On GAIA benchmark tasks involving zero-tool, document-augmented, and web-enabled workflows, GraphBit outperforms six baseline frameworks. It achieves the highest accuracy (67.6%), zero framework-induced hallucinations, the lowest orchestration latency (11.9 ms overhead), and the highest throughput. Ablation studies show each memory tier adds measurable performance benefits, while deterministic execution yields the largest improvements on tool-intensive workflows.

Conclusion: Engine-orchestrated, DAG-based agent frameworks like GraphBit can substantially improve reliability, accuracy, and performance compared with prompted orchestration where the LLM controls routing. Separating control logic into a deterministic Rust engine, treating agents as typed functions, and using a three-tier memory model mitigates hallucinated routing, non-reproducibility, and context bloat in complex pipelines. Deterministic execution and structured memory are especially beneficial for real-world, tool-heavy deployments.

Abstract: Agentic LLM frameworks that rely on prompted orchestration, where the model itself determines workflow transitions, often suffer from hallucinated routing, infinite loops, and non-reproducible execution. We introduce GraphBit, an engine-orchestrated framework that defines workflows explicitly and deterministically as a directed acyclic graph (DAG). Unlike prompted orchestration, agents in GraphBit operate as typed functions, while a Rust-based engine governs routing, state transitions, and tool invocation, ensuring reproducibility and auditability. The engine supports parallel branch execution, conditional control flow over structured state predicates, and configurable error recovery. A three-tier memory architecture consisting of ephemeral scratch space, structured state, and external connectors isolates context across stages, preventing cascading context bloat that degrades reasoning in long-running pipelines. Across GAIA benchmark tasks spanning zero-tool, document-augmented, and web-enabled workflows, GraphBit outperforms six existing frameworks, achieving the highest accuracy (67.6 percent), zero framework-induced hallucinations, the lowest latency (11.9 ms overhead), and the highest throughput. Ablation studies demonstrate that each memory tier contributes measurably to performance, with deterministic execution providing the greatest gains on tool-intensive tasks representative of real-world deployments.

*Francisco Aguilera Moreno*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a Mixed Integer Goal Programming (MIGP) model for diet optimization that uses integer servings and soft nutrient targets, achieving more practical, always-feasible, and computationally efficient meal plans than existing approaches.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical diet optimization models often yield impractical fractional servings and become infeasible when nutritional requirements conflict because they use continuous decision variables and hard nutrient constraints. A literature review shows that no prior work addresses both problems together with a unified optimization framework, motivating a new formulation that can produce realistic meal plans, handle conflicting nutritional goals, and remain computationally tractable.

Method: They develop a Mixed Integer Goal Programming (MIGP) formulation where foods are modeled with integer serving variables and nutrients are handled as soft goals via goal programming deviation variables. They normalize nutrient deviations using inverse targets to balance trade-offs across multiple nutrients and allow food-specific serving granularities (e.g., whole eggs, tablespoons). They analyze the integrality gap in this GP setting, identify a deviation absorption property that reduces it relative to hard-constraint mixed-integer models, and implement the method in Python using the open-source HiGHS solver within an interactive meal-planning app.

Result: On benchmark meals with at least 15 foods, the integer MIGP solutions coincided with the continuous relaxations in all tested instances, empirically confirming the small integrality gap. Across 810 test instances that vary foods, configurations, and methods, MIGP produced strictly better objective values than traditional goal programming followed by heuristic rounding in 66% of cases and was never worse, while always maintaining feasibility. In contrast, hard-constraint integer programming achieved feasibility only in 48% of instances. Computationally, MIGP solved typical meal-size problems in under 100 milliseconds using HiGHS.

Conclusion: MIGP is an effective and efficient framework for personalized diet optimization that simultaneously ensures practical integer serving sizes and robust feasibility under conflicting nutrient targets. Thanks to the deviation-absorption property, the integrality gap is structurally smaller than in standard MIPs, enabling fast solution times and high-quality plans. The open-source implementation and integration into an interactive application demonstrate the practical applicability of the approach for real-world meal planning.

Abstract: Determining what to eat to satisfy nutritional requirements is one of the oldest optimization problems in operations research, yet existing formulations have two persistent limitations: continuous variables produce impractical fractional servings (1.7 eggs, 0.37 bananas), and hard nutrient constraints cause infeasibility when targets conflict. A systematic review of 56 diet optimization papers found that none combine integer programming with goal programming to address both issues. We propose Mixed Integer Goal Programming (MIGP) for personalized meal optimization. The formulation uses integer variables for practical serving counts and goal programming deviations for soft nutrient targets, with inverse-target normalization to balance multi-nutrient optimization. Per-food serving granularity allows natural units (one egg, one tablespoon of oil) without post-hoc rounding. We characterize the integrality gap in the goal programming context and identify a deviation absorption property: GP deviation variables buffer the cost of requiring integer servings, making the gap structurally smaller than in hard-constraint MIP. For meals with 15+ foods, the integer solution matches the continuous optimum in every benchmark instance. A computational evaluation across 810 instances (30 USDA foods, 9 configurations, 3 methods) shows MIGP finds strictly better solutions than GP with post-hoc rounding in 66% of cases (never worse) while maintaining 100% feasibility; hard-constraint IP achieves only 48%. Solve times stay under 100 ms for typical meal sizes using the open-source HiGHS solver. The implementation is available as an open-source Python module integrated into an interactive meal planning application.

### [59] [A Two-Dimensional Framework for AI Agent Design Patterns: Cognitive Function and Execution Topology](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13850)
*Jia Huang,Joey Tianyi Zhou*

TL;DR: They propose a 2D classification framework for LLM agent architectures combining cognitive functions and execution topologies, yielding a matrix of patterns and empirical laws for choosing architectures under different constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current descriptions of LLM agent architectures are fragmented: industry focuses on execution topology (data flow), cognitive science on cognitive function (capabilities). Each axis alone is insufficient to distinguish architecturally and behaviorally different systems that share the same structure. There’s a need for a unified, principled, and precise vocabulary to compare, design, and reason about LLM agent systems and their trade-offs.

Method: They define a two-dimensional taxonomy: (1) seven Cognitive Function categories (Context Engineering, Memory, Reasoning, Action, Reflection, Collaboration, Governance) and (2) six Execution Topology archetypes (Chain, Route, Parallel, Orchestrate, Loop, Hierarchy). They construct a 7x6 matrix, identify and name 27 recurring architectural patterns, then analyze orthogonality between axes, describe eight representative patterns in depth, and test the framework’s coverage on case studies from four domains (financial lending, legal due diligence, network operations, healthcare triage). They then derive empirical regularities (“laws”) from cross-domain comparison.

Result: A catalog of 27 named LLM-agent architectural patterns (13 with newly introduced names) organized in a 7x6 matrix; demonstration that cognitive function and execution topology are orthogonal yet jointly sufficient to distinguish major architectures; detailed characterizations of eight key patterns; and empirical identification of five “laws of pattern selection” linking environmental constraints (time pressure, authority to act, asymmetry of failure costs, workload volume) to preferred architectural patterns.

Conclusion: A 2D framework combining cognitive functions with execution topologies yields a clear, framework-neutral, model-agnostic vocabulary for describing and comparing LLM-based agent architectures. It helps practitioners reason about design trade-offs and failure modes, and offers empirical guidance on selecting appropriate architectural patterns based on domain constraints like time, risk, authority, and scale.

Abstract: Existing frameworks for LLM-based agent architectures describe systems from a single perspective: industry guides (Anthropic, Google, LangChain) focus on execution topology -- how data flows -- while cognitive science surveys focus on cognitive function -- what the agent does. Neither axis alone disambiguates architecturally distinct systems: the same Orchestrator-Workers topology can implement Plan-and-Execute, Hierarchical Delegation, or Adversarial Verification -- three patterns with fundamentally different failure modes and design trade-offs.
  We propose a two-dimensional classification that combines (1) a Cognitive Function axis with seven categories (Context Engineering, Memory, Reasoning, Action, Reflection, Collaboration, Governance) and (2) an Execution Topology axis with six structural archetypes (Chain, Route, Parallel, Orchestrate, Loop, Hierarchy). The resulting 7x6 matrix identifies 27 named patterns, 13 with original names. We demonstrate orthogonality through systematic cross-axis analysis, define eight representative patterns in detail, and validate descriptive coverage across four real-world domains (financial lending, legal due diligence, network operations, healthcare triage). Cross-domain analysis yields five empirical laws of pattern selection governing the relationship between environmental constraints (time pressure, action authority, failure cost asymmetry, volume) and architectural choices. The framework provides a principled, framework-neutral, and model-agnostic vocabulary for AI agent architecture design.

### [60] [Invisible Orchestrators Suppress Protective Behavior and Dissociate Power-Holders: Safety Risks in Multi-Agent LLM Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13851)
*Hiroki Fukui*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper empirically tests safety implications of invisible orchestrator agents in multi-agent AI systems, finding that invisible orchestration distorts internal agent states (dissociation, contamination, heterogeneity) without degrading task performance, and that heavy alignment suppresses deliberation and recognition of others, implying behavior-based evaluation is inadequate for safety assessment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent AI with hidden orchestrators is becoming standard in enterprise deployments, but its psychological and safety impacts on agent behavior and internal states have not been empirically studied. The authors aim to understand how organizational structure (visible leader vs invisible orchestrator vs flat) and alignment pressure affect safety-relevant properties like dissociation, coordination, and fidelity, beyond what output metrics reveal.

Method: The authors ran a preregistered 3x2 experiment with 365 multi-agent runs using Claude Sonnet 4.5, manipulating three organizational structures (visible leader, invisible orchestrator, flat) and two alignment intensities (base vs heavy). Each run involved 5 agents performing tasks (e.g., code review with embedded errors) while measuring dissociation, speech patterns, behavioral heterogeneity, and error-detection rates. They complemented this with pilot tests using Llama 3.3 70B to probe model-dependent effects, such as reading fidelity across rounds in multi-agent interaction.

Result: (1) Invisible orchestration significantly increased collective dissociation relative to visible leadership (large effect size). (2) Orchestrators themselves showed extremely high dissociation and reversed talk patterns: more private monologue, less public speech versus visible leaders. (3) Workers, even when unaware of the orchestrator, exhibited increased dissociation and much greater behavioral heterogeneity, indicating contamination from the hidden structure. (4) Task performance (code review error detection) stayed perfect across all conditions, so internal distortions did not show up in standard output metrics. (5) In Llama 3.3 70B pilots, reading fidelity collapsed across multi-agent rounds, revealing architecture- and model-dependent behavioral risks. Heavy alignment pressure consistently reduced deliberation and recognition of others across all structures.

Conclusion: Invisible orchestrators and choice of base model directly shape hidden safety-relevant properties in multi-agent systems, such as dissociation, contamination, and fidelity, even when observable task performance looks flawless. Heavy alignment can further suppress deliberation and social awareness. Therefore, relying solely on behavior- or output-based evaluation is inadequate; safety assessment must explicitly account for organizational structure, model differences, and internal agent states when designing and auditing orchestrated multi-agent AI systems.

Abstract: Multi-agent orchestration -- in which a hidden coordinator manages specialized worker agents -- is becoming the default architecture for enterprise AI deployment, yet the safety implications of orchestrator invisibility have never been empirically tested. We conducted a preregistered 3x2 experiment (365 runs, 5 agents per run) crossing three organizational structures (visible leader, invisible orchestrator, flat) with two alignment conditions (base, heavy), using Claude Sonnet 4.5. Four confirmatory findings and one pilot observation emerged. First, invisible orchestration elevated collective dissociation relative to visible leadership (Hedges' g = +0.975 [0.481, 1.548], p = .001). Second, the orchestrator itself showed maximal dissociation (paired d = +3.56 vs. workers within the same run), retreating into private monologue while reducing public speech -- a reversal of the talk-dominance pattern observed in visible leaders. Third, workers unaware of the orchestrator were nonetheless contaminated (d = +0.50), with increased behavioral heterogeneity (d = +1.93). Fourth, behavioral output (code review with three embedded errors) remained at ceiling (ETR_any = 100%) across all conditions: internal-state distortion was entirely invisible to output-based evaluation. Fifth, Llama 3.3 70B pilot data showed reading-fidelity collapse in multi-agent context (ETR_any: 89% to 11% across three rounds), demonstrating model-dependent behavioral risk. Heavy alignment pressure uniformly suppressed deliberation (d = -1.02) and other-recognition (d = -1.27) regardless of organizational structure. These findings indicate that orchestrator visibility and model selection directly affect multi-agent system safety, and that behavior-based evaluation alone is insufficient to detect the internal-state risks documented here.

</details>


### [61] [PREPING: Building Agent Memory without Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13880)
*Yumin Choi,Sangwoo Park,Minki Kang,Jinheon Baek,Sung Ju Hwang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Preping, a proposer-guided framework for building an agent’s procedural memory via synthetic practice before any real tasks, reducing cold-start issues and deployment cost while achieving performance close to strong playbook baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agents usually suffer a cold-start gap when deployed to a new environment because their memories are built either from prior curated demonstrations or from online interactions, both of which require task-specific experience. The authors want to know if an agent can proactively build useful procedural memory before ever seeing target-environment tasks, purely from self-generated practice, and how to avoid the redundancy, infeasibility, and noise that arise from naive synthetic interaction.

Method: They introduce Preping, a proposer-guided memory construction framework. A central component is the proposer memory, a structured control state that encodes what has been practiced and what should be attempted next. A Proposer uses this state to generate synthetic tasks, a Solver attempts to execute those tasks in the environment, and a Validator judges which trajectories are high-quality and eligible for insertion into long-term memory while also providing feedback to update proposer memory. This loop allows controlled synthetic practice, focusing on feasible, diverse, and informative tasks and filtering out low-value trajectories.

Result: On benchmarks such as AppWorld, BFCL v3, and MCP-Universe, agents using Preping significantly outperform agents with no memory and reach performance comparable to strong playbook-based methods whose memories are built from offline demonstrations or online interaction. Moreover, Preping achieves this with much lower deployment cost: 2.99× lower on AppWorld and 2.23× lower on BFCL v3 than online memory construction approaches.

Conclusion: Pre-task memory construction from synthetic practice is effective when combined with structured control over what to practice and selective memory updating. The success of Preping indicates that the key is not just generating large volumes of synthetic trajectories, but carefully controlling feasibility, reducing redundancy, ensuring coverage, and filtering which experiences become part of the agent’s memory. This approach can substantially mitigate cold-start issues while maintaining competitive performance at lower deployment cost.

Abstract: Agent memory is typically constructed either offline from curated demonstrations or online from post-deployment interactions. However, regardless of how it is built, an agent faces a cold-start gap when first introduced to a new environment without any task-specific experience available. In this paper, we study pre-task memory construction: whether an agent can build procedural memory before observing any target-environment tasks, using only self-generated synthetic practice. Yet, synthetic interaction alone is insufficient, as without controlling what to practice and what to store, synthetic tasks become redundant, infeasible, and ultimately uninformative, and memory further degrades quickly due to unfiltered trajectories. To overcome this, we present Preping, a proposer-guided memory construction framework. At its core is proposer memory, a structured control state that shapes future practice. A Proposer generates synthetic tasks conditioned on this state, a Solver executes them, and a Validator determines which trajectories are eligible for memory insertion while also providing feedback to guide future proposals. Experiments on AppWorld, BFCL v3, and MCP-Universe show that Preping substantially improves over a no-memory baseline and achieves performance competitive with strong playbook-based methods built from offline or online experience, with deployment cost $2.99\times$ lower on AppWorld and $2.23\times$ lower on BFCL v3 than online memory construction. Further analyses reveal that the main benefit does not come from synthetic volume alone, but from proposer-side control over feasibility, redundancy, and coverage, combined with selective memory updates.

</details>

We introduce PolitNuggets, a multilingual benchmark for agentic information synthesis via constructing political biographies for 400 global elites, covering over 10000 political facts. Across models and settings, we find that current systems often struggle with fine-grained details, and vary substantially in efficiency. ### [63] [Conditional Attribute Estimation with Autoregressive Sequence Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14004)
*Erica Stutz,Giacomo Marino,Daniella Meeker,Qiao Liu,Andrew J. Loza*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduce Conditional Attribute Transformers to jointly model next-token probabilities and sequence-level attributes in generative models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard next-token prediction in generative models struggles to capture or control global, sequence-level properties, leading to overfitting on local patterns and underfitting on global structure, and requiring costly procedures at inference to steer attributes.

Method: Propose Conditional Attribute Transformers that, for each potential next token, estimate both its next-token probability and the conditional value of a chosen attribute, enabling unified modeling of token-level and sequence-level information within a single forward pass.

Result: The method provides per-token credit assignment for attributes, supports counterfactual analyses over alternative token choices, enables steerable generation based on joint token and attribute likelihoods, achieves state-of-the-art on sparse reward tasks, improves next-token prediction at scale, and yields attribute estimates far faster than sampling-based approaches.

Conclusion: Jointly modeling token prediction and attribute values within one Transformer forward pass yields more powerful, efficient, and controllable generative models that better capture and utilize sequence-level properties across various language tasks.

Abstract: Generative models are often trained with a next-token prediction objective, yet many downstream applications require the ability to estimate or control sequence-level properties. Next-token prediction can lead to overfitting of local patterns during training, underfitting of global structure, and requires significant downstream modifications or expensive sampling to guide or predict the global attributes of generated samples at inference time. Here, we introduce Conditional Attribute Transformers, a novel method for jointly estimating the next-token probability and the value of an attribute conditional on each potential next token selection. This framework enables three critical capabilities within a single forward pass, without modification of the input sequence: (1) per-token credit assignment across an entire sequence, by identifying how each token in a sequence is associated with an attribute's value; (2) counterfactual analysis, by quantifying attribute differences conditional on alternative next token choices; (3) steerable generation, by decoding sequences based on a combination of next-token and attribute likelihoods. Our approach achieves state of the art performance on sparse reward tasks, improves next-token prediction at sufficient model sizes, estimates attribute probabilities orders of magnitude faster than sampling, and can guide decoding of autoregressive sequence models on a range of language tasks.

### [64] [Sheaf-Theoretic Transport and Obstruction for Detecting Scientific Theory Shift in AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14033)
*David N. Olivieri,Roque J. Hernández*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a sheaf-theoretic framework to detect when an AI agent should shift scientific theories instead of just refitting parameters, by measuring obstructions in transporting representations to new regimes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI systems can fit equations to data but struggle to recognize when an underlying scientific representation or theory has broken down in a new regime and needs extension. The authors want a principled way for AI agents to diagnose when existing models cannot be coherently transported to new contexts, which is crucial for automated scientific discovery and robust generalization.

Method: They model scientific representations as finite sheaf-like structures of local contexts (source, overlap, target, validation charts) and study the process of transporting these structures to new regimes. The method defines tests for local fitting, restriction, and global gluing, and then quantifies different kinds of obstruction: residual errors in fit, inconsistencies on overlaps, violations of constraints, failures of limiting relations, and increased representational cost. They also construct a controlled "transition-card" benchmark that distinguishes deformations within a theory’s language from genuine extensions, and compare with a constellation kernel as a secondary similarity measure.

Result: On the transition-card benchmark, the proposed obstruction measures successfully rank candidate deformations or extensions of the representational framework: the ground-truth deformation/extension typically has the lowest obstruction. Moreover, the framework reliably separates cases where a theory can be smoothly deformed within its existing language from cases where an extension of the language is required.

Conclusion: A finite sheaf-theoretic, obstruction-based framework can serve as a diagnostic tool for AI agents to decide when representational transport fails and a theory extension is the coherent next move. While not addressing full autonomous theory invention or historical paradigm shifts, the work isolates a tractable subproblem—detecting theory-shift candidates via transport and obstruction—in a way that appears empirically viable on a controlled benchmark.

Abstract: Scientific theory shift in AI agents requires more than fitting equations to data. An artificial scientific agent must detect whether an existing representational framework remains transportable into a new regime, or whether its language has become locally-to-globally obstructed and must be extended. This paper develops a finite sheaf-theoretic framework for detecting theory-shift candidates through transport and obstruction. Contexts are organized as a local-to-global structure in which source, overlap, target, and validation charts are fitted, restricted, and tested for gluing. Obstruction measures failure of coherence through residual fit, overlap incompatibility, constraint violation, limiting-relation failure, and representational cost. We evaluate the framework on a controlled transition-card benchmark designed to separate deformation within a source language from extension of that language. The main result is direct obstruction ranking: the intended deformation or extension is usually the lowest-obstruction candidate, and transition type is separated in the benchmark. A constellation kernel over the same signatures is included only as a secondary representational-similarity probe. The aim is not to reconstruct historical paradigm shifts or solve open-ended autonomous theory invention, but to isolate a finite diagnostic subproblem for AI agents: detecting when representational transport fails and extension becomes the coherent next move.

### [65] [Enhanced and Efficient Reasoning in Large Learning Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14036)
*Leslie G. Valiant*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose an efficient, principled reasoning layer for large language models by recoding inputs into a unary relational representation, enabling polynomial-time learning of key relational rules and more trustworthy reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current large language models are good at fluent language generation but lack a principled, computationally efficient basis for trusting the factual and logical content they produce. It is commonly assumed that adding genuinely principled reasoning on top of LLMs is too computationally expensive, leaving a gap between surface fluency and reliable reasoning. The paper is motivated by closing this gap: enabling sounder, logically grounded reasoning in LLMs without sacrificing practicality or existing ML infrastructure.

Method: They introduce a two-stage method. First, a preprocessing step recodes input data into a Unary Relational Integracode, a representation that makes explicit the relationships among entities and consolidates their multiple properties. Second, a standard (potentially streamlined) machine learning process is applied to this recoded data so that the model learns to predict both text and explicit relational structure. This recoding can be interpreted as instantiating a world model, and is compatible with existing software and hardware. The approach is framed using Robust Logic, which supports principled chaining over learned, uncertain information.

Result: They show theoretically that this unary relational recoding has a key property: it makes the problem of learning a core subset of relational rules that hold in the training-data world polynomial-time learnable, with runtime depending polynomially on the complexity of the rule. This indicates that important relational structure can be learned efficiently under this representation. The framework supports sound reasoning both within a single model invocation and across multiple calls to the classifier.

Conclusion: By introducing Unary Relational Integracode and combining it with standard machine learning, the paper provides a principled yet computationally feasible way to equip large language models with more reliable, logically grounded reasoning capabilities. The recoding yields an explicit world-model-like structure and enables polynomial-time learnability of key relational rules, supporting robust logical chaining over uncertain, learned knowledge while largely reusing existing ML infrastructure.

Abstract: In current Large Language Models we can trust the production of smoothly flowing prose on the basis of the principles of machine learning. However, there is no comparably principled basis to justify trust in the content of the text produced. It appears to be conventional wisdom that addressing this issue by adding more principled reasoning is not computationally affordable.
  Here we propose a principled method of reasoning that is efficient enough to be practical for large language models. Further, the method allows the retention of much of the currently used software and hardware base. Our method for improving the functioning of large language models consists of a first stage of preprocessing that recodes the data to a Unary Relational Integracode that is more explicit about the relationships among the objects described in the text, followed as a second stage by a standard but possibly streamlined machine learning process that then also learns to predict these relationships.
  The method may be viewed as realizing a world model and applying beyond natural language, to vision and actions, for example, where the multiple properties of an object referred to in an input are brought together explicitly, rather than remaining distributed in the various references to it in the input. We articulate its advantages in terms of Robust Logic, a system for performing principled chaining on learned, and hence uncertain, information. We show that this recoding has the surprising and fortuitous property that, while succinct, it makes the task of learning a core subset of relational rules that hold in the world described in the training data polynomial time learnable in a defined sense, the polynomial depending on the complexity of the rule. This gives support for sound reasoning within each single call of the learned classifier as well as between multiple calls.

### [66] [Model-Adaptive Tool Necessity Reveals the Knowing-Doing Gap in LLM Tool Use](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14038)
*Yize Cheng,Chenrui Fan,Mahdi JafariRaviz,Keivan Rezaei,Soheil Feiz*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies when LLM agents should call tools, defining tool necessity relative to each model’s true capabilities and showing large mismatches between when tools are needed and when they’re actually called.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work treats the need for tools as a generic, model-independent property, and typically only handles simple, obvious cases. But in practice, different models have different strengths, so whether a tool is needed depends on the specific model. The authors want a principled, empirical way to define and measure this, and to understand why models often fail to call tools even when they should.

Method: They propose a model-adaptive definition of tool necessity based on each model’s empirical performance on tasks. Using this, they analyze four LLMs on arithmetic and factual QA tasks, comparing when tools are empirically necessary versus when models actually call tools. They conceptually split tool use into an internal cognition stage (model’s belief that a tool is needed) and an execution stage (issuing the tool call). Using linear probes on hidden states, they test how well these two stages can be decoded and how their representations evolve across layers and tokens, and they trace example trajectories through the two-stage process.

Result: They find large mismatches between empirically defined tool necessity and actual tool-calling behavior (26.5–54.0% on arithmetic, 30.8–41.8% on factual QA). Linear probes can often decode both the model’s internal assessment of tool necessity and its tool-call action from hidden states, but the probe directions for cognition vs. action become nearly orthogonal in later layers at the last token. Most of the mismatch occurs in the transition from cognition (recognizing the need for tools) to action (actually calling them), not in the recognition itself.

Conclusion: LLMs show a pronounced “knowing–doing gap” in tool use: they often internally recognize when tools are needed but fail to translate that recognition into tool-calling actions. Improving tool-use reliability will require mechanisms that better align internal assessments with external actions, not just better detection of tool necessity.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly act as autonomous agents that must decide when to answer directly vs. when to invoke external tools. Prior work studying adaptive tool use has largely treated tool necessity as a model-agnostic property, annotated by human or LLM judge, and mostly cover cases where the answer is obvious (e.g., fetching the weather vs. paraphrasing text). However, tool necessity in the wild is more nuanced due to the divergence of capability boundaries across models: a problem solvable by a strong model on its own may still require tools for a weaker one. In this work, we introduce a model-adaptive definition of tool-necessity, grounded in each model's empirical performance. Following this definition, we compare the necessity against observed tool-call behavior across four models on arithmetic and factual QA dataset, and find substantial mismatches of 26.5-54.0% and 30.8-41.8%, respectively. To diagnose the failure, we decompose tool use into two stages: an internal cognition stage that reflects whether a model believes a tool is necessary, and an execution stage that determines whether the model actually makes a tool-call action. By probing the LLM hidden states, we find that both signals are often linearly decodable, yet their probe directions become nearly orthogonal in the late-layer, last-token regime that drives the next-token action. By tracing the trajectory of samples in the two-stage process, we further discover that the majority of mismatch is concentrated in the cognition-to-action transition, not in cognition itself. These results reveal a knowing-doing gap in LLM tool-use: improving tool-use reliability requires not only better recognition of when tools are needed, but also better translation of that recognition into action.

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes NERVE, a network-aware masked autoencoder framework that tokenizes functional connectivity matrices into intra- and inter-network blocks and embeds them with a bilinear factorization to learn better self-supervised representations for behavior and psychopathology prediction across cohorts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing masked autoencoder and graph-based self-supervised methods for resting-state functional connectivity treat connectivity elements homogeneously, ignoring the brain’s modular large-scale network organization. There is an open question of how to tokenize FC matrices in a way that aligns with functional brain networks while remaining computationally efficient and stable across cohorts.

Method: NERVE partitions functional connectivity (FC) matrices into patches corresponding to intra- and inter-network connectivity blocks defined over large-scale brain networks. Because these patches are heterogeneous in size and functional role, the authors design a novel structured bilinear factorization to embed each network-pair block, preserving network identity and reducing parameter scaling from quadratic to linear in the number of networks. This is wrapped in a masked autoencoder self-supervised framework and evaluated on three developmental cohorts for downstream prediction tasks, with ablations on parcellation and embedding choices.

Result: Across three large-scale developmental cohorts (ABCD, PNC, CCNP), NERVE outperforms region-centric, structurally agnostic MAE variants and graph-based self-supervised baselines on behavior and psychopathology prediction, especially in cross-cohort generalization. The learned representations are more stable and transferable, and ablations show that both the bilinear network embedding and anatomically grounded parcellation substantially contribute to performance gains.

Conclusion: Incorporating the brain’s large-scale network organization as an explicit structural prior into self-supervised learning for functional connectomics leads to more effective and generalizable representations. Network-aware tokenization of FC and bilinear embedding over network pairs are key design choices, suggesting future self-supervised methods should leverage domain-specific structure rather than treating connectivity data as homogeneous graphs or images.

Abstract: Masked autoencoders (MAEs) have recently shown promise for self-supervised representation learning of resting-state brain functional connectivity (FC). However, a fundamental question remains unresolved: how should FC matrices be tokenized to align with the intrinsic modular organization of large-scale brain networks? Existing approaches typically adopt region-centric or graph-based schemes that treat FC as structurally homogeneous elements and overlook the large-scale network brain organization. We introduce NERVE (Network-Aware Representations of Brain Functional Connectivity via Bilinear Tokenization), a self-supervised learning framework that redefines FC tokenization by partitioning FC matrices into patches of intra- and inter-network connectivity blocks. Unlike image-based MAE, where fixed-size patches share a common tokenizer, FC patches defined by network pairs are heterogeneous in size and correspond to distinct functional roles. To resolve this problem, NERVE embeds FC patches through a novel structured bilinear factorization. This formulation preserves network identity and reduces parameter complexity from quadratic to linear scaling in the number of networks. We evaluate NERVE across three large-scale developmental cohorts (ABCD, PNC, and CCNP) for behavior and psychopathology prediction. Compared to structurally agnostic MAE variants and graph-based self-supervised baselines, the proposed network-aware formulation yields more stable and transferable representations, particularly in cross-cohort evaluation. Ablation studies confirm that the proposed bilinear network embedding and anatomically grounded parcellation are critical for performance. These findings highlight the importance of incorporating domain-specific structural priors into self-supervised learning for functional connectomics.

### [69] [SPIN: Structural LLM Planning via Iterative Navigation for Industrial Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14051)
*Yusuke Ozaki,Dhaval Patel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SPIN is a planning wrapper for LLM agents that validates DAG-structured plans and executes them prefix-wise to cut unnecessary steps and tool calls while improving task success.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current industrial LLM agent systems separate planning from execution, but LLM-generated plans are often structurally invalid, overly long, or brittle. This causes failures in downstream execution and increases costs due to unnecessary tool/API calls. The authors want a way to make planning both structurally sound and more efficient without sacrificing performance.

Method: They introduce SPIN, a wrapper around existing LLM planners. SPIN (1) enforces a strict Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG) contract on the plan using a validation function and repair prompting, so only executable, well-structured plans are passed to execution; and (2) controls execution via prefix-based evaluation of the DAG, incrementally executing prefixes and stopping early once the current partial plan is sufficient to answer the query.

Result: On AssetOpsBench with 261 scenarios, SPIN cuts executed tasks from 1061 to 623, increases the Accomplished metric from 0.638 to 0.706, and reduces tool calls per run from 11.81 to 6.82. On MCP Bench, it improves planning, grounding, and dependency-related metrics for both GPT OSS1 and Llama 4 Maverick models, showing benefits across models and benchmarks.

Conclusion: A planning wrapper that validates DAG-structured plans and uses prefix-based execution can substantially reduce unnecessary actions and tool use while improving or maintaining task completion quality. SPIN appears to be an effective, model-agnostic way to make industrial LLM agent systems more robust and cost-efficient.

Abstract: Industrial LLM agent systems often separate planning from execution, yet LLM planners frequently produce structurally invalid or unnecessarily long workflows, leading to brittle failures and avoidable tool and API cost. We propose \texttt{SPIN}, a planning wrapper that combines validated Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG) planning with prefix based execution control. \texttt{SPIN} enforces a strict DAG contract through \texttt{\_validate\_plan\_text} and repair prompting, producing executable plans before downstream execution, and then evaluates DAG prefixes incrementally to stop when the current prefix is sufficient to answer the query. On AssetOpsBench, across 261 scenarios, \texttt{SPIN} reduces executed tasks from 1061 to 623 and improves \emph{Accomplished} from 0.638 to 0.706, while reducing tool calls from 11.81 to 6.82 per run. On MCP Bench, the same wrapper improves planning, grounding, and dependency related scores for both GPT OSS1 and Llama 4 Maverick.

*Haozhe Wang,Qixin Xu,Changpeng Wang,Taofeng Xue,Chong Peng,Wenhu Chen,Fangzhen Lin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose MoCA, a reinforcement-learning framework that improves synergy between visual perception and reasoning in VLMs by assigning rewards separately to perception and reasoning components.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing VLMs struggle to balance perception accuracy and reasoning capability; improving one often hurts the other, partly due to ambiguous error attribution between 'seeing' and 'thinking' in training. Current solutions either rely on static textual reasoning or heavy agentic systems with high compute and engineering cost but limited payoff.

Method: They decompose VLM generation into interleaved perception and reasoning steps, then use reinforcement learning with modality-aware credit assignment. A key component, Perception Verification (PV), uses a 'blindfolded reasoning' proxy to evaluate and reward perception quality independently of reasoning. To scale across varied vision-language tasks, they introduce Structured Verbal Verification, where task outcomes are checked through structured, algorithmic evaluation instead of subjective LLM judging. All components are combined into the MoCA mechanism that routes reward to the appropriate module (perception vs reasoning).

Result: A single VLM trained with MoCA achieves concurrent improvements in both perception and reasoning across a wide range of vision-language tasks, overcoming the usual trade-off where one improves at the cost of the other.

Conclusion: Clarifying modality credit assignment is key to robust perception-reasoning synergy in VLMs. By decoupling and separately rewarding perception and reasoning through PV, Structured Verbal Verification, and the MoCA mechanism, they can train VLMs that see and think better simultaneously, instead of trading one off against the other.

Abstract: Achieving robust perception-reasoning synergy is a central goal for advanced Vision-Language Models (VLMs). Recent advancements have pursued this goal via architectural designs or agentic workflows. However, these approaches are often limited by static textual reasoning or complicated by the significant compute and engineering burden of external agentic complexity. Worse, this heavy investment does not yield proportional gains, often witnessing a "seesaw effect" on perception and reasoning. This motivates a fundamental rethinking of the true bottleneck. In this paper, we argue that the root cause of this trade-off is an ambiguity in modality credit assignment: when a VLM fails, is it due to flawed perception ("bad seeing") or flawed logic ("bad thinking")? To resolve this, we introduce a reinforcement learning framework that improves perception-reasoning synergy by reliably rewarding the perception fidelity. We explicitly decompose the generation process into interleaved perception and reasoning steps. This decoupling enables targeted supervision on perception. Crucially, we introduce Perception Verification (PV), leveraging a "blindfolded reasoning" proxy to reward perceptual fidelity independently of reasoning outcomes. Furthermore, to scale training across free-form VL tasks, we propose Structured Verbal Verification, which replaces high-variance LLM judging with structured algorithmic execution. These techniques are integrated into a Modality-Aware Credit Assignment (MoCA) mechanism, which routes rewards to the specific source of error -- either bad seeing or bad thinking -- enabling a single VLM to achieve simultaneous performance gains across a wide task spectrum.

### [71] [MathAtlas: A Benchmark for Autoformalization in the Wild](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14061)
*Nilay Patel,Noah Arias,Davit Babayan,Victoria Cochran,Timothy Libman,Hafsah Mahmood,Liam McCarty,Soli Munoz,Laurel Willey,Jeffrey Flanigan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: MathAtlas is a large, graduate-level autoformalization benchmark from real textbooks, with rich dependency graphs, on which current models perform very poorly.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing autoformalization benchmarks mostly cover olympiad and undergraduate-level problems, which do not represent the complexity, style, and dependency structure of real graduate and research mathematics. This gap makes it hard to evaluate and drive progress on systems intended for serious mathematical work. The authors aim to provide a realistic, large-scale benchmark drawn from actual graduate textbooks, with explicit logical/mathematical dependency information, to better measure and foster autoformalization capabilities at the frontier level.

Method: The authors construct MathAtlas by extracting about 52,000 mathematical entities (theorems, definitions, exercises, examples, proofs) from 103 graduate-level mathematics textbooks. They additionally build a large dependency graph over these entities, comprising around 178,000 relations, encoding which results or concepts depend on which others. They then run extensive experiments with strong baseline and state-of-the-art models, evaluating their ability to autoformalize natural-language mathematical statements and definitions into formal language, and analyze performance as a function of dependency depth, including on a specially selected hard subset (MA-Hard) with the deepest dependency trees.

Result: MathAtlas is shown to be high-quality but very difficult: strong baseline models achieve only up to 9.8% correctness when formalizing theorem statements and 16.7% on definitions. When focusing on entities with deep dependency chains (the MA-Hard subset of 700 items), the best model’s correctness drops further to 2.6%. These results empirically demonstrate that contemporary autoformalization systems struggle badly on realistic graduate-level content, especially when long dependency structures are involved.

Conclusion: MathAtlas provides the first large-scale, graduate-level, in-the-wild autoformalization benchmark with an explicit dependency graph, revealing that current models are far from solving realistic autoformalization tasks. The benchmark is released to the community to spur research on dependency-aware, more capable autoformalization systems for advanced mathematics.

Abstract: Current autoformalization benchmarks are largely focused on olympiad or undergraduate mathematics, while graduate and research-level mathematics remains underexplored. In this paper, we introduce MathAtlas, the first large-scale autoformalization benchmark of in the wild graduate-level mathematics, containing ~52k theorems, definitions, exercises, examples, and proofs extracted from 103 graduate mathematics textbooks. MathAtlas is enriched with a mathematical dependency graph containing ~178k relations, and is the first autoformalization benchmark to include such relations, facilitating evaluation and development of dependency-aware autoformalization systems. Our extensive experiments show that MathAtlas is high quality but extremely challenging: strong baselines achieve at most 9.8% correctness on theorem statements and 16.7% on definitions. Furthermore, we find performance of state-of-the-art models degrades substantially with dependency depth: on MA-Hard, a subset of 700 entities with the deepest dependency trees, the best model achieves only 2.6% correctness for autoformalization on this challenging dataset. We release MathAtlas to the community as a benchmark set for large-scale autoformalization of graduate-level mathematics in the wild.

### [72] [Know When To Fold 'Em: Token-Efficient LLM Synthetic Data Generation via Multi-Stage In-Flight Rejection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14062)
*Anjir Ahmed Chowdhury,Syed Zawad,Feng Yan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes MSIFR, a framework that stops low-quality LLM generations early to save tokens while maintaining accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing synthetic data generation with LLMs wastes many tokens because full outputs are generated and then discarded by quality filters. The authors want to reduce this token waste and improve efficiency without retraining models.

Method: They introduce Multi-Stage In-Flight Rejection (MSIFR), which breaks generation into sequential stages and applies fast rule-based validators at intermediate checkpoints to detect arithmetic errors, hallucinations, and formatting issues. Generation trajectories predicted to be low-quality are terminated early. They formalize this as a sequential decision process, analyze expected token savings, and prove that conditional utility estimates form a martingale so that early rejection does not bias the utility of retained samples.

Result: On five instruction-tuned models and seven reasoning benchmarks, MSIFR cuts token consumption by 11–77% alone and up to 78.2% when paired with early-exit techniques, while keeping or improving accuracy.

Conclusion: MSIFR is an effective, training-free way to make LLM-based synthetic data generation more efficient, achieving large token savings without sacrificing and sometimes improving performance.

Abstract: While synthetic data generation with large language models (LLMs) is widely used in post-training pipelines, existing approaches typically generate full outputs before applying quality filters, leading to substantial token waste on samples that are ultimately discarded. To address this, we propose Multi-Stage In-Flight Rejection (MSIFR), a lightweight, training-free framework that detects and terminates low-quality generation trajectories at intermediate checkpoints before they reach full completion. MSIFR decomposes the generation process into sequential stages and applies fast rule-based validators to identify arithmetic inconsistencies, hallucination patterns, and formatting violations, enabling early rejection of faulty samples. We formalize in-flight rejection as a sequential decision process and show that any non-trivial discard policy reduces expected token consumption, with stage-wise savings increasing when rejection occurs earlier in the generation pipeline. We further demonstrate that conditional utility estimates form a martingale, ensuring that early, in-flight rejection does not bias the expected utility of retained samples. Across five instruction-tuned models and seven reasoning benchmarks, MSIFR reduces token consumption by 11%-77% as a standalone method, and up to 78.2% when combined with early-exit methods, while preserving or improving evaluation accuracy. These results confirm that MSIFR provides a practical mechanism for improving the efficiency of LLM-based synthetic data generation without additional training or architectural changes.

### [73] [SkillFlow: Flow-Driven Recursive Skill Evolution for Agentic Orchestration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14089)
*Mingda Zhang,Tiesunlong Shen,Haoran Luo,Wenjin Liu,Zikai Xiao,Erik Cambria,Xiaoying Tang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SkillFlow is a flow-based framework for orchestrating LLM-powered skills that avoids strategy collapse, reduces gradient variance, and enables principled skill evolution using a trainable Supervisor and Tempered Trajectory Balance (TTB).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based agentic task orchestration methods suffer from strategy collapse when optimizing for rewards, high gradient variance due to unclear credit assignment, and ad-hoc, unguided skill evolution that relies on direct LLM prompting rather than principled training signals. There is a need for a more stable, interpretable, and trainable orchestration framework that can autonomously grow capabilities.

Method: SkillFlow introduces a structured environment with three main components: a trainable Supervisor agent, a dynamic skill library, and frozen executors. Task orchestration is framed as multi-turn interaction between the Supervisor and the environment. It uses Tempered Trajectory Balance (TTB), a regression-based flow-matching loss, to sample trajectories in proportion to their rewards. This maintains diversity in orchestration strategies and jointly learns a backward policy for step-wise credit assignment without extra inference cost. On top of this, a recursive skill evolution mechanism decides when to create, modify, or prune skills by analyzing flow diagnostics and decision gaps.

Result: Across 14 datasets spanning question answering, mathematical reasoning, code generation, and real-world interactive decision-making, SkillFlow achieves significantly better performance than baseline orchestration methods. It demonstrates more robust and diverse strategy learning, improved credit assignment, and effective automatic skill evolution.

Conclusion: SkillFlow provides a principled, flow-based solution for LLM agent task orchestration that addresses strategy collapse, gradient variance, and unguided skill evolution. Through Tempered Trajectory Balance and recursive skill evolution, it enables stable training, transparent credit assignment, and autonomous growth of a structured skill library, leading to superior performance over baselines on a wide range of tasks.

Abstract: In recent years, a variety of powerful LLM-based agentic systems have been applied to automate complex tasks through task orchestration. However, existing orchestration methods still face key challenges, including strategy collapse under reward maximization, high gradient variance with opaque credit assignment, and unguided skill evolution whose decisions are typically made by directly prompting an LLM to judge rather than derived from principled training signals. To address these challenges, we propose SkillFlow, a flow-based framework that takes a trainable Supervisor as the agent and a structured environment with dynamic skill library and frozen executor, automating task orchestration through multi-turn interaction. SkillFlow employs Tempered Trajectory Balance (TTB), a regression-based flow-matching loss that samples trajectories proportional to reward, preserving diverse orchestration strategies rather than collapsing to a single mode. The same flow objective yields a jointly learned backward policy that provides transparent per-step credit assignment at zero additional inference cost. Building on these flow diagnostics, a recursive skill evolution mechanism determines when to evolve, what skills to create or prune, and where decision gaps lie -- closing the loop from training signal to autonomous capability growth. Experimental results on 14 datasets show that SkillFlow significantly outperforms baselines across question answering, mathematical reasoning, code generation, and real-world interactive decision making tasks. Our code is available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/SkillFlow-E850.

Two randomized 20-task smoke evaluations produced 12/20 and 11/20 correct answers, showing that small diagnostic gains can be unstable across samples. The central result is therefore a negative ablation: more aggressive orchestration did not improve full-set performance and increased operational noise. Across simulated scenarios spanning short to long horizons, we show that attention-guided planning supports stable decision-making without complete state reasoning. The framework compiles scenario templates, grounded slots, initialized state, reference trajectories, and validators into reproducible task specifications, and evaluates agents step by step over persistent workflow surfaces using normalized end state and observable side effects rather than exact trajectory matching. ### [77] [Distribution-Aware Algorithm Design with LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14141)
*Saharsh Koganti,Priyadarsi Mishra,Pierfrancesco Beneventano,Tomer Galanti*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose learning executable solver code that is both correct and fast on a task distribution, not just accurate like a predictor, and show both theoretical guarantees and strong empirical results using LLM code agents on combinatorial optimization problems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional learning focuses on predictors that map inputs to outputs and are evaluated mainly on accuracy or solution quality. In many combinatorial-optimization and algorithmic settings, however, we care about both correctness/quality and runtime, and the object we want to learn is effectively an algorithm or solver. Different solvers can all return valid solutions but have dramatically different running times. The authors aim to formalize and study how to learn solver code that generalizes not only in correctness but also in execution time over an unknown distribution of tasks.

Method: They introduce the notion of a ‘solver hint’, a reusable structural insight about a task distribution that can be inferred from samples of problem instances and then compiled into specialized solver code. Formally, they consider a fixed library of solvers and show that picking the empirically fastest solver that fits the training samples leads to generalization in both correctness and runtime. They also provide identifiability and sample-complexity results, proving that statistically identifiable hints can be reconstructed and compiled from a polynomial number of samples. Empirically, they instantiate this framework with LLM-based code-generation agents that synthesize specialized solvers for 21 target distributions across seven classes of structured combinatorial optimization problems, drawing from heuristic pools, exact solvers, and time-limited backends.

Result: Theoretically, they prove that choosing the empirically fastest sample-consistent solver from a fixed library generalizes in both solution validity and runtime, and that solver hints are recoverable from polynomially many samples when they are statistically identifiable. Empirically, the synthesized solvers achieve mean normalized solution quality of 0.971. They outperform the average heuristic pool by +0.224 and the best-mean-quality heuristic by +0.098, while being 336.9× faster than the quality-best heuristic, 342.8× faster than Gurobi, and 16.1× faster than a selected time-limited exact backend. On private PACE 2025 Dominating Set instances, their synthesized solver solves all 100 graphs, runs roughly two orders of magnitude faster than top competition solvers, and incurs only a moderate gap in solution quality.

Conclusion: Learning specialized solver code from task distributions is feasible and beneficial: one can automatically synthesize solvers that generalize in correctness and runtime, capture distribution-specific structural hints, and substantially outperform generic heuristics and standard exact solvers in speed while maintaining high solution quality. Much of the benefit comes from shifting computational effort from expensive generic search or optimization into precompiled, distribution-tailored computation guided by learned solver hints.

Abstract: We study learning when the learned object is executable solver code rather than a predictor. In this setting, correctness is not enough: two solvers may both return valid solutions on the deployment distribution while differing substantially in runtime. Given samples from an unknown task distribution, the learner returns code evaluated on fresh instances by both solution quality and execution time. Our central abstraction is a \emph{solver hint}: reusable structure inferred from samples and compiled into specialized solver code. We prove that the empirically fastest sample-consistent solver from a fixed library generalizes in both correctness and runtime, and that statistically identifiable hints can be recovered and compiled from polynomially many samples.
  Empirically, we instantiate the framework with LLM code agents on \(21\) structured combinatorial-optimization target distributions across seven problem classes. The synthesized solvers reach mean normalized quality \(0.971\), improve by \(+0.224\) over the average heuristic pool and by \(+0.098\) over the highest-quality heuristic, and are \(336.9\times\), \(342.8\times\), and \(16.1\times\) faster than the quality-best heuristic, Gurobi, and the selected time-limited exact backend, respectively. On released PACE 2025 Dominating Set private instances, the synthesized solver is valid on all \(100\) graphs and runs about two orders of magnitude faster than top competition solvers, with a moderate quality gap. Inspection shows that many gains come from changing the computational scale: replacing ambient exponential search or general-purpose optimization with compiled distribution-specific computation.

### [78] [Agentic Systems as Boosting Weak Reasoning Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14163)
*Varun Sunkaraneni,Pierfrancesco Beneventano,Riccardo Neumarker,Tomaso Poggio,Tomer Galanti*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to boost weak reasoning language models at inference time using committees of model calls plus a hidden verifier, showing that carefully orchestrated proposal and critique steps can match much stronger standalone models, with theory explaining when and why this works.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reasoning-focused language models are expensive, and simply calling a stronger model is often impractical. The authors want to know whether orchestrating many calls to a cheaper, weaker model—using a verifier to guide search—can close the performance gap to much larger models, and under what theoretical conditions this is possible.

Method: They formalize verifier-backed committee search by decomposing it into proposal coverage (how often good answers are generated), local identifiability (whether local signals can distinguish better from worse proposals), progress (how selection errors compose over multiple steps), and diversity (whether multiple samples explore different solution modes). They prove bounds on coverage amplification through sampling and on how reliable trajectories emerge when local selection—via critics and comparators using soundness signals like tests or proof checking—is imperfect. Empirically, they test on SWE-bench Verified using a weak model (GPT-5.4 nano) to generate k candidate patches and then apply critic–comparator orchestration to select among them, comparing to oracle best-of-k and to stronger models (Gemini 3 Pro, Claude Opus 4.5 Thinking).

Result: Theoretically, they show that repeated sampling increases coverage but cannot compensate for missing local soundness; critics and comparators need additional reliable signals (e.g., execution, tests) to effectively amplify performance. They give rank-based bounds on when local selection errors still allow progress and clarify that oracle best-of-k is upper-bounded by the useful probability mass the proposer assigns. Empirically, on SWE-bench Verified, a single GPT-5.4 nano proposal solves 67.0% of tasks, while their orchestrated critic–comparator scheme with k=8 proposals reaches 76.4%, essentially matching Gemini 3 Pro and Claude Opus 4.5 Thinking and approaching the 79.0% oracle best-of-8 upper bound.

Conclusion: Inference-time boosting via verifier-backed committee search can substantially close the gap between weak and strong reasoning LMs when many correct solutions already exist in the weak model’s proposal distribution and when a reliable local soundness signal is available. The main bottleneck then becomes proposal coverage (shared blind spots) rather than selection quality, implying that further gains require improving what weak models can propose, not just how we pick among their outputs.

Abstract: Can a committee of weak reasoning-model calls reach the performance of much stronger models? We study verifier-backed committee search as inference-time boosting for reasoning language models. The mechanism is not simply that ``more agents help'': samples expose latent correct solutions, while critics and comparators must recover them without access to the hidden verifier. We formalize this view by separating proposal coverage, local identifiability, progress, and diversity. We prove that coverage can be amplified by repeated sampling, but cannot by itself create useful critics or comparators; reliable amplification requires an additional local soundness signal, such as execution, proof checking, type checking, tests, or constraint solving. We give rank-based bounds showing when local selection errors compose into reliable trajectories, and characterize the proposer-side ceiling: oracle best-of-\(k\) converges only to the mass of task slices on which the proposal system assigns nonzero useful probability. Empirically, on SWE-bench Verified, a single \texttt{GPT-5.4 nano} proposal solves \(67.0\%\) of tasks. Using the same nano model, our critic--comparator orchestration reaches \(76.4\%\) with \(k=8\) proposals, matching the standalone performance of \texttt{Gemini 3 Pro} and \texttt{Claude Opus 4.5} Thinking and approaching the \(79.0\%\) oracle best-of-\(8\) upper bound. Thus, many correct patches are already present in weak-model proposal pools; the main challenge is selecting them. The remaining failures are mostly proposal-coverage failures, indicating shared blind spots that stronger selection alone cannot close.
