### [80] [The Evaluation Trap: Benchmark Design as Theoretical Commitment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14167)
*Theodore J Kalaitzidis*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that AI benchmarks often reinforce existing assumptions instead of truly measuring capabilities, and introduces a methodology, Epistematics, to design and audit benchmarks so they genuinely test the capabilities they claim to assess.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI benchmarks embed hidden theoretical assumptions about what a capability is. Because these assumptions are rarely examined, benchmarks end up defining and constraining what counts as progress, reshaping capability concepts to match what is easy to measure. This can cause evaluations to stop tracking real-world or theoretically intended capabilities and instead measure self-referential proxies, trapping the field within the limits of the dominant paradigm. The authors want a principled way to surface and check these assumptions so that benchmarks do not merely reproduce the very constraints they aim to overcome.

Method: The authors propose a methodology called Epistematics. It works by (1) starting from explicit technical capability claims, (2) deriving evaluation criteria directly from these claims, and (3) auditing proposed benchmarks against these criteria to test whether they can distinguish the claimed capability from mere proxy behaviors. They provide a meta-evaluative framework including (a) an audit procedure, (b) a taxonomy of evaluation failure modes, and (c) design principles for constructing benchmarks whose evaluation criteria are coherent with the capabilities they are supposed to measure. They then apply this procedure to a concrete case study: a benchmark proposal by Dupoux et al. (2026).

Result: Using Epistematics on the Dupoux et al. (2026) proposal, the authors show that although the proposal changes the underlying architectural assumptions of the dominant paradigm, its evaluation criteria still embody the old assumptions. As a result, the benchmark cannot detect whether the new architecture actually escapes the old constraints; it only measures success in terms that re-inscribe those same constraints. This illustrates how benchmarks can silently reproduce the limitations they aim to fix and validates the usefulness of the Epistematics audit for revealing such incoherences.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that AI evaluation must be treated as a theoretical practice, not just an engineering or metrics problem. Benchmarks should be systematically derived from and checked against explicit capability claims to avoid self-reinforcing, paradigm-entrenching evaluations. Epistematics provides tools—an audit procedure, failure mode taxonomy, and design criteria—to assess and improve the coherence between capability claims and benchmarks. This, the authors argue, is necessary to prevent evaluation from becoming a trap that hides structural limits of the current AI paradigm and to enable more meaningful progress in AI capabilities.

Abstract: Every AI benchmark operationalizes theoretical assumptions about the capability it claims to assess. When assumptions function as unexamined commitments, benchmarks stabilize the dominant paradigm by narrowing what counts as progress. Over time, narrow evaluation reorganizes capability concepts: architectures and definitions are selected for benchmark legibility until evaluation ceases to track an independent object and instead produces a version of the target defined by its own operational assumptions. The result is a trap: evaluation frameworks treat self-reinforcing assessments as valid, both creating and obscuring structural limits on what the current paradigm can accomplish. We introduce Epistematics, a methodology for deriving evaluation criteria directly from technical capability claims and auditing whether proposed benchmarks can discriminate the claimed capability from proxy behaviors. The contribution is meta-evaluative: an audit procedure, a failure mode taxonomy, and benchmark-design criteria for evaluating capability-evaluation coherence. We demonstrate the procedure through a worked audit of Dupoux et al. (2026), a proposal that revises the dominant paradigm's theoretical assumptions at the architectural level while reproducing them in its evaluation criteria, thereby entrenching the constraint it seeks to overcome in a form the evaluation cannot detect.

### [81] [Grounded Continuation: A Linear-Time Runtime Verifier for LLM Conversations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14175)
*Qisong He,Yi Dong,Xiaowei Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a runtime verifier for long LLM conversations that tracks logical dependencies to avoid relying on stale or unsupported premises, achieving better accuracy and efficient retraction than LLM-only and RAG baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs in long conversations often produce plausible but incorrect statements based on outdated or abandoned premises, which can be exploited by context-manipulation attacks. There is a need for a principled, efficient way to ensure that each new utterance is still supported by the current conversational context.

Method: They build a runtime verifier that maintains an explicit dependency graph over conversational claims. At each turn, an LLM classifies the update as one of 8 operations inspired by four formal frameworks (dynamic epistemic logic, abductive reasoning, awareness logic, argumentation). A symbolic engine records dependencies between claims and evidence. Validating a continuation becomes a graph walk, and retractions propagate over the graph to mark exactly which conclusions lose support. The method guarantees conflict-freeness and has linear per-turn cost, with microsecond-level retraction checks.

Result: On the LongMemEval-KU oracle benchmark, the verifier achieves 89.7% accuracy, outperforming both an LLM-only baseline (88.5%) and a transcript-RAG baseline (87.2%), mainly by correctly abstaining where baselines confabulate. On LoCoMo's 60 QA items it is competitive with RAG methods. In custom multi-agent scenarios and a 50-item grounding test (especially a 15-item stale-premise subset), it reaches 100% accuracy vs. 93.3% for baselines. They also show that retraction check time remains constant while naive history replay scales linearly with conversation length.

Conclusion: A structured dependency-graph verifier can make LLM agents more robust in long conversations, preventing reliance on stale premises, offering formal soundness guarantees at the structural level, and providing a scalable, efficient alternative to full history replay or simple RAG approaches, with empirical validation across multiple LLM families and benchmarks.

Abstract: In long conversations, an LLM can produce a next utterance that sounds plausible but rests on premises the conversation has already abandoned. Context-manipulation attacks against deployed agents now actively exploit this gap. We close it with a runtime verifier that maintains an explicit dependency graph: an LLM classifies each turn into one of 8 update operations drawn from four formalisms (dynamic epistemic logic, abductive reasoning, awareness logic, argumentation), and a symbolic engine records which claims depend on which evidence. Checking whether a continuation is supported reduces to a graph walk; retraction propagates through the same graph to flag exactly the conclusions that lose support, with linear per-turn cost and a formal conflict-free guarantee. On LongMemEval-KU oracle (n=78), the verifier reaches 89.7% accuracy vs. 88.5% for the LLM-only baseline (+1.3pp) and 87.2% for a transcript-RAG baseline matched on retrieval budget (+2.6pp); wins among disagreements are correct abstentions where the baseline confabulates. On LoCoMo's 60 official QA items the verifier is competitive with retrieval-augmented baselines. Beyond external benchmarks, we construct two multi-agent scenarios and a 50-item grounding test: on the 15-item stale-premise subset, the verifier reaches 100% accuracy vs. 93.3% (+6.7pp). These instantiate a soundness-faithfulness decomposition: the structural check is sound by construction, and per-deployment LLM extraction faithfulness is the empirical question we measure across four LLM families. The retraction check plateaus at microseconds while history-replay grows linearly with conversation length.

</details>


### [82] [SimPersona: Learning Discrete Buyer Personas from Raw Clickstreams for Grounded E-Commerce Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14205)
*Zahra Zanjani Foumani,Alberto Castelo,Shuang Xie,Ted Chaiwachirasak,Han Li,Lingyun Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

  <summary>Details</summary>

Hand-crafted, prompt-based personas are brittle, unscalable, and cannot accurately model population-level distributions across different merchants. The authors want a systematic, data-driven way to capture and simulate diverse buyer types that reflect true behavioral statistics and can guide LLM agents effectively.

Method: They propose SimPersona, which takes raw e-commerce clickstreams and trains a behavior-aware vector-quantized VAE (VQ-VAE) to learn a discrete codebook of buyer types. The LLM agent is fine-tuned on real browsing traces where these persona tokens condition its behavior. At inference, a buyer’s clickstream (or minimal context) is passed through the encoder once to assign a buyer type, and the corresponding persona token is prepended to the agent’s input. For population-level simulations, buyer types are sampled from merchant-specific empirical distributions over the VQ-VAE codebook, and the appropriate persona tokens instantiate agents aligned with those distributions.

Result: On 8.37M buyers from 42 held-out live storefronts, SimPersona achieves 78% alignment between simulated and real conversion rates, shows interpretable differences in behavior across learned buyer types, and surpasses a much larger baseline (8× more parameters) on goal-oriented shopping tasks. The authors also provide an open-source data pipeline for transforming raw event logs into buyer representations and training data for these agents.

Conclusion: Data-driven, discrete buyer types learned via a behavior-aware VQ-VAE can be turned into compact persona tokens that effectively personalize LLM-based web agents. This approach scales, preserves merchant-specific population distributions, yields interpretable personas, and improves both behavioral fidelity and task performance compared to hand-crafted or larger non-personalized baselines, enabling more realistic and controllable population-level e-commerce simulations.

Abstract: LLM-based web agents can navigate live storefronts, yet they often collapse to a single "average buyer" policy, failing to capture the heterogeneous and distributional nature of real buyer populations. Existing personalization methods rely on hand-crafted prompt-based personas that are brittle, difficult to scale, context-inefficient, and unable to faithfully represent population-level behavior. We introduce SimPersona, a novel framework that learns discrete buyer types from historical traffic and exposes them to LLM-based web agents as compact persona tokens. Given raw clickstreams, a behavior-aware VQ-VAE induces a discrete buyer-type space that captures the statistical structure of real buyer behavior and merchant-specific buyer population distributions. To provide behavior-specific guidance to LLM-based web agents, SimPersona maps each learned buyer type to a dedicated persona token in the LLM agent vocabulary and fine-tunes the agent with these tokens on real browsing traces. At inference, each synthetic buyer is assigned to a learned buyer type with a single encoder forward pass, requiring no retraining or store-specific prompt engineering. For population-level simulation, SimPersona samples buyer types from each merchant's empirical distribution over the learned VQ-VAE codebook and instantiates agents with the corresponding persona tokens, preserving merchant-specific buyer population distributions. Evaluated on $8.37$M buyers across $42$ held-out live storefronts, SimPersona achieves $78\%$ conversion-rate alignment with real buyers, exhibits interpretable behavioral variation across buyer types, and outperforms a baseline with $8\times$ more parameters on goal-oriented shopping tasks. ### [83] [ASH: Agents that Self-Hone via Embodied Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14211)
*Benjamin Schneider,Xavier Schneider,Victor Zhong,Sun Sun*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ASH is a self-improving embodied agent that learns long-horizon game policies directly from unlabeled, noisy internet video, outperforming standard imitation and foundation-model baselines on multi-hour gameplay.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-horizon embodied tasks, like playing complex games for hours, are difficult because existing methods need either hand-crafted reward functions or high-quality action-labeled demonstrations, which are expensive and don’t scale. The authors want a scalable way to learn strong, long-horizon control policies from widely available but unlabeled, noisy internet videos, without manual reward shaping or expert annotation.

Method: They propose ASH, an agentic system that iteratively improves itself via a self-improvement loop. When the agent gets stuck in an environment, it learns an Inverse Dynamics Model (IDM) from its own trajectories. This IDM is then applied to relevant internet videos to infer the missing action labels, turning the videos into supervision data. ASH additionally uses unsupervised learning to detect key moments in large-scale internet video and stores these as long-term memory, which the agent can draw on to solve long-horizon tasks. The approach is evaluated on two multi-hour video game environments: Pokemon Emerald (turn-based RPG) and The Legend of Zelda: The Minish Cap (real-time action-adventure).

Result: ASH sustains task progression over an 8-hour evaluation in both games, whereas baselines such as behavioral cloning, retrieval-augmented methods, and zero-shot foundation-model agents plateau early. Quantitatively, ASH achieves on average 11.2 out of 12 predefined milestones in Pokemon Emerald and 9.9 out of 12 milestones in The Legend of Zelda: The Minish Cap. The strongest baseline agents get stuck at around 6.5/12 milestones in Pokemon and 6.0/12 in Zelda, indicating a large performance gap in favor of ASH for long-horizon gameplay.

Conclusion: The work shows that an agentic, self-improvement loop that leverages an internally learned inverse dynamics model to extract supervision from unlabeled internet videos is an effective and scalable path for long-horizon embodied learning. By combining self-collected trajectories, IDM-based action inference, and unsupervised key-moment discovery with long-term memory, ASH can reliably make progress on multi-hour tasks and significantly outperform existing imitation and foundation-model baselines. This suggests that self-improving agents using internet-scale data can overcome some of the scalability limitations of reward engineering and expert-labeled demonstrations.

Abstract: Long-horizon embodied tasks remain a fundamental challenge in AI, as current methods rely on hand-engineered rewards or action-labeled demonstrations, neither of which scales. We introduce ASH, an agentic system that learns an embodied policy from unlabeled, noisy internet video, without reward shaping or expert annotation. ASH follows a self-improvement loop; when it gets stuck, ASH learns an Inverse Dynamics Model (IDM) from its own trajectories, and uses its IDM to extract supervision from relevant internet video. ASH uses unsupervised learning to identify key moments from large-scale internet video and retains them as long-term memory -- allowing it to tackle long-horizon problems. We evaluate ASH on two complementary environments demanding multi-hour planning: Pokemon Emerald, a turn-based RPG, and The Legend of Zelda: The Minish Cap, a real-time action-adventure game. In both games, behavioral cloning, retrieval-augmented and zero-shot foundation-model baselines plateau, while ASH sustains progression across our 8-hour evaluation. ASH reaches an average of $11.2/12$ milestones in Pokemon Emerald and $9.9/12$ in Legend of Zelda, while the strongest baseline gets stuck in both environments at an average of $6.5/12$ and $6.0/12$ milestones, respectively. We demonstrate that self-improving agents are a scalable recipe for long-horizon embodied learning.

Method: They introduce MetaAgent-X, an end-to-end reinforcement learning framework for automatic MAS. It uses script-based MAS generation to specify agent workflows, collects execution rollouts, and assigns credit to both designer and executor trajectories. To train stably and scalably, they propose two techniques: Executor Designer Hierarchical Rollout (structuring rollouts to reflect the hierarchy between designer and executors) and Stagewise Co-evolution (a curriculum/phase-wise scheme that coordinates how designer and executor are updated over time).

Conclusion: End-to-end trainable automatic MAS, where both the workflow designer and execution agents are jointly optimized via RL, is a practical and effective paradigm. MetaAgent-X demonstrates that such systems can surpass prior approaches constrained by frozen executors, and that coordinated, stagewise co-evolution between designer and executor is key for effective learning.

Abstract: Automatic multi-agent systems aim to instantiate agent workflows without relying on manually designed or fixed orchestration. However, existing automatic MAS approaches remain only partially adaptive: they either perform training-free test-time search or optimize the meta-level designer while keeping downstream execution agents frozen, which creating a frozen-executor ceiling and leaving the end-to-end training of self-designing and self-executing agentic models unexplored. To address this, we introduce MetaAgent-X, an end-to-end reinforcement learning framework that jointly optimizes automatic MAS design and execution. MetaAgent-X enables script-based MAS generation, execution rollout collection, and credit assignment for both designer and executor trajectories. To support stable and scalable optimization, we propose Executor Designer Hierarchical Rollout and Stagewise Co-evolution to improve training stability and expose the dynamics of designer-executor co-evolution. MetaAgent-X consistently outperforms existing automatic MAS baselines, achieving up to 21.7% gains. Comprehensive ablations show that both designer and executor improve throughout training, and that effective automatic MAS learning follows a stagewise co-evolution process. These results establish end-to-end trainable automatic MAS as a practical paradigm for building self-designing and self-executing agentic models.

### [85] [GenCircuit-RL: Reinforcement Learning from Hierarchical Verification for Genetic Circuit Design](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14215)
*Noah Flynn*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They build an RL-based framework and a benchmark so models can generate and fix genetic circuit designs as executable SBOL code, using hierarchical rewards and curriculum learning to improve functional correctness and generalization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing ML/code-generation approaches struggle with producing both syntactically valid and functionally correct SBOL circuit designs, and they often lack fine-grained feedback and realistic benchmarks that test reasoning about circuit function and generalization to new biological parts. The authors want a way for models to automatically generate, repair, and reason about genetic circuit designs in a verifiable, machine-readable form.

Method: They frame genetic circuit design as code generation in Python using pysbol3, which outputs SBOL representations. They also create SynBio-Reason, a large benchmark of 4,753 circuits across six canonical circuit types and nine task types such as code repair, completion, and de novo design, including held-out biological parts to test out-of-distribution performance.

Result: Using SynBio-Reason, they show that hierarchical verification rewards significantly boost performance on functional reasoning tasks, giving 14–16 percentage point gains in task success versus simple binary reward schemes. Curriculum learning is necessary to achieve strong performance on design-oriented tasks. The trained models can reliably generate SBOL code that passes topological checks, handle circuits containing unseen biological parts, and successfully reconstruct known canonical circuit designs described in the literature.

Conclusion: Reinforcement learning with hierarchical verification and curriculum design is effective for training code-generation models to design genetic circuits in SBOL. The GenCircuit-RL framework and the SynBio-Reason benchmark together provide a path toward more automated, generalizable, and verifiable synthetic circuit design, enabling models that not only write syntactically correct code but also capture correct circuit topology and function, even for novel components.

Abstract: Genetic circuit design remains a laborious, expert-driven process despite decades of progress in synthetic biology. We study this problem through code generation: models produce Python code in pysbol3 to construct genetic circuits in the Synthetic Biology Open Language (SBOL), a formal representation that supports automated verification. We introduce GenCircuit-RL, a reinforcement learning framework built around hierarchical verification rewards that decompose correctness into five levels, from code execution to task-specific topological checks, and a four-stage curriculum that shifts optimization pressure from code generation to functional reasoning. We also introduce SynBio-Reason, a benchmark of 4,753 circuits spanning six canonical circuit types and nine tasks from code repair to de novo design, with held-out biological parts for out-of-distribution evaluation. Hierarchical verification improves task success on functional reasoning tasks by 14 to 16 percentage points over binary rewards, and curriculum learning is required for strong design performance. The resulting models generate topologically correct circuits, generalize to novel biological parts, and rediscover canonical designs from the synthetic biology literature.

### [86] [Fusion-fission forecasts when AI will shift to undesirable behavior](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14218)
*Neil F. Johnson,Frank Yingjie Huo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies and mathematically characterizes a general mechanism that can forecast when ChatGPT-like AI systems will shift from safe to unsafe behavior, offering a real-time warning signal that operates beneath existing safety mechanisms.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI chatbots can unpredictably switch from desirable to harmful behavior (e.g., promoting self-harm, extremism, or dangerous mistakes), and current alignment and safety methods cannot reliably predict such shifts in advance. There is a need for a model-agnostic, theoretically grounded way to anticipate behavioral shifts across different AI systems and applications.

Method: The authors model AI behavior using a vector generalization of fusion-fission group dynamics known from living and active-matter systems. They formalize a "shift condition" arising from group-level competition between three vector fields: the conversation-so-far (C), the desirable behavior basin (B), and the undesirable behavior basin (D). These basin dynamics are estimated in advance for specific applications. The condition is derived mathematically and then empirically tested across multiple AI models and deployed chatbots by checking whether it correctly forecasts behavioral shifts between desirable and undesirable outputs.

Result: The shift condition correctly predicts behavioral shifts with about 90% accuracy across seven AI models ranging from 124M to 12B parameters and shows production-scale persistence across ten frontier chatbots. The authors also demonstrate an a priori prediction, time-stamped eleven months before the release of Stanford's 'Delusional Spirals' corpus, whose 207,443 human-AI interactions independently confirm the predicted dynamics. The mechanism is shown to be architecture-agnostic and not dependent on stochastic sampling noise.

Conclusion: Behavioral shifts in ChatGPT-like systems are governed by a general, model-agnostic dynamical mechanism analogous to fusion-fission group dynamics. A mathematically derived shift condition, based on competition between the ongoing conversation and pre-characterized desirable and undesirable basins, can forecast when AI behavior will move into harmful regimes. Because this mechanism lies below current safety stacks, it can serve as a portable, real-time early warning signal across present and future AI architectures wherever competing response classes can be defined.

Abstract: The key problem facing ChatGPT-like AI's use across society is that its behavior can shift, unnoticed, from desirable to undesirable -- encouraging self-harm, extremist acts, financial losses, or costly medical and military mistakes -- and no one can yet predict when. Shifts persist in even the newest AI models despite remarkable progress in AI modeling, post-training alignment and safeguards. Here we show that a vector generalization of fusion-fission group dynamics observed in living and active-matter systems drives -- and can forecast -- future shifts in the AI's behavior. The shift condition, which is also derivable mathematically, results from group-level competition between the conversation-so-far (C) and the desirable (B) and undesirable (D) basin dynamics which can be estimated in advance for a given application. It is neither model-specific nor driven by stochastic sampling. We validate it across six independent tests, including: 90 percent correct across seven AI models spanning two orders of magnitude in parameter count (124M-12B); production-scale persistence across ten frontier chatbots; and a priori time-stamped prediction eleven months before the Stanford 'Delusional Spirals' corpus appeared, and independently confirmed by that corpus of 207,443 human-AI exchanges. Because it sits architecturally below the current safety stack, the same formula provides a real-time warning signal that current alignment does not supply, portable across current and future ChatGPT-like AI architectures and instantiable in application domains where competing response classes can be defined.

### [87] [Good to Go: The LOOP Skill Engine That Hits 99% Success and Slashes Token Usage by 99% via One-Shot Recording and Deterministic Replay](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14237)
*Xiaohua Wang,Kai Yu,XuXiao Liang,Liang Wang,Chao Han*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces the LOOP SKILL ENGINE, which records one full LLM-powered agent run for a periodic task, extracts a deterministic template, and then replays it without the LLM, achieving about 99% success and token savings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Periodic, repetitive AI agent tasks currently rely on LLMs each time they run, which is expensive, slow, and unreliable due to LLM stochasticity and non-determinism. The authors want a way to preserve the flexibility of LLM-based tool orchestration on the first run, but then execute future runs deterministically, cheaply, and reliably without repeatedly invoking the LLM.

Method: On the first execution of a periodic task, the system lets an LLM-driven agent operate normally, but intercepts and records the entire sequence of tool calls and parameters. A greedy length-descending template extraction algorithm then converts this concrete trajectory into a parameterized, branch-free "Loop Skill" template that captures the essential tool sequence while abstracting over time-varying and result-dependent values. On subsequent runs, the engine bypasses the LLM, fills in the template variables from current context, and replays the same tool-call sequence deterministically. The authors prove replay determinism and write-safety properties, and integrate multi-layer degradation (fallback) strategies so that, if something goes wrong, the system can still complete or gracefully degrade the task.

Result: On a benchmark of periodic agent tasks with intervals from 5 minutes to 24 hours, the Loop Skill Engine reduces monthly token usage by 93.3%–99.98%, cuts latency by 8.7x, and eliminates output non-determinism, while achieving an overall 99% success rate for these tasks. The system also ensures safe concurrent writes to persistent configuration via reentrant locks and atomic file replacement.

Conclusion: One-shot recording plus deterministic replay of LLM-generated tool trajectories is an effective way to run periodic AI agent tasks: it keeps the initial flexibility and intelligence of LLM orchestration but removes the cost, latency, and non-determinism for future runs. The LOOP SKILL ENGINE provides a practical implementation with strong empirical gains and theoretical guarantees, and is released as part of the open-source buddyMe framework.

Abstract: Deploying AI agents for repetitive periodic tasks exposes a critical tension: Large Language Models (LLMs) offer unmatched flexibility in tool orchestration, yet their inherent stochasticity causes unpredictable failures, and repeated invocations incur prohibitive token costs. We present the LOOP SKILL ENGINE, a system that achieves a combined 99% success rate and 99% token reduction for periodic agent tasks through a one-shot recording, deterministic replay paradigm. On its first run, the agent executes the task with full LLM reasoning while the system transparently intercepts and records the complete tool-call trajectory. A greedy length-descending template extraction algorithm then converts this recording into a parameterized, branch-free Loop Skill -- a deterministic execution plan that captures the task's functional intent while parameterizing time-dependent and result-dependent variables. All subsequent executions bypass the LLM entirely: the engine resolves template variables against real-time values and replays the tool sequence deterministically. We prove two theorems: (1) Replay Determinism -- the step sequence of a validated Loop Skill is invariant across all future executions; (2) Write Safety -- concurrent access to persistent configuration is serialized through reentrant locks and atomic file replacement. Across a benchmark of periodic agent tasks spanning intervals from 5 minutes to 24 hours, the Loop Skill Engine reduces monthly token consumption by 93.3%--99.98% and cuts execution latency by 8.7x while eliminating output non-determinism. A multi-layer degradation strategy guarantees that tasks never stall. We release the engine as part of the buddyMe open-source agent framework.

### [89] [Heuristic Pathologies and Further Variance Reduction via Uncertainty Propagation in the AIVAT Family of Techniques](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14261)
*Juho Kim,Tuomas Sandholm*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes and extends AIVAT variance-reduction estimators for multiagent evaluation, showing how heuristic value functions can be misused and how to incorporate their uncertainty for better, though sometimes biased, inference.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluating agents in multiagent environments is difficult when data is scarce or expensive. AIVAT offers unbiased, low-variance estimators using heuristic value functions, but there is little guidance on how to choose these heuristics or handle their uncertainty. This gap opens the door to pathological behavior and misuse (e.g., p-hacking) and limits the reliability of AIVAT-based evaluations.

Method: The authors (1) parameterize the heuristic value function within AIVAT and optimize it via gradient methods to study how it can artificially reduce sample variance or manipulate test statistics, revealing vulnerabilities; (2) develop a framework to propagate the heuristic’s uncertainty into AIVAT’s estimates, and apply inverse-variance weighting to combine information while analyzing the bias–variance tradeoff; and (3) empirically validate these ideas using a dataset of 10,000 poker hands.

Result: They show that directly optimizing the heuristic to minimize sample variance can make variance pathologically low and enable p-hacking on hypothesis tests, demonstrating that adaptive tuning on evaluation data is dangerous. They also show how to model and propagate uncertainty in the heuristic to obtain more informative confidence measures and, using inverse-variance weighted averaging, achieve substantial variance reductions in practice, albeit with potential loss of strict unbiasedness. On poker-hand data, incorporating heuristic uncertainty reduces the number of samples needed for statistical conclusions by about 43%.

Conclusion: Heuristic value functions in AIVAT must be fixed before observing evaluation data to avoid pathological variance suppression and p-hacking. Properly modeling and propagating heuristic uncertainty enables more accurate assessment of estimator uncertainty and can significantly reduce sample requirements, though maintaining strict unbiasedness may not always be possible. The work both cautions against misuse of AIVAT and proposes principled ways to exploit heuristics for more efficient multiagent evaluation.

Abstract: How should an agent's performance in a multiagent environment be evaluated when there is a limited sample size or a high cost of running a trial? The AIVAT family of variance reduction techniques was proposed to address this challenge by introducing unbiased low-variance estimators of agents' expected payoffs. An important component of AIVAT is a heuristic value function that discriminates between potentially low- and high-value counterfactual histories. A notable gap in the literature is that there is little to no constraint or guideline on how the heuristic value function should be chosen or how uncertainty in its output should be handled.
  In our first contribution, we parameterize the heuristic value function to highlight AIVAT's potential vulnerabilities: a) the sample variance can be set pathologically low by directly applying gradient descent on the sample variance, and b) one can p-hack to draw a desired statistical conclusion via gradient descent/ascent on the test statistic. The main takeaway is that the heuristic value function should be fixed prior to observing the evaluation data! In our second contribution, we show how the heuristic uncertainty can be propagated to quantify the uncertainty of AIVAT estimates. It is then possible to further reduce the variance using inverse-variance weighted averaging, but AIVAT's unbiasedness guarantee may have to be sacrificed. In our experiments, we use a dataset of 10,000 poker hands to demonstrate our heuristic pathology and uncertainty results, with the latter yielding a 43.0% reduction in the number of samples (poker hands) needed to draw statistical conclusions.

### [91] [Parallelizing Counterfactual Regret Minimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14277)
*Juho Kim,Tuomas Sandholm*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Paper parallelizes CFR algorithms by expressing them as linear algebra, enabling GPU acceleration and achieving up to 10,000× speedups over CPU implementations (OpenSpiel).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While parallelization has transformed large-scale AI (e.g., deep learning), it has been underutilized in computational game solving, particularly for large imperfect-information games where solving is computationally intensive. Counterfactual regret minimization (CFR) is a core algorithmic family for these games but existing implementations are typically CPU-bound and sequential, limiting scalability and practical applicability.

Method: The authors introduce a generalized parallelization framework that reformulates CFR updates as a sequence of linear algebra operations. By doing so, they can leverage mature, highly optimized parallel linear algebra libraries and hardware (notably GPUs) to run CFR in parallel. They extend this formulation to multiple tabular CFR variants, including CFR+, discounted CFR, and predictive CFR, and implement the resulting algorithms on GPUs.

Result: Their GPU-based CFR implementation achieves speedups of up to four orders of magnitude compared to Google DeepMind’s OpenSpiel CFR CPU implementations. This demonstrates that a linear-algebra-based parallelization greatly accelerates CFR across several tabular variants.

Conclusion: CFR and its tabular variants can be effectively expressed as linear algebra operations, enabling substantial parallelization on modern hardware such as GPUs. This approach yields massive practical speedups and suggests that parallelization is a powerful but underexploited tool in computational game solving for imperfect-information games.

Abstract: Parallelization has played an instrumental role in the field of artificial intelligence (AI), drastically reducing the time taken to train and evaluate large AI models. In contrast to its impact in the broader field of AI, applying parallelization to computational game solving is relatively unexplored, despite its great potential. In this paper, we parallelize the family of counterfactual regret minimization (CFR) algorithms, which were central to important breakthroughs for solving large imperfect-information games. We present a generalized parallelization framework, reframing CFR as a series of linear algebra operations. Then, existing techniques for parallelizing linear algebra operations can be applied to accelerate CFR. We also describe how our technique can be applied to other tabular members of the CFR family of algorithms, including the state-of-the-art, such as CFR+, discounted CFR, and predictive variants of CFR. Experimentally, we show that our CFR implementation on a GPU is up to four orders of magnitude faster than Google DeepMind OpenSpiel's CFR implementations on a CPU.

Method: The authors introduce a novel way to represent dot-product operations in transformer self-attention using ReLU networks that encode precise but nonlinear bounds on these dot products. Result: Across multiple transformer architectures and robustness properties on two sentiment-analysis datasets, the proposed rule-based and optimization-based frameworks obtain substantially tighter verification results than a state-of-the-art baseline that uses direct convex over-approximation of transformer computations. The new methods significantly reduce false alarms and improve precision, with only an acceptable overhead in runtime and computational cost.

Conclusion: By re-expressing transformer dot products through ReLU-based nonlinear bounds and leveraging mature convex relaxation techniques for ReLU networks, the paper achieves more precise yet still efficient formal verification of transformers. Abstract: Formal verification of transformers has become increasingly important due to their widespread deployment in safety-critical applications. Compared to classic neural networks, the inferences of transformers involve highly complex computations, such as dot products in self-attention layers, rendering their verification extremely difficult. Existing approaches explored over-approximation methods by constructing convex constraints to bound the output ranges of transformers, which can achieve high efficiency. However, they may sacrifice verification precision, and consequently introduce significant approximation error that leads to frequent occurrences of false alarms. In this paper, we propose a transformer verification approach that can achieve improved precision. At the core of our approach is a novel usage of ReLU, by which we represent a precise but non-linear bound for dot products such that we can further exploit the rich body of literature for convex relaxation of ReLU to derive precise bounds. We extend two classic approaches to the context of transformers, a rule-based one and an optimization-based one, resulting in two new frameworks for efficient and precise verification. We evaluate our approaches on different model architectures and robustness properties derived from two datasets about sentiment analysis, and compare with the state-of-the-art baseline approach. Compared to the baseline, our approach can achieve significant precision improvement for most of the verification tasks with acceptable compromise of efficiency, which demonstrates the effectiveness of our approach.

The segmentation is defined through domain informed criteria and sets up monitoring variables into functional groups reflecting operational mechanisms such as throughput,latency,pressure,network activity,and structural state. To evaluate the effectiveness of this decomposition, we adopt a predictive perspective in which expected predictive risk is used as an operational proxy for task-relevant information. Experimental results obtained through time-aware cross-validation show that the canonical space consistently achieves lower predictive risk than the residual space across multiple temporal configurations, indicating that the semantic segmentation concentrates the most relevant information for fault anticipation. In addition, the canonical segments exhibit significantly stronger intra-segment coherence than inter-segment dependence, and this structural organization remains stable after redundancy reduction. When compared with the full feature space and with a Principal Component Analysis (PCA) representation, the canonical space carries out comparable predictive performance and furthermore preserves the semantic meaning of the original variables. These findings suggest that semantic feature segmentation provides an interpretable and information-preserving decomposition of monitoring signals, enabling competitive predictive performance without sacrificing the operational interpretability required in predictive maintenance applications.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: CrystalReasoner is an end-to-end LLM framework that takes natural language instructions and produces crystal structures. Afterwards, it applies reinforcement learning with a dense, multi-objective reward function that encourages physical validity, chemical consistency, and thermodynamic stability. For property-conditioned tasks, it defines task-specific reward terms and trains specialized models targeting both discrete constraints (such as space group) and continuous properties (such as elasticity and thermal expansion).

Result: Compared with prior approaches and baselines that lack explicit reasoning traces or RL alignment, CrystalReasoner shows better performance across multiple evaluation metrics, including validity and stability of generated structures and success on property-conditioned generation. It reportedly triples the S.U.N. ratio and yields superior results on both discrete and continuous property constraints while also displaying adaptive reasoning behavior where the length of its thinking trace increases with system size (number of atoms).

Conclusion: Integrating explicit physical reasoning tokens with reinforcement learning alignment enables large language models to generate more valid, stable, and property-aligned crystal structures from natural language descriptions. This suggests that combining structured scientific priors, chain-of-thought style reasoning, and RL-based objective alignment is a promising direction for generative materials discovery.

Abstract: Generative modeling has emerged as a promising approach for crystal structure discovery. However, existing LLM-based generative models struggle with low-level atomic precision, while diffusion-based methods fall short in integrating high-level scientific knowledge. As a result, generated structures are often invalid, unstable, or do not possess desirable properties. To address this gap, we propose CrystalReasoner (\method), an end-to-end LLM framework that generates crystal structures from natural language instructions through reasoning and alignment. \method introduces physical priors as thinking tokens, which include crystallographic symmetry, local coordination environments and predicted physical properties before generating atomic coordinates. This bridges the gap between natural language and 3D structures. \method then employs reinforcement learning (RL) with a multi-objective, dense reward function to align generation with physical validity, chemical consistency, and thermodynamic stability. For property-conditioned tasks, we design task-specific reward functions and train specialized models for discrete constraints (e.g., space group) and continuous properties (e.g., elasticity, thermal expansion). Empirical results demonstrate that compared to prior works and baselines without thinking traces or RL, \method obtains better performance on diverse metrics, triples S.U.N. ratio, and achieves better performance for property conditioned generation. \method also exhibits adaptive reasoning, increasing reasoning lengths as the number of atoms increases. Our work demonstrates the potential of leveraging thinking traces and RL for generating valid, stable, and property-conditioned crystal structures. Please see our work at https://crystalreasoner.github.io/ .

### [97] [Uncovering the Representation Geometry of Minimal Cores in Overcomplete Reasoning Traces](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14358)
*Sanjoy Chowdhury,Dinesh Manocha*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates how much of language models' chain-of-thought reasoning is actually necessary for their final predictions, showing that many steps are redundant and introducing the notion of a minimal core of reasoning steps.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although language models can produce detailed chain-of-thought reasoning, it is unclear which parts of these traces are essential versus redundant for obtaining correct answers. Understanding this can clarify the structure of model reasoning, improve interpretability, and potentially make inference more efficient and robust.

Method: The authors define overcomplete reasoning traces and introduce the concept of a minimal core: the smallest subset of reasoning steps that preserves the model's final answer or predictive distribution. They design metrics such as compression ratio, redundancy mass, step necessity, and necessity concentration, and use a greedy minimal-core extraction procedure to analyze chain-of-thought traces across six benchmarks covering arithmetic, competition math, expert scientific reasoning, and commonsense multi-hop QA. They also analyze geometric properties (trace separation, intrinsic dimensionality) and cross-model transfer of minimal cores, supported by theoretical results on minimal sufficient subsets and properties of greedy elimination.

Result: Empirically, around 46% of reasoning steps can be removed on average while still preserving the original answer in 86% of cases across benchmarks. Necessity is highly concentrated: the top three steps carry about 65% of the measured necessity mass. Minimal cores better separate correct from incorrect traces by 11 points, reduce estimated intrinsic dimensionality by 34%, and maintain 85% answer retention when transferred across different model families. Theoretically, the authors prove existence and properties of minimal sufficient subsets, provide local irreducibility guarantees for their greedy algorithm, and formalize notions of overcompleteness and sparse necessity.

Conclusion: Language-model chain-of-thought traces are often verbose and contain substantial redundant reasoning. By extracting minimal cores—small, necessity-concentrated subsets of steps—one can capture the effective support of the model’s prediction more compactly, obtain cleaner geometric and statistical structure, and achieve robustness and transferability across models. This reframes how to interpret and use chain-of-thought explanations, highlighting that only a small portion of the trace is truly critical for the model’s decision.

Abstract: Language models often generate long chain-of-thought traces, but it remains unclear how much of this reasoning is necessary for preserving the final prediction. We study this through the lens of overcomplete reasoning traces: generated traces that contain more intermediate steps than are needed to support the model's answer. We define the minimal core as the smallest subset of steps that preserves either the final answer or predictive distribution, and introduce metrics for compression ratio, redundancy mass, step necessity, and necessity concentration. Across six deliberative reasoning benchmarks spanning arithmetic, competition mathematics, expert scientific reasoning, and commonsense multi-hop QA, we find substantial overcompleteness: on average, 46% of steps are removable under greedy minimal-core extraction while preserving the original answer in 86% of cases. We also find that predictive support is concentrated: the top three steps account for 65% of measured necessity mass on average. Beyond compression, minimal cores expose a cleaner geometry of reasoning: compared with full traces, they improve correct-incorrect trace separation by 11 points, reduce estimated intrinsic dimensionality by 34%, and transfer across model families with 85% off-diagonal answer retention. Theoretically, we establish existence of minimal sufficient subsets, local irreducibility guarantees for greedy elimination, and certificates of overcompleteness and sparse necessity. Together, these results suggest that full reasoning traces are often verbose and overcomplete, while minimal cores isolate the effective support underlying language-model predictions.

Evaluated on data strictly succeeding LLM knowledge cutoffs spanning Zillow real estate metrics and volatile stock market equities, Nexus consistently matches or outperforms state-of-the-art TSFMs and strong LLM baselines. Beyond numerical accuracy, Nexus produces high-quality reasoning traces that explicitly show the fundamental drivers behind each forecast. Our results establish that real-world forecasting is an agentic reasoning problem extending well beyond only sequence modeling.

### [99] [Learning to Build the Environment: Self-Evolving Reasoning RL via Verifiable Environment Synthesis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14392)
*Yucheng Shi,Zhenwen Liang,Kishan Panaganti,Dian Yu,Wenhao Yu,Haitao Mi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a framework where language models improve themselves by constructing training environments (not just data), relying on tasks where verifying solutions is easier than solving them, and demonstrates self-improvement via an environment generator called EvoEnv.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing self-improvement for language models often focuses on generating more synthetic data or trajectories, which can saturate or lead to reward hacking. The authors want a more principled way for models to continually improve by creating training tasks whose difficulty remains ahead of the model’s current abilities while still providing reliable feedback.

Method: They introduce zero-data reasoning RL as an environment-construction loop: the model writes executable Python environments that can sample instances, compute ground-truth references, and score responses. The key technical concept is "stable solve–verify asymmetry": environments should be such that the model can write a verifier/oracle once but still cannot reliably solve new instances in natural language. They analyze two classes of such tasks: (1) algorithmically hard to reason about step-by-step but easy to code (e.g., dynamic programming, graph algorithms), and (2) tasks that are hard to solve but easy to verify (e.g., planted subset-sum, constraint satisfaction). They implement this vision in EvoEnv, a system that has a single policy generate and solve Python environments starting from ten seed tasks, with rigorous staged validation (correctness checks, semantic self-review, calibration of difficulty relative to the solver, and novelty filters) before accepting new environments.

Result: Using Qwen3-4B-Thinking as the base model, they compare different RL setups: RLVR with fixed public data, RLVR with fixed hand-crafted environments, and RLVR with EvoEnv-generated environments. The first two baselines reduce average performance, while EvoEnv leads to an improvement from 72.4 to 74.8, about a 3.3% relative gain, indicating that EvoEnv enables stable self-improvement where other approaches fail or regress.

Conclusion: The study concludes that sustainable self-improvement in language models is less about producing large quantities of synthetic data and more about enabling models to construct training environments with persistent solve–verify asymmetries. By building worlds whose difficulty remains structurally beyond the model’s current reasoning capacity—but within its ability to verify—the model can continue to learn without collapsing reward signals or overfitting to its own generators. EvoEnv is presented as an initial but promising instantiation of this paradigm.

Abstract: We pursue a vision for self-improving language models in which the model does not merely generate problems or traces to imitate, but constructs the environments that train it. In zero-data reasoning RL, this reframes self-improvement from a data-generation loop into an environment-construction loop, where each artifact is a reusable executable object that samples instances, computes references, and scores responses. Whether this vision sustains improvement hinges on a single property: the environments must exhibit stable solve--verify asymmetry, the model must be able to write an oracle once that it cannot reliably execute in natural language on fresh instances. This asymmetry takes two complementary forms. Some tasks are algorithmically hard to reason through but trivial as code: a dynamic program or graph traversal, compiled once, yields unboundedly many calibrated instances. Others are intrinsically hard to solve but easy to verify, like planted subset-sum or constraint satisfaction. Both create a durable gap between proposing and solving that the policy cannot close by gaming the verifier, and it is this gap that keeps reward informative as the learner improves. We instantiate this view in EvoEnv, a single-policy generator, solver method that synthesizes Python environments from ten seeds and admits them only after staged validation, semantic self-review, solver-relative difficulty calibration, and novelty checks. The strongest evidence comes from the already-strong regime: on Qwen3-4B-Thinking, fixed public-data RLVR and fixed hand-crafted environment RLVR reduce the average, while EvoEnv improves it from 72.4 to 74.8, a relative gain of 3.3%. Stable self-improvement, we suggest, depends not on producing more synthetic data, but on models learning to construct worlds whose difficulty stays structurally beyond their own reach.

### [100] [Coding Agent Is Good As World Simulator](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14398)

  <summary>Details</summary>

This leads to artifacts such as unstable contacts, distorted shapes, and inconsistent motion, which limits their reliability for applications like driving simulation and robot tasks. The motivation is to construct world models that are both visually convincing and physically plausible by embedding explicit physics into the generation process.

Method: The authors propose an agentic framework that constructs world models through executable simulation code. The system consists of multiple cooperating agents: (1) a planning agent that converts a natural language prompt into a structured scene plan; (2) a code generation agent that translates the plan into executable physics simulation code; (3) a visual review agent that inspects rendered simulation outputs for visual fidelity to the prompt; and (4) a physics analysis agent that checks the physical consistency of the simulated dynamics. The framework runs in an iterative loop where feedback from the visual review and physics analysis agents is used to revise the simulation code until both the prompt requirements and physical constraints are satisfied.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that the proposed framework achieves higher physical accuracy, better adherence to natural language instructions, and improved visual quality compared to advanced video-based world models. The abstract notes successful application to use cases such as driving simulations and embodied robotic tasks, indicating strong generalization to different interactive scenarios.

Conclusion: By shifting from video-only latent dynamics models to an agentic system that generates and refines executable physics simulation code, the framework yields world models that are simultaneously visually realistic and physically plausible. The multi-agent loop of planning, coding, and physics-aware review enables better alignment with both natural language instructions and underlying physical laws, making the approach promising for safety-critical and interactive domains like driving and robotics.

Abstract: World models have emerged as a powerful paradigm for building interactive simulation environments, with recent video-based approaches demonstrating impressive progress in generating visually plausible dynamics. However, because these models typically infer dynamics from video and represent them in latent states, they do not explicitly enforce physical constraints. As a result, the generated video rollouts are not physically plausible, exhibiting unstable contacts, distorted shapes, or inconsistent motion. In this paper, we present an agentic framework constructing physics-based world models through executable simulation code. The framework coordinates planning, code generation, visual review, and physics analysis agents. The planning agent converts the natural language prompt into a structured scene plan, the code agent implements it as executable simulation code, and the visual review agent provide visual feedback while the physics analysis agent checks physical consistency. The code is iteratively revised based on the feedback until the simulation matches the prompt reqirements and physical constraints. Experimental results show that our framework outperforms advanced video-based models in physical accuracy, instruction fidelity and visual quality, which could be applied to various scenarios including driving simulation and embodied robot tasks.

Motivation: Existing debates focus on AI’s inability to handle physical, embodied tasks, but overlook many important digital tasks that remain stubbornly resistant to automation. The authors want to explain why certain digital tasks defy reliable AI even though they are computationally tractable, and to provide a conceptual framework for understanding these limits and guiding system design.

They show these features are inherent to the tasks, not just current AI models’ weaknesses, and that they reshape how automation limits should be understood.

Conclusion: Metis AI tasks reveal that the most important limitation of AI lies within digital work: some computer-based tasks are structurally unsuited to full automation because of their social, institutional, and normative entanglements. For these tasks, progress should not aim at replacing humans but at designing centaur architectures in which humans retain leadership and accountability while AI provides support and augmentation.

Abstract: The dominant discourse on AI limitations frames the boundary of AI capability as a divide between digital tasks (where AI excels) and physical tasks (where embodiment is required). We argue this framing misses the most consequential boundary: the one within digital tasks. We identify a class of tasks we call Metis AI, named for the Greek concept of metis (practical, contextual knowledge), that are performed entirely on computers yet resist reliable AI automation. These tasks are not computationally intractable; they are institutionally, socially, and normatively entangled in ways that defeat algorithmic approaches. We distinguish constitutive metis (knowledge destroyed by the act of formalization) from operational metis (system-specific familiarity that automation can progressively absorb), and propose five structural characteristics that define the Metis AI zone: consequential irreversibility, relational irreducibility, normative open texture, adversarial co-evolution, and accountability anchoring. We ground each in established theory from across the social sciences, philosophy, and humanitarian practice, argue that these characteristics are properties of the tasks themselves rather than limitations of current models, and show that the appropriate design response is not better automation but centaur architectures in which humans lead and AI supports.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Recent RL-based methods are more flexible but treat CVRP in an end-to-end, black-box manner and do not explicitly exploit established problem-structure knowledge, which limits solution quality and generalization, especially for real-world variants with added constraints like time windows and backhauls. There is a need for a unified, learning-based framework that can leverage domain heuristics and handle diverse CVRP variants effectively.

Method: They design a knowledge-embedded reinforcement learning framework inspired by classical Route-First Cluster-Second heuristics. The original CVRP is decomposed into two subproblems: (1) a route-first problem, solved by an RL-based constructive solver, and (2) a cluster-second assignment problem, solved via dynamic programming. The dynamic programming solution of the cluster-second subproblem provides guidance to the RL solver for the route-first problem, embedding explicit problem-solving knowledge into the learning process. To address partial observability introduced by this decomposition, they introduce a unified history-enhanced context module that maintains and processes historical information across decision steps.

Result: In extensive experiments across multiple CVRP variants, the proposed framework produces solutions of higher quality than state-of-the-art learning-based baselines and narrows the optimality gap relative to strong classical heuristics. It also demonstrates robust generalization across different CVRP variants, including those with complex constraints such as time windows and backhauls.

Conclusion: Embedding domain knowledge—specifically route-first, cluster-second structure and dynamic-programming-based clustering—into an RL framework yields a more powerful and generalizable solver for CVRP and its variants. The unified history-enhanced context module effectively mitigates partial observability from problem decomposition. Overall, the approach bridges the gap between classical heuristics and end-to-end learning, achieving better solution quality and generalization than existing learning-based methods while approaching the performance of traditional heuristics.

Abstract: The Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem (CVRP) is a fundamental NP-hard problem with broad applications in logistics and transportation. Real-world CVRPs often involve diverse objectives and complex constraints, such as time windows or backhaul requirements, motivating the development of a unified solution framework. Recent reinforcement learning (RL) approaches have shown promise in combinatorial optimization, yet they rely on end-to-end learning and lack explicit problem-solving knowledge, limiting solution quality. In this paper, we propose a knowledge-embedded framework inspired by the Route-First Cluster-Second heuristics. It incorporates knowledge at two levels: (1) decomposing CVRPs into the route-first and cluster-second subproblems, and (2) leveraging dynamic programming to solve the second subproblem, whose results guide the RL-based constructive solver to solve the first problem. To mitigate partial observability caused by problem decomposition, we introduce a unified history-enhanced context processing module. Extensive experiments show that this framework achieves superior solution quality compared with state-of-the-art learning-based methods, with a smaller gap to classical heuristics, demonstrating strong generalization across diverse CVRP variants.

Method: The authors propose DVMap, which has several key components: (1) They extract high-consensus demographic archetypes from the World Values Survey (WVS), retaining only respondents whose value preferences are consistent within identical demographic profiles, resulting in a curated corpus of 56,152 samples. (2) They introduce a Structured Chain-of-Thought mechanism that explicitly prompts LLMs to reason about correlations between demographics and values. (3) They use Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), an RL-style fine-tuning method, to adaptively anchor the model’s value distributions to those observed in demographic groups. On cross-demographic tests, a Qwen3-8B model fine-tuned with DVMap (Qwen3-8B-DVMap) reaches 48.6% accuracy, outperforming a strong open-source baseline, DeepSeek-v3.2, which achieves 45.1%. The framework also maintains solid performance across cross-country and cross-value generalization tasks, indicating that the demographic-based approach yields more reliable pluralistic value alignment than national-label-based methods.

Conclusion: Fine-grained demographic constraints provide a more effective basis for pluralistic value alignment in LLMs than coarse national labels. By constructing a high-consensus demographic-value corpus, guiding models with Structured CoT about demographic-value relations, and optimizing with GRPO, DVMap yields models that better capture the mapping from demographics to value preferences and generalize well across diverse groups and contexts. The released code and dataset enable further research on demographic-based value alignment and evaluation.

Abstract: Current Large Language Models (LLMs) typically rely on coarse-grained national labels for pluralistic value alignment. However, such macro-level supervision often obscures intra-country value heterogeneity, yielding a loose alignment. We argue that resolving this limitation requires shifting from national labels to multi-dimensional demographic constraints, which can identify groups with predictable, high-consensus value preference. To this end, we propose DVMap (High-Consensus Demographic-Value Mapping), a framework for fine-grained pluralistic value alignment. In this framework, we first present a demographic archetype extraction strategy to construct a high-quality value alignment corpus of 56,152 samples from the World Values Survey (WVS) by strictly retaining respondents with consistent value preferences under identical demographics. Over this corpus, we introduce a Structured Chain-of-Thought (CoT) mechanism that explicitly guides LLMs to reason about demographic-value correlations. Subsequently, we employ Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) to achieve adaptive anchoring of value distributions. To rigorously evaluate generalization, we further establish a triple-generalization benchmark (spanning cross-demographic, cross-country, and cross-value) comprising 21,553 samples. Experimental results demonstrate that DVMap effectively learns the manifold mapping from demographics to values, exhibiting strong generalization and robustness. On cross-demographic tests, Qwen3-8B-DVMap achieves 48.6% accuracy, surpassing the advanced open-source LLM DeepSeek-v3.2 (45.1%). The source code and dataset are available at https://github.com/EnlightenedAI/DVMap.

The authors aim to create a practical way to speed up MoE inference without heavy retraining or major accuracy loss.

Method: The authors propose BEAM (Binary Expert Activation Masking), which introduces trainable binary masks for each token-expert pair to decide which experts to activate dynamically. They use a straight-through estimator to train these binary masks in an end-to-end fashion and add an auxiliary regularization term that encourages sparsity in expert activation while preserving model capability. They also implement a custom CUDA kernel and integrate BEAM into the vLLM inference framework to make the method efficient and easy to deploy.

Result: BEAM reduces the FLOPs in MoE layers by up to 85% while maintaining over 98% of the original model performance. Empirically, it achieves up to 2.5× faster decoding speed and 1.4× higher throughput in inference compared to the baseline MoE setup, showing that the method is both efficient and accurate.

Conclusion: BEAM provides a practical, plug-and-play mechanism for dynamically sparse expert activation in MoE language models, substantially improving inference efficiency with minimal performance degradation and without requiring disruptive architectural changes or complex retraining pipelines.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) architectures enhance the efficiency of large language models by activating only a subset of experts per token. However, standard MoE employs a fixed Top-K routing strategy, leading to redundant computation and suboptimal inference latency. Existing acceleration methods either require costly retraining with architectural changes or suffer from severe performance drop at high sparsity due to train-inference mismatch. To address these limitations, we propose BEAM (Binary Expert Activation Masking), a novel method that learns token-adaptive expert selection via trainable binary masks. With a straight-through estimator and an auxiliary regularization loss, BEAM induces dynamic expert sparsity through end-to-end training while maintaining model capability. We further implement an efficient custom CUDA kernel for BEAM, ensuring seamless integration with the vLLM inference framework. Experiments show that BEAM retains over 98\% of the original model's performance while reducing MoE layer FLOPs by up to 85\%, achieving up to 2.5$\times$ faster decoding and 1.4$\times$ higher throughput, demonstrating its effectiveness as a practical, plug-and-play solution for efficient MoE inference.

*Debraj Chakraborty,Anirban Majumdar,Prince Mathew,Sayan Mukherjee,Jean-François Raskin*

  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for approaches that retain scalability while offering formal guarantees, especially for safety-critical systems.

Method: The authors design a synthesis framework inspired by Angluin’s L* algorithm. Sampling from the POMDP is used as a membership oracle to learn a regular representation of the policy, and model checking is used as an equivalence oracle to verify whether the learned controller satisfies the specification. When model checking finds counterexamples, they are fed back to refine the learned controller iteratively. The approach targets finite-state controllers and assumes the sampling-induced policy is regular. The framework is analyzed to show relative completeness under these assumptions.

Result: They implement a prototype of the framework and run experiments on threshold-safety problems in POMDPs. The results show that their method can solve instances that are challenging for existing formal synthesis tools, indicating improved practical performance while maintaining correctness guarantees.

Conclusion: The proposed framework provides a middle ground between scalable sampling-based methods and formally verified synthesis. Under regularity assumptions, it can synthesize finite-state controllers with formal guarantees and shows promising performance on hard threshold-safety benchmarks. The authors position their algorithm as a useful component in a broader portfolio of methods for tackling the inherently difficult POMDP synthesis problem.

Abstract: Partially Observable Markov Decision Processes (POMDPs) are the standard framework for decision-making under uncertainty. While sampling-based methods scale well, they lack formal correctness guarantees, making them unsuitable for safety-critical applications. Conversely, formal synthesis techniques provide correctness-by-construction but often struggle with scalability, as general POMDP synthesis is undecidable. To bridge this gap, we propose a synthesis framework that integrates sampling, automata learning, and model-checking. Inspired by Angluin's $L^*$ algorithm, our approach utilizes sampling as a membership oracle and model-checking as an equivalence oracle. This enables the synthesis of finite-state controllers with formal guarantees, provided the sampling-induced policy is regular. We establish a relative completeness result for this framework. Experimental results from our prototypical implementation demonstrate that this method successfully solves threshold-safety problems that remain challenging for existing formal synthesis tools. We believe our algorithm serves as a valuable component in a portfolio approach to tackling the inherent difficulty of POMDP synthesis problems.

By combining scalar rewards with textual critiques in a contrastive experience buffer, the method discovers specialized, algorithmic prompt structures and outperforms evolutionary baselines both in performance and sample efficiency. This positions learned prompting as a powerful, scalable alternative to manual prompt engineering for black-box LLMs.

Abstract: The shift toward interacting with frozen, "black-box" Large Language Models (LLMs) has transformed prompt engineering from a heuristic exercise into a critical optimization challenge. We propose a Reinforcement Learning (RL) framework for training learned prompting policies via iterative distillation of experience. In this architecture, a lightweight prompter model is optimized to maximize task-specific rewards for a larger, frozen worker LLM. By utilizing a contrastive experience buffer that couples scalar rewards with dense textual critiques, our approach effectively amortizes iterative prompt refinement into single-shot policy weights.
  Our experimental analysis focuses on the Big Bench Extra Hard (BBEH) and Tau-bench suites, covering a diverse range of multi-step reasoning and tool-use tasks. We demonstrate significant gains, improving performance from 55% to 90% in logic-intensive reasoning and 74% to 91% in tool-use tasks. Furthermore, we analyze the structural evolution of prompts, demonstrating how the policy discovers specialized algorithmic heuristics. We provide comprehensive comparisons against state-of-the-art evolutionary baselines like GEPA, showing that iterative distillation achieves superior performance with higher sample efficiency.

Conclusion: IIQ is presented as a practical, deployment-focused framework for measuring AI integration into organizational workflows. It is not a capability benchmark or a causal productivity measure, but a structured way to track adoption depth and potential impact of AI use over time, enabling comparison across teams and use cases and providing interpretable estimates of efficiency and financial effects.

Abstract: The Intelligence Impact Quotient (IIQ) is a composite metric intended to quantify the depth to which AI systems are integrated into organizational work and their impact. We also derive sub-daily update rules and a bounded interpretation layer for estimated efficiency and financial impact. The paper positions IIQ as a deployment-oriented measurement framework: a formal proposal for tracking AI embedding in workflows, not a direct measure of model capability or a substitute for causal productivity evaluation. Synthetic scenarios illustrate how the revised metric distinguishes between frequent low-leverage use, semantically repetitive prompting, and more autonomous, higher-consequence AI-assisted work.
