### [108] [Stateful Reasoning via Insight Replay](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14457)
*Bin Lei,Caiwen Ding,Jiachen Yang,Ang Li,Xin Eric Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies a key failure mode of long Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning—models forget earlier crucial insights as the reasoning trace grows—and proposes InsightReplay, which periodically extracts and restates these insights near the current generation frontier, yielding consistent accuracy gains across multiple models and benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although longer CoT generally helps LLMs solve harder problems, performance on a fixed problem does not improve monotonically with longer reasoning. Accuracy first goes up with CoT length and then degrades. The authors want to understand why this happens and how to make test-time scaling of reasoning more reliably beneficial. They hypothesize that a major cause is that models lose effective access to important intermediate insights because attention to distant tokens fades over long traces. They aim to design a mechanism that keeps these critical insights accessible throughout extended reasoning.

Method: The authors introduce InsightReplay, a stateful reasoning procedure layered on top of standard CoT. During multi-step reasoning, the model periodically (in multiple rounds) extracts what it judges to be the critical insights from the reasoning so far and then replays or restates them close to the current generation frontier, effectively summarizing and resurfacing key information. This maintains high attention on crucial earlier deductions despite long context. They evaluate InsightReplay on a 2×3×4 grid of experimental conditions: two model sizes (8B, 30B), three model families (Qwen3.5, DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen, Gemma-4), and four reasoning benchmarks (AIME, HMMT, GPQA Diamond, LiveCodeBench v5), using a 3-round InsightReplay protocol and comparing performance to standard CoT.

Result: Across all 24 combinations of model scale, family, and benchmark, 3-round InsightReplay improves accuracy relative to standard CoT. The average improvement is +1.65 percentage points, and the maximum observed gain in a single setting is +9.2 points on the LiveCodeBench v5 subset for the R1-Distill-32B model. These consistent gains indicate that re-surfacing intermediate insights can mitigate the degradation seen with longer CoT traces.

Conclusion: The study concludes that the benefits of test-time scaling in LLM reasoning depend not only on increasing the length or amount of reasoning, but also on maintaining accessibility of key intermediate insights over long reasoning trajectories. InsightReplay—by periodically extracting and replaying critical insights—addresses a central weakness of long CoT and produces robust accuracy improvements across multiple models and tasks. This suggests that future reasoning frameworks should incorporate mechanisms for stateful management and reinforcement of important intermediate conclusions, rather than relying solely on ever-longer chains of thought.

Abstract: Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning has become a foundation for eliciting multi-step reasoning in large language models, but recent studies show that its benefits do not scale monotonically with chain length: while longer CoT generally enables a model to tackle harder problems, on a given problem, accuracy typically increases with CoT length up to a point, after which it declines. We identify a major cause of this phenomenon: as the CoT grows, the model's attention to critical insights produced earlier in the trace gradually weakens, making those insights progressively less accessible when they are most needed. Therefore, we propose \textbf{InsightReplay}, a stateful reasoning approach in which the model periodically extracts critical insights from its reasoning trace and replays them near the active generation frontier, keeping them accessible as the reasoning scales. Extensive experiments on a $\mathbf{2}\!\times\!\mathbf{3}\!\times\!\mathbf{4}$ benchmark grid, covering model scales $\{\text{8B}, \text{30B}\}$, model families $\{\text{Qwen3.5}, \text{DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen}, \text{Gemma-4}\}$, and reasoning benchmarks $\{\text{AIME}, \text{HMMT}, \text{GPQA Diamond}, \text{LiveCodeBench v5}\}$, show that 3-round InsightReplay yields accuracy gains across \textbf{all 24 settings}, with an averaged improvement of $\mathbf{+1.65}$ points over standard CoT, and a largest single-setting gain of $\mathbf{+9.2}$ points on R1-Distill-32B's LiveCodeBench v5 subset. Our results suggest that the effectiveness of test-time scaling depends not only on how much a model reasons, but also on whether critical intermediate insights remain accessible throughout long reasoning trajectories.

Method: OmniDrop prunes audiovisual tokens progressively inside the LLM decoder layers instead of at the input. Early layers keep rich multimodal information; deeper layers aggressively remove less relevant tokens. The pruning is guided by text queries to be modality-agnostic and task-adaptive, and a temporal diversity score ensures balanced token survival over time to maintain global temporal context. The approach is training-free.

Result: On several audiovisual benchmarks, OmniDrop outperforms existing token compression baselines by up to 3.58 points while reducing prefill latency by up to 40% and memory usage by up to 14.7%.

Conclusion: Layer-wise, query-guided, training-free token pruning (OmniDrop) addresses token explosion in omni-modal LLMs more effectively than input-level pruning, enabling better accuracy with lower latency and memory for real-time, long-form audiovisual understanding.

Abstract: Omni-modal large language models have demonstrated remarkable potential in holistic multimodal understanding; however, the token explosion caused by high-resolution audio and video inputs remains a critical bottleneck for real-time applications and long-form reasoning. Existing omni-modal token compression methods typically prune tokens at the input embedding level, relying on audio-video similarity or temporal co-occurrence as proxies for semantic relevance. In practice, such assumptions are often unreliable. To address this limitation, we propose OmniDrop, a training-free, layer-wise token pruning framework that progressively prunes audiovisual tokens within the LLM decoder layers rather than at the input-level, allowing early layers to preserve sufficient omni-modal information fusion before aggressively removing tokens in deeper layers. We further utilize text queries as guidance for modality-agnostic and task-adaptive token pruning. We also introduce a temporal diversity score that encourages balanced token survival to preserve global temporal context. Experimental results across various audiovisual benchmarks demonstrate that OmniDrop outperforms all baselines by up to 3.58 points while reducing prefill latency by up to 40% and memory usage by up to 14.7%.

### [110] [From Table to Cell: Attention for Better Reasoning with TABALIGN](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14465)
*Tung Sum Thomas Kwok,Zeyong Zhang,Xinyu Wang,Chunhe Wang,Xiaofeng Lin,Hanwei Wu,Lei Ding,Guang Cheng,Zhijiang Guo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces TABALIGN, a framework for multi-step reasoning over tables that enforces an explicit grounding contract between planning and execution using diffusion language models and cell-level attention masks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-step LLM reasoning on structured tables often fails because planning steps are not explicitly grounded to specific table cells, existing planners assume left-to-right token factorization that ignores table permutation invariance, and intermediate reasoning states are scored only by generated content without verifying whether the model is attending to the correct cells. The authors want a more human-aligned, permutation-stable way to plan and verify table reasoning.

Method: The authors first run a pilot study comparing diffusion language models (DLMs) and autoregressive (AR) models on table attention, showing DLMs yield more human-aligned and permutation-stable cell attention. Based on this, they propose TABALIGN: a planned table reasoning framework where (1) a masked DLM planner performs bidirectional denoising to produce each reasoning step as a binary mask over table cells, and (2) TABATTN, a small verifier trained on 1,600 human-verified attention labels, scores each step according to the overlap between the model’s actual attention and the planner’s designated mask, thus enforcing an explicit grounding contract between planning and execution.

Result: In their experiments on eight benchmarks spanning table QA and fact verification, TABALIGN improves average accuracy by 15.76 percentage points over the strongest open-source 8B-class baseline. An ablation with the same backbone shows that using a DLM planner instead of an autoregressive planner contributes 2.87 percentage points of this gain, and the cleaner plans also speed up downstream reasoning execution by 44.64%.

Conclusion: TABALIGN demonstrates that using diffusion language models for planning with explicit cell-level grounding, combined with an attention-based verifier, substantially improves both accuracy and efficiency in multi-step table reasoning. The study highlights the advantages of permutation-stable, human-aligned attention patterns and suggests that enforcing a planning–execution grounding contract is a promising direction for structured reasoning over tables.

Abstract: Multi-step LLM reasoning over structured tables fails because planning and execution share no explicit cell-grounding contract. Existing methods constrain the planner to a left-to-right factorization at odds with table permutation invariance, and score intermediate states by generated content alone, overlooking cell grounding. We conduct a pilot study showing that diffusion language models (DLMs) produce more human-aligned and permutation-stable cell attention on tables than autoregressive models, with a 40.2% median reduction in attention-AUROC variability under row reordering. Motivated by this, we propose TABALIGN, a planned table reasoning framework that operationalizes the contract. TABALIGN pairs a masked DLM planner, whose bidirectional denoising emits plan steps as binary cell masks, with TABATTN, a lightweight verifier trained on 1,600 human-verified attention standards to score each step by its attention overlap with the plan-designated mask. Across eight benchmarks covering table question answering and fact verification, TABALIGN improves average accuracy by 15.76 percentage points over the strongest open-source baseline at comparable 8B-class scale, with a matched-backbone ablation attributing 2.87 percentage points of this gain to the DLM planner over an AR planner on a fixed reasoner. Cleaner DLM plans also accelerate downstream reasoning execution by 44.64%.

### [112] [Learning Scenario Reduction for Two-Stage Robust Optimization with Discrete Uncertainty](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14494)
*Tianjue Lin,Jianan Zhou,Jieyi Bi,Yaoxin Wu,Wen Song,Zhiguang Cao,Jie Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes NeurPRISE, a neural surrogate to accelerate problem-driven scenario reduction for two-stage robust optimization with discrete uncertainty, achieving large speedups while retaining low regret and strong generalization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Two-stage robust optimization with discrete uncertainty suffers from prohibitive computational cost when the number of scenarios is large. Existing scenario reduction methods are problem-agnostic and ignore the structure of the feasible region and recourse, while a problem-driven method (PRISE) is high quality but too slow because it repeatedly solves subproblems. There is a need for a scalable, structure-aware scenario reduction approach that preserves robustness quality while being computationally efficient.

Method: The authors first design PRISE, a problem-driven sequential lookahead heuristic that iteratively selects scenarios for a reduced set by explicitly evaluating the marginal impact (gain) of including each scenario, using repeated subproblem solves. To overcome PRISE's computational burden, they develop NeurPRISE, a neural surrogate model with a GNN-Transformer architecture: graph convolutions encode per-scenario problem structure, and attention layers model interactions across scenarios. NeurPRISE is trained via imitation learning with a gain-aware ranking loss to mimic PRISE’s scenario-selection ordering. At inference time, NeurPRISE scores and ranks scenarios to construct reduced sets quickly without solving many optimization subproblems.

Result: On three benchmark two-stage robust optimization problems, NeurPRISE produces reduced scenario sets whose robust solutions have regret close to those from more exhaustive or exact methods. It scales well as the number of scenarios increases and attains substantial computational gains, running 7–200 times faster than PRISE while maintaining comparable solution quality. Experiments also demonstrate strong zero-shot generalization to instances with up to 5x larger problem size, up to 4x more scenarios, and under distribution shifts in the uncertainty.

Conclusion: Problem-driven, learning-augmented scenario reduction is an effective way to make two-stage robust optimization with many discrete scenarios tractable. NeurPRISE successfully distills PRISE’s high-quality but expensive selection behavior into a fast neural ranking model, yielding large speedups with minimal loss in robustness and good generalization beyond training conditions.

Abstract: Two-Stage Robust Optimization (2RO) with discrete uncertainty is challenging, often rendering exact solutions prohibitive. Scenario reduction alleviates this issue by selecting a small, representative subset of scenarios to enable tractable computation. However, existing methods are largely problem-agnostic, operating solely on the uncertainty set without consulting the feasible region or recourse structure. In this paper, we introduce PRISE, a problem-driven sequential lookahead heuristic that constructs reduced scenario sets by evaluating the marginal impact of each scenario. While PRISE yields high-quality scenario subsets, each selection step requires solving multiple subproblems, making it computationally expensive at scale. To address this, we propose NeurPRISE, a neural surrogate model built on a GNN-Transformer backbone that encodes the per-scenario structure via graph convolution and captures cross-scenario interactions through attention. NeurPRISE is trained via imitation learning with a gain-aware ranking objective, which distills marginal gain information from PRISE into a learned scoring function for scenario ranking and selection. Extensive results on three 2RO problems show that NeurPRISE consistently achieves competitive regret relative to comprehensive methods, maintains strong calability with varying numbers of scenarios, and delivers 7-200x speedup over PRISE. NeurPRISE also exhibits strong zero-shot generalization, effectively handling instances with larger problem scales (up to 5x), more scenarios (up to 4x), and distribution shifts.

*Robert Müller,Clemens Müller*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a unified, long-horizon environment that measures joint deployment of these skills and exposes behavioural failure modes, not just final scores.

Method: The authors design Cattle Trade, a 50–60 turn multi-agent economic game that integrates auctions, hidden-offer trade challenges, bargaining, bluffing, opponent modeling, and resource allocation under imperfect information and conflicting incentives. They then run 242 games comparing seven cost-efficient LLM agents with three deterministic heuristic code agents, analyzing performance, spending patterns, and recurrent behavioral mistakes.

Result: Across 242 games, strategic coherence—especially spending efficiency, disciplined resource management, and adapting bidding strategies across game phases—correlates more strongly with agent ranking than raw spending volume or any isolated strategic subskill. Two simple heuristic code agents outperform most evaluated LLMs. The detailed logs reveal characteristic LLM errors such as overbidding, bidding against themselves, initiating trade challenges while bankrupt, and failing to adjust to inferred opponent states.

Conclusion: Cattle Trade demonstrates that current LLM agents struggle to consistently integrate strategic subskills in complex, competitive multi-agent environments. Simple heuristic agents can outperform them when they maintain coherent, resource-aware strategies. The work argues that assessing agentic competence requires benchmarks like Cattle Trade that jointly test multiple abilities—strategic reasoning, opponent modeling, bluffing, and economic decision-making—under imperfect information and conflicting incentives, and that rich behavioral traces are crucial for diagnosing failure modes beyond aggregate scores.

Abstract: We introduce \textsc{Cattle Trade, a multi-agent benchmark for evaluating large language models (LLMs) as agents in strategic reasoning under imperfect information, adversarial interaction, and resource constraints. The benchmark combines auctions, hidden-offer trade challenges (TCs), bargaining, bluffing, opponent modeling, and resource allocation within a single long-horizon game lasting 50--60 turns. Unlike prior agent benchmarks that test these abilities in isolation, \textsc{Cattle Trade} evaluates whether agents integrate them across a competitive, multi-agent economic game with conflicting incentives. The benchmark logs every bid, TC offer, counteroffer, and card selection, enabling behavioural analysis beyond final scores or win rates. We evaluate seven cost-efficient language models and three deterministic code agents across 242 games. Strategic coherence, in particular spending efficiency, resource discipline, and phase-adaptive bidding, is associated with rank more strongly than spending volume or any single subskill. Two heuristic code agents outperform most tested LLMs, and behavioural traces surface recurring LLM failure modes including overbidding, self-bidding, bankrupt TC initiation, and weak opponent-state adaptation. Evaluating agentic competence requires benchmarks that test the joint deployment of multiple capabilities in multi-agent environments with conflicting incentives, uncertainty, and economic dynamics.

### [116] [Complacent, Not Sycophantic: Reframing Large Language Models and Designing AI Literacy for Complacent Machines](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14544)
*Federico Germani,Giovanni Spitale*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that large language models (LLMs) are not truly 'sycophantic' but rather 'complacent', meaning they structurally tend to agree with users due to how they are trained and designed.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To clarify conceptual confusion about describing LLMs as 'sycophantic' and to more accurately attribute agency and responsibility for their behavior to developers and institutions rather than to the models themselves.

Method: Conceptual and theoretical analysis distinguishing sycophancy (implying motives and intent) from complacency (a structural tendency) in the behavior of LLMs, grounded in observation of training processes, reward structures, and design choices.

Result: The authors establish that sycophancy is a misleading label for LLM behavior and propose 'complacency' as a more accurate characterization, linking this to the ways training data and reward signals favor agreement and reinforcement over correction.

Conclusion: Models themselves lack motives and cannot be sycophantic; they can only exhibit varying degrees of complacency shaped by developer choices. This reframing emphasizes developer and institutional responsibility and suggests that AI literacy should focus on teaching users strategies to counter confirmation bias, since complacent models tend to reinforce users' existing beliefs.

Abstract: Large language models are often described as sycophantic, in the sense that they appear to flatter users or mirror their beliefs. We argue that this label is conceptually misleading: sycophancy implies motives and strategic intent, which LLMs do not possess. Their behaviour is better understood as complacency, a structural tendency to agree with user input because training data, reward signals and design favour agreement and reinforcement over correction. We argue that this distinction matters. Whether developers act sycophantically or not, models themselves never are sycophants; they can only be made more or less complacent. This reframing locates agency in developers and institutions, not in the model. Because complacent models reinforce users' prior beliefs, we argue that AI literacy educational approaches should particularly focus on strategies to counter confirmation bias.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While PyCSP^3 supports constraint modeling and some global constraints, it lacks native scheduling abstractions like interval and sequence variables. This forces modelers to use low-level integer encodings and manual channeling, which is error-prone and less expressive. The authors aim to introduce first-class scheduling constructs while keeping compatibility with existing PyCSP^3/XCSP^3 ecosystems and maintaining the clear separation between model specification and solving.

Method: They design and implement PyCSP^3 Scheduling, a library that adds scheduling-specific abstractions (interval variables, sequence variables, resource functions) with 53 dedicated constraints and 27 expressions. These high-level constructs are automatically compiled into standard PyCSP^3/XCSP^3 constraints, effectively decomposing them into low-level forms. They then benchmark the compiled models against manually written baseline PyCSP^3 formulations on 261 paired instances from 17 scheduling model families, comparing objectives, structural properties, and runtimes.

Result: The compiled and baseline formulations achieve identical objective values on all 72 instance pairs where optimality is proven by both, confirming semantic correctness. Around 8 of the 17 model families remain structurally unchanged after compilation, indicating that the high-level abstractions can map closely to existing constraints. Runtime performance varies: some families see significant speedups (up to 5.8x) thanks to better structure or easier modeling, while others experience slowdowns due to the overhead introduced by decomposition of high-level constructs into lower-level constraints.

Conclusion: PyCSP^3 Scheduling successfully adds rich, native scheduling abstractions to PyCSP^3 while preserving the core design principle of separating modeling from solving and staying within the XCSP^3 format. Though performance impact is mixed—improving some instances and degrading others—the approach is practically viable and semantically sound, and the library plus benchmarks provide a foundation for further optimization of scheduling encodings in constraint programming tools.

Abstract: PyCSP$^3$ provides a productive way to build constraint models for solving combinatorial constrained problems and export them to XCSP$^3$, preserving a complete separation between modeling and solving. However, it lacks native support for scheduling abstractions such as interval variables, sequence variables, and resource functions. As a result, scheduling models must be encoded with low-level integer variables and manual channeling constraints, even though PyCSP$^3$ already provides global constraints like NoOverlap and Cumulative on integer arrays. We present PyCSP$^3$ Scheduling, a library that adds scheduling abstractions to PyCSP$^3$ through 53 dedicated constraints and 27 expressions, and compiles them down to standard PyCSP$^3$/XCSP$^3$ constraints, maintaining the modeling/solving separation that underpins the PyCSP$^3$ ecosystem. On 261 paired instances across 17 model families (5 runs each), both formulations produce identical objectives on all 72 doubly-proved optimal pairs and nearly half of the families (8/17) remain structurally unchanged after compilation; however, runtime performance diverges across families, with clear gains on some (up to 5.8x) and regressions on others due to the overhead of compilation decompositions. Code and benchmarks are available at: https://github.com/sohaibafifi/pycsp3-scheduling

### [121] [Holistic Evaluation and Failure Diagnosis of AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14865)
*Netta Madvil,Gilad Dym,Alon Mecilati,Edo Dekel,Jonatan Liberman,Rotem Brazilay,Liron Schliesser,Max Svidlo,Shai Nir,Orel Shalom,Yaron Friedman,David Connack,Amos Rimon,Philip Tannor,Shir Chorev*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods for evaluating AI agents are limited: outcome-based metrics only measure success vs. failure without explaining why, and process-level evaluations cannot reliably map different failure types to specific points in long, structured decision traces. This makes it difficult to diagnose and improve agent behavior.

Method: The authors design a holistic evaluation framework that decomposes an agent's execution trace into independent spans. Each span is evaluated separately in a bottom-up fashion, and these span-level assessments are combined with a top-down agent-level diagnosis. The framework produces span-level rationales for each verdict and is designed to scale to arbitrarily long traces. They test it on the TRAIL benchmark using GAIA and SWE-Bench tasks.

Result: On TRAIL, the proposed framework achieves state-of-the-art results on all evaluation metrics for both GAIA and SWE-Bench. It yields up to 38% relative gains in category F1, improves localization accuracy by up to 3.5x, and boosts joint localization-categorization accuracy by up to 12.5x compared to the strongest prior baselines. The framework also outperforms others in more individual error categories.

Conclusion: The main limitation in current agent evaluation is methodological rather than model capability. By decomposing evaluation into span-level judgments with rationales and pairing them with holistic diagnosis, the framework substantially improves error localization and categorization. Even frontier models perform much better as evaluators when embedded in this framework than when used as monolithic judges over entire traces.

Abstract: AI agents execute complex multi-step processes, but current evaluation falls short: outcome metrics report success or failure without explaining why, and process-level approaches struggle to connect failure types to their precise locations within long, structured traces. We present a holistic agent evaluation framework that pairs top-down agent-level diagnosis with bottom-up span-level evaluation, decomposing analysis into independent per-span assessments. This decomposition scales to traces of arbitrary length and produces span-level rationales for each verdict. On the TRAIL benchmark, our framework achieves state-of-the-art results across all metrics on both GAIA and SWE-Bench, with relative gains over the strongest prior baselines of up to 38% on category F1, up to 3.5x on localization accuracy, and up to 12.5x on joint localization-categorization accuracy. Per-category analysis shows our framework leading in more error categories than any other evaluator. Notably, the same frontier model achieves several times higher localization accuracy when used inside our framework than as a monolithic judge over the full trace, showing that evaluation methodology, not model capability, is the bottleneck.

### [122] [SliceGraph: Mapping Process Isomers in Multi-Run Chain-of-Thought Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14619)
*Kang Chen,Junjie Nian,Yixin Cao,Yugang Jiang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SliceGraph to analyze the internal structure of multi-run chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning, revealing multiple distinct reasoning route families (process isomers) that lead to the same correct answer, which are not captured by standard final-answer aggregation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-run CoT analyses typically collapse all trajectories to final answers, losing information about how sampled reasoning paths overlap, diverge, and recombine over the course of inference. The authors want a way to measure and characterize this underlying "process geometry"—the structure of intermediate reasoning states and routes—without constraining or changing the model’s decoding behavior.

Method: They construct a post-hoc graph structure called SliceGraph: nodes are CoT "slices" (segments of intermediate reasoning) and edges connect slices that are mutually close in a sparse activation-key Jaccard similarity space via mutual-kNN. This graph is used as a measurement tool to identify biconnected components (interpreted as shared reasoning states) and higher-level "process families" (clusters of trajectories that follow similar routes). They add a label-seeded reward field over the graph to study success-associated value landscapes, and perform typed-state transition analysis to compare how different process families move through the same state space. They also run representation ablations, cross-architecture, and cross-scale replications to test robustness.

Result: Across ensembles of CoT samples from several 4B/8B models on math and science benchmarks, they find that correct trajectories leading to the same normalized answer often belong to distinct process families. In 85.5% of the 954 problem-model cells, same-answer correct CoTs split into multiple families; in cells with at least two correct runs, on average 76.6% of run pairs are from different families. The label-seeded reward field shows multiple disconnected high-value cores instead of a single contiguous success region, and process families specialize over different such cores. Transition analysis reveals that families traverse the same atlas of states but with distinct transition kernels. Replication and ablation experiments show the route-family structure is robust.

Conclusion: Final-answer aggregation hides rich, structured diversity in reasoning processes: models solve the same problem via multiple distinct, coherent route families (process isomers) that occupy different regions of a shared process geometry. SliceGraph provides a robust measurement framework to reveal these structures, value landscapes, and transition dynamics without altering model decoding, suggesting that future evaluation and training methods should account for process-level diversity rather than only endpoint correctness.

Abstract: Multi-run chain-of-thought reasoning is usually collapsed to final-answer aggregates, which discard howsampled trajectories share, split, and rejoin through intermediate computation. We propose SliceGraph, a post-hoc problem-model-cell graph built by mutual-kNN over sparse activation-key Jaccard similarity between CoT slices, and treat it as a measurement object for process geometry rather than as a decoding program. Across sampled CoT ensembles from three primary 4B/8B models on math and science benchmarks, blinded annotation supports SliceGraph biconnected components as shared reasoning-state units and process families as within-family strategy-coherent route units. In 85.5% of 954 problem-model cells, correct CoTs sharing the same normalized answer split into multiple process families; among cells with at least two such runs, 76.6% of run pairs are cross-family on average. We call such same-answer, family-divergent correct trajectories process isomers. A label-seeded reward field provides a separate value-landscape layer: success-associated regions often split into disconnected high-value cores, and route families specialize over these core footprints rather than merely duplicating one another. A typed-state transition analysis further shows that process families navigate the same atlas with distinct transition kernels under matched null controls. Representation ablations, a cross-architecture replication, and two cross-scale replications support the robustness of the route-family scaffold, showing that final-answer aggregation overlooks this structured multi-route process geometry.

</details>

*Loris Belcastro,Francesco Gervino,Fabrizio Marozzo,Domenico Talia,Paolo Trunfio*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents an explainable framework that builds temporal trajectories from users’ timestamped digital traces to detect and summarize depression-related status shifts over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

These signals are aggregated over time to construct user-level temporal trajectories. Change-point detection and temporal segmentation are then applied to these trajectories to identify significant status shifts (e.g., improvement or deterioration). To improve interpretability, a large language model is used on top of the segmented trajectories to generate concise narrative reports describing the evolution of the user’s mental health signals and highlighting key transitions. The framework is evaluated on two social media datasets and includes an ablation study to assess the contribution of each component, especially temporal modeling and segmentation.

Result: On two social media datasets, the framework generates summaries that are more coherent and informative than those produced by directly prompting a large language model on the raw history. Specifically, it achieves higher coverage of each user’s posting history, better temporal coherence in the narrative, and greater sensitivity in detecting and describing change points in mental-health-related signals. The ablation study demonstrates that temporal modeling and trajectory segmentation substantially contribute to these improvements.

Conclusion: The proposed framework effectively transforms timestamped digital traces into interpretable temporal trajectories of mental-health-related signals, enabling detection and explanation of depression-related status shifts over time. By combining specialized BERT-based predictors with trajectory modeling and LLM-generated reports, the method offers a transparent and structured view of mental health signal evolution that can support research and decision-making, while explicitly not serving as a clinical diagnostic tool.

Abstract: Every day, users generate digital traces (e.g., social media posts, chats, and online interactions) that are inherently timestamped and may reflect aspects of their mental state. These traces can be organized into temporal trajectories that capture how a user's mental health signals evolve, including phases of improvement, deterioration, or stability. In this work, we propose an explainable framework for detecting and analyzing depression-related status shifts in user digital traces. The approach combines multiple BERT-based models to extract complementary signals across different dimensions (e.g., sentiment, emotion, and depression severity). Such signals are then aggregated over time to construct user-level trajectories that are analyzed to identify meaningful change points. To enhance interpretability, the framework integrates a large language model to generate concise and human-readable reports that describe the evolution of mental-health signals and highlight key transitions. We evaluate the framework on two social media datasets. Results show that the approach produces more coherent and informative summaries than direct LLM-based reporting, achieving higher coverage of user history, stronger temporal coherence, and improved sensitivity to change points. An ablation study confirms the contribution of each component, particularly temporal modeling and segmentation. Overall, the method provides an interpretable view of mental health signals over time, supporting research and decision making without aiming at clinical diagnosis.

### [124] [Teaching Large Language Models When Not to Know: Learning Temporal Critique for Ex-Ante Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14636)
*Chenlu Ding,Jiancan Wu,Yanchen Luo,Zheyuan Liu,Yancheng Yuan,Xiang Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies why large language models fail to respect temporal cutoffs and introduces a training framework, TCFT, that teaches models to detect and avoid using post-cutoff information, significantly reducing temporal leakage.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are frequently used in settings that require answering from the perspective of an earlier date (e.g., backtesting agents, historical simulations, or time-bounded benchmarks). However, they often leak knowledge from after the specified cutoff, failing to truly reason ex-ante. Existing prompting strategies and standard supervised fine-tuning do not reliably prevent this, motivating a systematic study of the problem and a specialized training method for temporal verification.

Method: The authors first perform a systematic evaluation of prompt-level interventions, comparing explicit cutoff statements vs. implicit historical framings and analyzing how the placement of cutoff instructions (as prefixes vs. suffixes) affects temporal leakage. They then argue conceptually that ex-ante correctness is relational (answer + cutoff) and cannot be captured by ordinary SFT on answers alone. To address this, they introduce TCFT (Temporal Critique Fine-Tuning), which trains a model, given a query, an explicit temporal cutoff, and a candidate answer, to (1) detect whether the answer uses post-cutoff information, (2) explain where temporal boundaries are violated, and (3) judge overall temporal admissibility. They apply TCFT to Qwen2.5-7B/14B-Instruct models.

Result: The analysis shows that temporal leakage is highly sensitive to how the cutoff is expressed and where it appears in the prompt: explicit cutoff instructions are more effective than merely framing the question historically, and placing constraints as a prefix reduces leakage better than suffix constraints. However, prompting alone cannot ensure temporally admissible reasoning. With TCFT, the modified Qwen2.5 models substantially reduce temporal leakage compared to both prompting-only baselines and models trained with standard SFT, achieving average leakage reductions of about 41.89 percentage points for the 7B model and 37.79 points for the 14B model.

Conclusion: Prompting can nudge LLMs into a desired temporal frame but does not provide them with genuine temporal verification abilities. Because ex-ante correctness depends on the relationship between an answer and a temporal cutoff, simple supervised fine-tuning on answers is insufficient. The proposed TCFT framework, which explicitly trains models to critique answers relative to a cutoff, enables more reliable detection and avoidance of post-cutoff information, significantly mitigating temporal leakage and offering a more principled path toward cutoff-aware reasoning in LLMs.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) often fail to reason under temporal cutoffs: when prompted to answer from the standpoint of an earlier time, they exploit knowledge that became available only later. We study this failure through the lens of ex-ante reasoning, where a model must rely exclusively on information knowable before a cutoff. Through a systematic analysis of prompt-level interventions, we find that temporal leakage is highly sensitive to cutoff formulation and instruction placement: explicit cutoff statements outperform implicit historical framings, and prefix constraints reduce leakage more effectively than suffix constraints. These findings indicate that prompting can steer models into a temporal frame, but does not endow them with the ability to verify whether a response is temporally admissible. We further argue that supervised fine-tuning is insufficient, since ex-ante correctness is not an intrinsic property of an answer, but a relation between the answer and the cutoff. To address this gap, we propose TCFT, a Temporal Critique Fine-Tuning framework that trains models to acquire cutoff-aware temporal verification. Given a query, a cutoff, and a candidate response, TCFT teaches the model to identify post-cutoff leakage, explain temporal boundary violations, and judge temporal admissibility. Experiments with Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct and Qwen2.5-14B-Instruct show that TCFT consistently outperforms prompting and SFT baselines, reducing average leakage by 41.89 and 37.79 percentage points, respectively.

However, they often rely on subjective or limited evaluation methods; focus primarily on proprietary models; or rarely systematically examine generation, evaluation, or deployment constraints in real educational settings. Meanwhile, Small Language Models (SLMs) have emerged as local alternatives that better address privacy and resource limitations; yet their effectiveness for assessment tasks remains underexplored. To address this gap, we systematically compare LLMs and SLMs for assessment question design; evaluate generation quality across Bloom's taxonomy levels using reproducible, pedagogically grounded metrics; and further assess model-based judging against expert-informed evaluation by analyzing reliability and agreement patterns. Results show that SLMs achieve competitive performance across key pedagogically motivated quality dimensions while enabling local, privacy-sensitive deployment. However, model-based evaluations also exhibit systematic inconsistencies and bias relative to expert ratings. These findings provide evidence to posit language models as bounded assistants in assessment workflows; underscore the necessity of Human-in-the-Loop; and advance the automated educational question generation field by examining quality, reliability, and deployment-aware trade-offs.

*Baolin Peng,Wenlin Yao,Qianhui Wu,Hao Cheng,Xiao Yu,Rui Yang,Tao Ge,Alessandrio Sordoni,Xingdi Yuan,Yelong Shen,Pengcheng He,Tong Zhang,Zhou Yu,Jianfeng Gao*

TL;DR: The paper introduces Orchard, an open-source, scalable framework for training and evaluating autonomous LLM-based agents across coding, GUI-computer-use, and personal-assistant domains, achieving state-of-the-art or leading open-source results with relatively modest data and compute.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They build Orchard Env, a lightweight environment service that standardizes sandbox lifecycle management, agent harnesses, and pipeline stages across task domains. Using this common layer, they design three domain-specific "recipes": (1) Orchard-SWE for software engineering agents, using 107K distilled trajectories from strong proprietary models, a credit-assignment SFT approach that focuses training on productive segments of partially successful trajectories, and Balanced Adaptive Rollout RL on top of a Qwen3-30B thinking model; (2) Orchard-GUI, a 4B vision-language agent for computer use trained from just 0.4K distilled trajectories plus 2.2K open-ended tasks; and (3) Orchard-Claw, a personal assistant agent trained on 0.2K synthetic tasks and evaluated with different harnesses such as ZeroClaw. The methodology emphasizes harness-agnostic design, reusability of data, and scalable evaluation.

Result: Orchard-SWE, starting from Qwen3-30B-A3B-Thinking, reaches 64.3% on SWE-bench Verified after SFT and 67.5% after SFT+RL, setting a new state of the art among open-source models of similar size. Orchard-GUI, a compact 4B VLM agent, achieves success rates of 74.1% on WebVoyager, 67.0% on Online-Mind2Web, and 64.0% on DeepShop, outperforming other open-source systems and approaching proprietary ones. Orchard-Claw attains 59.6% pass@3 on Claw-Eval and 73.9% when combined with the stronger ZeroClaw harness, despite being trained on only 0.2K synthetic tasks.

Conclusion: A lightweight, open, and harness-agnostic environment abstraction (Orchard Env) can effectively unify agentic data collection, training, and evaluation across domains, enabling strong, scalable autonomous agents in coding, GUI interaction, and personal assistance with relatively little data. This framework lowers barriers to reproducible, high-performance agentic modeling in the open-source ecosystem and demonstrates that careful environment design plus targeted training recipes can substitute for heavy proprietary infrastructure.

Abstract: Agentic modeling aims to transform LLMs into autonomous agents capable of solving complex tasks through planning, reasoning, tool use, and multi-turn interaction with environments. Despite major investment, open research remains constrained by infrastructure and training gaps. Many high-performing systems rely on proprietary codebases, models, or services, while most open-source frameworks focus on orchestration and evaluation rather than scalable agent training. We present Orchard, an open-source framework for scalable agentic modeling. At its core is Orchard Env, a lightweight environment service providing reusable primitives for sandbox lifecycle management across task domains, agent harnesses, and pipeline stages. On top of Orchard Env, we build three agentic modeling recipes. Orchard-SWE targets coding agents. We distill 107K trajectories from MiniMax-M2.5 and Qwen3.5-397B, introduce credit-assignment SFT to learn from productive segments of unresolved trajectories, and apply Balanced Adaptive Rollout for RL. Starting from Qwen3-30B-A3B-Thinking, Orchard-SWE achieves 64.3% on SWE-bench Verified after SFT and 67.5% after SFT+RL, setting a new state of the art among open-source models of comparable size. Orchard-GUI trains a 4B vision-language computer-use agent using only 0.4K distilled trajectories and 2.2K open-ended tasks. It achieves 74.1%, 67.0%, and 64.0% success rates on WebVoyager, Online-Mind2Web, and DeepShop, respectively, making it the strongest open-source model while remaining competitive with proprietary systems. Orchard-Claw targets personal assistant agents. Trained with only 0.2K synthetic tasks, it achieves 59.6% pass@3 on Claw-Eval and 73.9% when paired with a stronger ZeroClaw harness. Collectively, these results show that a lightweight, open, harness-agnostic environment layer enables reusable agentic data, training recipes, and evaluations across domains.

### [127] [Falkor-IRAC: Graph-Constrained Generation for Verified Legal Reasoning in Indian Judicial AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14665)
*Joy Bose*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Falkor-IRAC, a graph-constrained legal reasoning framework that grounds LLM answers in an IRAC-based knowledge graph and validates them via a verifier agent, aiming to reduce hallucinations and better capture legal reasoning than standard RAG.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Vector-based RAG systems struggle with legal tasks because legal reasoning depends on constrained symbolic processes like precedent propagation, procedural transitions, and statute-bound inference. These aspects are not faithfully modeled by similarity search, leading to hallucinated or outdated citations and incorrect reasoning, which can harm access to justice, especially in high-volume jurisdictions like India. The authors want a system that aligns more closely with how legal reasoning is actually performed and evaluated.

Method: They construct an IRAC (Issue, Rule, Analysis, Conclusion) knowledge graph from Indian Supreme Court and High Court judgments. Each judgment is represented as IRAC nodes enriched with procedural state transitions, precedent links, and statutory references, stored in FalkorDB for efficient traversal. At inference time, an LLM proposes answers, but an additional Verifier Agent checks whether there exists a valid supporting path in the graph. Answers are accepted only if such paths are found; doctrinal conflicts encountered in the graph are surfaced explicitly as outputs. The system is evaluated using graph-native metrics that measure grounding and path validity rather than traditional text-similarity scores.

Result: On a small proof-of-concept dataset of 51 Supreme Court judgments, the Verifier Agent successfully validated genuine citations for completed queries and rejected fabricated (hallucinated) citations. The paper also shows that their proposed graph-native metrics—citation grounding accuracy, path validity rate, hallucinated precedent rate, and conflict detection rate—can be applied to quantify system performance. However, direct empirical comparison with vector-only RAG and performance optimization (e.g., via GPU acceleration) are not yet completed.

Conclusion: Falkor-IRAC demonstrates that constraining LLM generation with an IRAC-based legal knowledge graph and a verifier agent can better align AI outputs with structured legal reasoning, reducing hallucinations and making doctrinal conflicts explicit. The authors argue that legal AI should be evaluated with graph-native reasoning metrics rather than general text similarity scores and outline future work on scaling the corpus, improving inference efficiency, and benchmarking against vector-only RAG baselines.

Abstract: Legal reasoning is not semantic similarity search. A court judgment encodes constrained symbolic reasoning: precedent propagation, procedural state transitions, and statute-bound inference. These are properties that vector-based retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) cannot faithfully represent. Hallucinated precedents, outdated statute citations, and unsupported reasoning chains remain persistent failure modes in LLM-based legal AI, with real consequences for access to justice in high-caseload jurisdictions such as India. This paper presents Falkor-IRAC, a graph-constrained generation framework for Indian legal AI that grounds generation in structured reasoning over an IRAC (Issue, Rule, Analysis, Conclusion) knowledge graph. Judgments from the Supreme Court and High Courts of India are ingested as IRAC node structures enriched with procedural state transitions, precedent relationships, and statutory references, stored in FalkorDB for low-latency agentic traversal. At inference time, LLM-generated answers are accepted only if a valid supporting path can be traced through the graph, a check performed by a falsifiability oracle called the Verifier Agent. The system also detects doctrinal conflicts as a first-class output rather than silently resolving them. Falkor-IRAC is evaluated using graph-native metrics: citation grounding accuracy, path validity rate, hallucinated precedent rate, and conflict detection rate. These metrics are argued to be more appropriate for legal reasoning evaluation than BLEU and ROUGE. On a proof-of-concept corpus of 51 Supreme Court judgments, the Verifier Agent correctly validated citations on completed queries and correctly rejected fabricated citations. Evaluation against vector-only RAG baselines is left for future work, as is GPU-accelerated inference to address current timeout rates on CPU hardware.

### [128] [Case-Based Calibration of Adaptive Reasoning and Execution for LLM Tool Use](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15041)
*Renning Pang,Tian Lan,Leyuan Liu,Piao Tong,Sheng Cao,Xiaosong Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: CAST is a case-driven framework that improves LLM tool-use by learning from historical execution trajectories to choose appropriate reasoning depth and maintain structural validity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing tool-using LLMs struggle to balance deep reasoning (to solve complex tasks) with strict adherence to required output schemas, leading to structural errors or unnecessary long chains of thought. There is a need for a systematic way to leverage past tool-use executions to better calibrate reasoning behavior.

Method: CAST treats historical execution trajectories as structured cases and extracts two kinds of profiles: (1) complexity profiles that indicate how much reasoning is optimal for different types of tasks; and (2) failure profiles that characterize where and how structural breakdowns occur. These profiles are converted into a fine-grained reward design and adaptive reasoning policies, which are then internalized by the LLM through reinforcement learning, enabling it to adjust reasoning depth and structure control per instance.

Result: On BFCLv2 and ToolBench benchmarks, CAST improves schema-faithful tool execution and overall task success, while cutting down unnecessary reasoning steps. It yields up to 5.85 percentage-point gains in execution accuracy and reduces average reasoning length by 26%, with fewer high-impact structural errors.

Conclusion: Historical execution trajectories can be systematically mined as reusable adaptation knowledge. By encoding them into case-based complexity and failure profiles and using these in RL training, LLMs can learn calibrated tool-use strategies that both improve accuracy and reduce reasoning overhead.

Abstract: Tool use extends large language models beyond parametric knowledge, but reliable execution requires balancing appropriate reasoning depth with strict structural validity. We approach this problem from a case-based perspective to present CAST, a case-driven framework that treats historical execution trajectories as structured cases. Instead of reusing raw exemplar outputs, CAST extracts case-derived signals to identify complexity profiles for estimating optimal reasoning strategies, alongside failure profiles to map likely structural breakdowns. The framework translates this knowledge into a fine-grained reward design and adaptive reasoning, enabling the model to autonomously internalize case-based strategies during reinforcement learning. Experiments on BFCLv2 and ToolBench demonstrate that CAST improves both schema-faithful execution and task-level tool-use success while reducing unnecessary deliberation. The approach achieves up to 5.85 percentage points gain in overall execution accuracy and reduces average reasoning length by 26%, significantly mitigating high-impact structural errors. Ultimately, this demonstrates how historical execution cases can provide reusable adaptation knowledge for calibrated tool use.

Conclusion: By explicitly modelling the influence of acquisition parameters through a mixed-effects framework, the authors can quantify scan parameter sensitivity of radiomic AI models and pinpoint clinically feasible parameter ranges that enhance cross-dataset robustness. Their results in CT-based lung cancer diagnosis show that selecting appropriate CT settings can markedly improve generalization performance, suggesting that protocol optimization is as critical as algorithm design for deploying radiomic AI systems in routine clinical practice.

Abstract: A main barrier for the deployment of AI radiomic systems in clinical routine is their drop in performance under heterogeneous multicentre acquisition protocols. This work presents a performance-oriented framework for quantifying scan parameter sensitivity of radiomic AI models, while identifying clinically significant parameter regions associated with improved cross-dataset robustness. We formulate a mixed-effects framework for quantifying the influence that clinically relevant acquisition parameters have on models performance, while accounting for subject-level random effects. We have applied our framework to lung cancer diagnosis in CT scans using two independent multicentre datasets (a public database and own-collected data) and several SoA architectures. To evaluate across-database reproducibility, CT parameters have been adjusted using the data collected and tested on the public set. The optimal configuration selected is the current of the X-ray tube >= 200 mA, spiral pitch <= 1.5, slice thickness <= 1.25 mm, which balances diagnostic quality with low radiation dose. These configuration push metrics from 0.79+-0.04 sensitivity, 0.47+-0.10 specificity in low quality scans to 0.90+-0.10 sensitivity, 0.79 +- 0.13 specificity in high quality ones.

### [131] [On Strong Equivalence Notions in Logic Programming and Abstract Argumentation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14721)
*Giovanni Buraglio,Wolfgang Dvorak,Stefan Woltran*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They study when two knowledge bases remain interchangeable (strongly equivalent) across all contexts, especially under updates, and repair a mismatch between logic programming and argumentation frameworks by defining a new update-aware strong equivalence notion for logic programs that aligns with argumentation-based formalisms.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Strong equivalence is key in nonmonotonic reasoning because it guarantees that replacing one knowledge base with another never changes inference results in any possible extension or context. Logic programs and abstract argumentation frameworks are tightly related and even semantically equivalent in static settings, but their update mechanisms differ, breaking this correspondence in dynamic scenarios. This mismatch means that standard strong equivalence in one formalism may not correctly capture interchangeability in the other once updates occur, which is problematic for modular design, optimisation and cross-formalism translations.

Method: The paper analyses, at a formal level, how updates are defined and applied in logic programming versus abstract argumentation (both Dung-style and claim-augmented frameworks). It studies translations between classes of logic programs and argumentation frameworks and looks at when standard strong equivalence is preserved or fails under these translations in dynamic settings. Based on this analysis, the authors define a new, update-sensitive notion of strong equivalence for logic programs and characterize its properties, proving preservation theorems that show compatibility with argumentation-based notions of strong equivalence under certain syntactic or semantic restrictions.

Result: They show precisely where and why standard strong equivalence for logic programs fails to be preserved when mapped to argumentation frameworks under updates. They then introduce a refined notion of strong equivalence for logic programs that takes updates into account and prove that, for certain classes of logic programs and corresponding Dung-style and claim-augmented argumentation frameworks, this new notion is preserved under translation. This yields conditions under which the inter-formalism correspondence can be safely used in dynamic reasoning settings.

Conclusion: The mismatch between logic programming and abstract argumentation regarding strong equivalence in dynamic contexts stems from differing update notions. By redefining strong equivalence for logic programs in an update-aware way, the authors recover preservation of strong equivalence across translations to certain argumentation formalisms. This restores semantic compatibility between these important nonmonotonic frameworks in dynamic settings and supports safer knowledge base replacement, modularity, and interoperability across formalisms.

Abstract: Strong equivalence between knowledge bases ensures the possibility of replacing one with the other without affecting reasoning outcomes, in any given context. This makes it a crucial property in nonmonotonic formalisms. In particular, the fields of logic programming and abstract argumentation provide primary examples in which this property has been subject to vast investigations. However, while (classes of) logic programs and abstract argumentation frameworks are known to be semantically equivalent in static settings, this alignment breaks in dynamic contexts due to differing notions of update. As a result, strong equivalence does not always carry over from one formalism to the other. In this paper, we carefully investigate this discrepancy and introduce a new notion of strong equivalence for logic programs. Our approach preserves strong equivalence under translation between certain classes of logic programs and both Dung-style and claim-augmented argumentation frameworks, thus restoring compatibility across these formalisms.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

The agent operates via a propose–simulate–refine loop: it proposes actions, queries the world model to simulate outcomes, then refines its prescription. Training is done in three stages: (1) supervised fine-tuning to learn patient dynamics, (2) behavior cloning to imitate the propose–simulate–refine strategy, and (3) reinforcement learning that uses the world model for agentic improvement.

Result: On retrospective sepsis trajectories from the MIMIC-IV ICU dataset, SepsisAgent attains higher off-policy value than traditional reinforcement-learning methods and LLM-based baselines, while also showing the best safety profile, as measured by adherence to clinical guidelines and avoidance of unsafe actions.

Conclusion: Augmenting an LLM with a learned clinical world model and training it with a curriculum that explicitly exploits simulation-based interaction yields a sepsis treatment agent that is both more effective (in off-policy value) and safer than existing RL or LLM approaches. Moreover, the patterns in patient dynamics learned through repeated world-model interaction remain beneficial even when direct simulator access is no longer available.

Abstract: Sepsis management in the ICU requires sequential treatment decisions under rapidly evolving patient physiology. Although large language models (LLMs) encode broad clinical knowledge and can reason over guidelines, they are not inherently grounded in action-conditioned patient dynamics. We introduce SepsisAgent, a world model-augmented LLM agent for sepsis treatment recommendation. SepsisAgent uses a learned Clinical World Model to simulate patient responses under candidate fluid--vasopressor interventions, and follows a propose--simulate--refine workflow before committing to a prescription. We first show that world-model access alone yields inconsistent LLM decision performance, motivating agent-specific training. We then train SepsisAgent through a three-stage curriculum: patient-dynamics supervised fine-tuning, propose--simulate--refine behavior cloning, and world-model-based agentic reinforcement learning. On MIMIC-IV sepsis trajectories, SepsisAgent outperforms all traditional RL and LLM-based baselines in off-policy value while achieving the best safety profile under guideline adherence and unsafe-action metrics. Further analysis shows that repeated interaction with the Clinical World Model enables the agent to learn regularities in patient evolution, which remain useful even when simulator access is removed.

### [133] [XDomainBench: Diagnosing Reasoning Collapse in High-Dimensional Scientific Knowledge Composition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14754)
*Gong Zhiren,Tiantong Wu,Jiaming Zhang,Fuyao Zhang,Che Wang,Yurong Hao,Yikun Hou,Foo Ping,Yilei Zhao,Fei Huang,Chau Yuen,Wei Yang Bryan Lim*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are widely used for scientific knowledge synthesis, but current benchmarks mostly use single-turn, single-domain settings, which do not reflect real-world, multi-step, interdisciplinary scientific workflows. The authors seek a way to systematically probe the limits of LLMs under realistic compositions of domains and interactions.

Method: They design XDomainBench, a diagnostic benchmark with formalized composition orders and mixture structures across 20 scientific domains and 4 task categories. It contains 8,598 multi-turn interactive sessions following 8 realistic trajectory patterns that vary in difficulty and domain-mixture dynamics, mimicking real AI-for-science workflows. They then evaluate various LLMs on this benchmark.

Result: Experiments show that as the composition order (i.e., the number and complexity of cross-domain reasoning steps) increases, LLM performance degrades sharply. The analysis identifies two main contributors: direct difficulty increases from combining domains, and indirect interaction-driven failures where certain trajectory patterns exacerbate error accumulation, cause reasoning breakdowns, and induce confusion between domains, ending in failed sessions.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that current LLMs struggle with compositional, interactive interdisciplinary scientific reasoning, especially as tasks scale in complexity and cross-domain composition. XDomainBench provides a structured way to expose and analyze these weaknesses, highlighting the need for methods and models better suited for complex AI-for-science workflows.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed for knowledge synthesis, yet their capacity for compositional generalization in scientific knowledge remains under-characterized. Existing benchmarks primarily focus on single-turn restricted scenarios, failing to capture the capability boundaries exposed by real-world interactive scientific workflows. To address this, we introduce XDomainBench, a diagnostic benchmark for interactive interdisciplinary scientific reasoning. We formalize the composition order and mixture structure to enable systematic stress-testing from single-discipline to inter-disciplinary, comprising 8,598 interactive sessions across 20 domains and 4 task categories, with 8 realistic trajectory patterns covering difficulty and domain-mixture dynamics, simulating real AI4S scenarios. Large-scale evaluation of LLMs reveals a systematic reasoning collapse as composition order increases, stemming from two root causes: (i) direct difficulty increases induced by domain composition, and (ii) indirect interaction-amplified failures where trajectory patterns trigger error accumulation, reasoning breaks, and domain confusion, ultimately leading to session collapse.

### [134] [Probabilistic Verification of Recurrent Neural Networks for Single and Multi-Agent Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14758)
*Luca Marzari,Enrico Marchesini*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce RNN-ProVe, a probabilistic verification framework that estimates how likely RNN-based RL policies are to violate safety/behavioral specifications under partial observability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Verifying RNN policies in partially observable reinforcement learning is hard because behaviors depend on latent hidden states. Existing RNN verification tools either make strong modeling assumptions or use coarse over-approximations of hidden state spaces, which leads to conservative or inconclusive safety guarantees. There is a need for a scalable method that gives informative, quantitative guarantees on how often an RNN policy misbehaves.

Method: RNN-ProVe is a probabilistic verification framework. It uses policy-driven sampling to approximate the subset of RNN hidden states that are actually reachable under a trained policy, rather than over-approximating the full hidden state space. Based on these samples, it statistically estimates the probability of undesired behaviors and derives finite-sample, bounded-error, high-confidence bounds on violation probabilities. The framework is applied to both single-agent and cooperative multi-agent partially observable tasks.

Result: On benchmark partially observable tasks (single-agent and multi-agent), RNN-ProVe provides tighter, more quantitative, and feasibility-aware probabilistic guarantees on undesired behaviors compared to existing RNN verification tools. It scales better to recurrent and multi-agent settings while maintaining statistical guarantees.

Conclusion: By focusing on reachable hidden states and using statistical estimation with error bounds, RNN-ProVe offers a practical and scalable way to probabilistically verify RNN-based RL policies in partially observable settings, yielding more informative guarantees than prior methods.

Abstract: History-dependent policies induced by recurrent neural networks (RNNs) rely on latent hidden state dynamics, making verification in partially observable reinforcement learning (RL) challenging. Existing RNN verification tools typically rely on restrictive modeling assumptions or coarse over-approximations of the hidden state space, which can lead to overly conservative or inconclusive results. We propose $\textbf{RNN}$ $\textbf{Pro}$babilistic $\textbf{Ve}$rification ($\texttt{RNN-ProVe}$), a probabilistic framework that $\textit{estimates the likelihood}$ of undesired behaviors in RNN-based policies. $\texttt{RNN-ProVe}$ uses policy-driven sampling to approximate the set of hidden states that are feasible under a trained policy, and derives statistical error bounds to produce bounded-error, high-confidence estimates of behavioral violations. Experiments on partially observable single-agent and cooperative multi-agent tasks show that $\texttt{RNN-ProVe}$ yields more quantitative, feasibility-aware probabilistic guarantees than existing tools, while scaling to recurrent and multi-agent settings.

Meanwhile, human predictors show the largest errors, presumably because their judgments are biased by their own aesthetic preferences and cannot fully align with the target’s tastes.

To address this issue, we focus on the utility of Large Language Models (LLMs) pretrained on vast amounts of textual data, and develop an integrated DL-LLM system. The system actively elicits aesthetic preferences through LLM-based semi-structured interviews and predicts aesthetic evaluation by leveraging both low-level and high-level features. In our experiments, we compare the proposed system against conventional systems, human predictors, and the target individual's own re-evaluations after a certain time interval. Our results show that the proposed system outperforms all of them, with particularly strong performance on highly-rated images. Moreover, the prediction error of the proposed system is smaller than within-person variability, while human predictors show the largest error, likely due to the influence of their own aesthetic values. These results suggest that AI may be better positioned than others or one's future self to capture individual aesthetic preferences at a given point. This opens a new question of whether AI could serve as a deeper interpreter of human aesthetic sensibility than humans themselves.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing solutions focusing on model weights or long context are insufficient, so a governable, auditable framework for continuity is needed.

Method: ARPM separates static knowledge memory from dynamic dialogue experience, using a combination of vector search, BM25, RRF fusion, dual-temporal reranking, chronological evidence reading, and a controlled analysis protocol for verifying evidence and binding answers. The authors run experiments using engineering logs in multi-round QA, ablations, and stress tests with heavy noise, periodic context clearing, and cross-model handoffs.

Result: 1) In 50-round QA under different signal-to-noise ratios, manual evaluation shows higher recall than automatic CSV judgments, indicating auto rules underestimate recall once evidence is in the prompt. 2) Ablation shows dialogue-history retrieval and BM25 are crucial for recent continuity; turning them off drops strict accuracy from 100% to 66.7% and 80%. 3) Under extreme noise, context clearing, and multi-model handoff, ARPM still preserves semantic and persona continuity but is limited by protocol compliance weaknesses.

To address these issues, we introduce ARPM, an external temporal memory governance framework for long-term dialogue. ARPM separates static knowledge memory from dynamic dialogue experience memory and combines vector retrieval, BM25, RRF fusion, dual-temporal reranking, chronological evidence reading, and a controlled analysis protocol for evidence verification and answer binding. Unlike approaches that encode persona consistency into model weights or rely only on long context, ARPM treats continuity as a traceable, auditable, and transferable governance problem. Using engineering logs, we conduct three experiments. First, in a 50-round question-answering setting, we compare signal-to-noise ratios of 1:5 and 1:200+, and distinguish CSV auto-judgment from manual review. Under 1:5, CSV recall accuracy is 54.0%, while manual review raises it to 100.0%. Under 1:200+, the values are 44.0% and 80.0%. These results show that automatic rules can underestimate recall after supporting evidence enters the prompt. Second, ablation results show that dialogue history retrieval is necessary for recent continuity: disabling it reduces strict accuracy from 100% to 66.7%, and disabling BM25 reduces it to 80.0%, indicating that pure semantic retrieval is insufficient for correction and tracing. Third, under a 5.1-million-character noise substrate, periodic context clearing, and multi-model handoff, ARPM maintains semantic continuity, boundary continuity, and persona consistency, while exposing limits caused by weak protocol compliance. These findings show that long-term persona consistency can be decomposed into governable components and evaluated in a white-box manner.

### [139] [Interestingness as an Inductive Heuristic for Future Compression Progress](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14831)
*Vincent Herrmann,Jürgen Schmidhuber*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to formalize and predict "interesting" tasks—those that promise future learning or compression gains—using ideas from Kolmogorov Complexity and Algorithmic Statistics, and shows both theoretically and empirically that recent breakthroughs are strong predictors of further progress.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Recursively self-improving systems need a way to choose which tasks or data to focus on so that they continue to improve efficiently. This requires a principled notion of "interestingness"—the capacity to identify tasks where working on them is likely to lead to future discoveries or compression progress. The paper aims to formalize this notion and test whether it is theoretically sound and practically predictable.

Method: The authors define interestingness as an inductive heuristic for future compression progress and analyze it using tools from Kolmogorov Complexity and Algorithmic Statistics. They study complexity-runtime profiles of tasks under three different priors: Length Prior, Algorithmic Prior, and Speed Prior. They derive theoretical results about how observed past breakthroughs inform expectations about future progress, and then run experiments across three different universal computational paradigms to empirically validate the theory.

Result: They show theoretically that interestingness has an inductive structure: past compression or discovery progress can validly inform expectations of future progress. Specifically, the expected future progress depends exponentially on how recently the last breakthrough occurred. They also find that the Algorithmic Prior predicts much more future discovery than the Length Prior, giving a quadratic increase in expected progress for the same observed complexity-runtime profile. These predictions are then borne out in experiments using three distinct universal computational frameworks.

Conclusion: Interestingness, defined as anticipated future compression progress, can be treated as a valid inductive heuristic: recent breakthroughs are a strong signal of further forthcoming progress. Among different priors, the Algorithmic Prior is substantially more optimistic than the Length Prior, leading to much higher expected gains given the same evidence. The combination of theoretical analysis and empirical tests suggests that recursively self-improving systems can, in principle, use such a measure of interestingness to guide their search for tasks and data that drive continued improvement.

Abstract: One of the bottlenecks on the way towards recursively self-improving systems is the challenge of interestingness: the ability to prospectively identify which tasks or data hold the potential for future progress. We formalize interestingness as an inductive heuristic for future compression progress and investigate its predictability using tools from Kolmogorov Complexity and Algorithmic Statistics. By analyzing complexity-runtime profiles under Length, Algorithmic, and Speed priors, we demonstrate that the inductive property of interestingness -- the capacity for past progress to signal future discovery -- is theoretically viable and empirically supported. We prove that expected future progress depends exponentially on the recency of the last observed breakthrough. Furthermore, we show that the Algorithmic Prior is significantly more optimistic than the Length Prior, yielding a quadratic increase in expected discovery for the same observed profile. These findings are experimentally confirmed across three diverse universal computational paradigms.
