### [141] [A Deterministic Agentic Workflow for HS Tariff Classification: Multi-Dimensional Rule Reasoning with Interpretable Decisions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14857)
*Yu Zhang,Dongjiang Zhuang,Qu Zhou,Zheng Huang,Junhe Wu,Jing Cao,Kai Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper builds a deterministic, interpretable LLM-based workflow for HS tariff code classification that handles complex multi-axis trade rules better than end-to-end prompting, achieving strong accuracy and revealing possible label issues in the benchmark.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: HS tariff classification requires mapping product descriptions to precise HS codes under complex, intersecting legal rules (materials, form, function, essential character, etc.). Existing end-to-end LLM prompting often fails by focusing on one reasoning axis while ignoring others and the priority constraints defined by the General Interpretive Rules and legal notes. The authors are motivated to design a system that reliably enforces these multi-dimensional rules, is auditable, and performs well in a real, high-stakes application.

Method: They design a deterministic agentic workflow with a fixed control flow and an online six-stage pipeline that constrains LLM usage to narrow, well-defined subtasks. Each stage produces structured outputs and cites relevant section/chapter notes, maintaining local reflection and verification. The system combines offline knowledge engineering of the Chinese HS tariff (encoding rules and notes) with this staged pipeline. They test this architecture using Qwen3.6-plus and an open-weight Qwen3.6-27B-FP8 (non-thinking mode) as backbones.

Result: On the HSCodeComp dataset, the workflow achieves 75.0% top-1 and 91.5% top-3 accuracy at the four-digit level, and 64.2% top-1 and 78.3% top-3 at the six-digit level using Qwen3.6-plus. With the open-weight Qwen3.6-27B-FP8 backbone, the pipeline reaches 84.2% top-1 at four digits and 77.4% top-1 at six digits, closely matching the frontier model. A manual audit of 226 six-digit disagreements indicates that many benchmark labels might be inconsistent with HS general rules.

Conclusion: A fixed, deterministic multi-stage workflow that tightly constrains LLM calls can handle the multi-dimensional legal reasoning required for HS tariff classification better than naive end-to-end prompting, while also providing interpretability through structured, cited decisions. The system achieves strong empirical performance and reveals that existing benchmarks (like HSCodeComp) may contain labeling errors relative to official HS rules, motivating future work on dataset quality and broader deployment of similar architectures in other rule-intensive domains.

Abstract: Harmonized System (HS) tariff classification is a high-stakes, expert-level task in which a free-form product description must be mapped to a specific six- or eight-digit code under the General Interpretive Rules (GIR), section notes, chapter notes, and Explanatory Notes. The difficulty lies not in knowledge volume but in *multi-dimensional rule reasoning*: a correct classification must satisfy competing priority rules along several axes simultaneously, including material, form, function, essential character, the part-versus-whole boundary, and specific listing versus residual headings. End-to-end prompting of large language models fails characteristically by resolving one axis while ignoring the priority constraints on the others. We present a *deterministic agentic workflow* in contrast to self-planning agents: the control flow is fixed, language model calls are confined to narrow stages, and reflection and verification are retained as local mechanisms. This design yields interpretability by construction--each decision is decomposed into stage-wise structured outputs with verbatim citation of the chapter or section notes that bear on it. The architecture combines offline knowledge-engineering of the Chinese HS tariff with an online six-stage pipeline. Evaluated on HSCodeComp at the six-digit level, the workflow reaches 75.0% top-1 and 91.5% top-3 at four digits, and 64.2% top-1 and 78.3% top-3 at six digits with Qwen3.6-plus; an open-weight Qwen3.6-27B-FP8 backbone in non-thinking mode achieves 84.2% four-digit and 77.4% six-digit top-1 agreement with the frontier model. A two-stage manual audit of 226 six-digit disagreements suggests that a non-trivial fraction of HSCodeComp ground-truth labels may deviate from HS general rules; full adjudication records are released in the appendix as preliminary findings for community review.

</details>


### [142] [BiFedKD: Bidirectional Federated Knowledge Distillation Framework for Non-IID and Long-Tailed ECG Monitoring](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14886)
*Zixuan Shu,Tiancheng Cao,Hen-Wei Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes BiFedKD, a bidirectional federated knowledge distillation framework to improve ECG classification in IoMT under bandwidth limits and non-IID, long-tailed data, achieving better accuracy/F1 with substantially lower communication and computation costs than baseline FL/FD methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: ECG monitoring in IoMT requires privacy-preserving collaborative learning. Standard federated learning suffers from high communication overhead due to large model updates, while existing federated distillation degrades with non-IID and long-tailed label distributions that are common in ECG data. There is a need for a communication-efficient, privacy-preserving training framework that remains robust under these realistic data challenges.

Method: The authors design BiFedKD, a bidirectional federated knowledge distillation framework. Instead of exchanging full model parameters, clients and server exchange logit-based knowledge. A key component is an aggregation-by-distillation pipeline with temperature scaling, which aggregates client knowledge into a stable global distillation signal for cross-client alignment, mitigating non-IID and long-tailed distribution issues. This global signal guides client model updates while keeping raw ECG data local and reducing communication payload.

Result: On the MIT-BIH Arrhythmia ECG dataset, BiFedKD improves classification performance compared to a baseline method, increasing accuracy by 3.52% and Macro-F1 by 9.93%. For a given target Macro-F1, BiFedKD also reduces communication overhead by 40% and computation cost by 71.7% relative to the baseline.

Conclusion: BiFedKD effectively addresses the communication and statistical challenges of federated ECG monitoring in IoMT by using bidirectional knowledge distillation with aggregation-by-distillation and temperature scaling. It achieves better predictive performance while significantly reducing communication and computation costs, making it a promising solution for practical, privacy-preserving ECG analysis in bandwidth-constrained IoMT environments.

Abstract: Electrocardiogram (ECG) monitoring in Internet of Medical Things (IoMT) networks is constrained by strict data-sharing regulations and privacy concerns. Federated learning (FL) enables collaborative learning by keeping raw ECG data on devices, but frequent transmissions of high-dimensional model updates incur heavy per-round traffic over bandwidth-limited links. To alleviate this bottleneck, federated distillation (FD) replaces parameter exchange with logit-based knowledge transfer. However, the performance of FD often degrades under the non-independent and identically distributed (non-IID) and long-tailed label distributions in ECG deployments. To address these challenges, we propose a bidirectional federated knowledge distillation (BiFedKD) framework that employs an aggregation-by-distillation pipeline with temperature scaling to produce a stable global distillation signal for cross-client alignment. Experiments on the MIT-BIH Arrhythmia dataset show that BiFedKD improves accuracy and Macro-F1 over the baseline by $3.52\%$ and $9.93\%$, respectively. Moreover, to reach the same Macro-F1, BiFedKD reduces communication overhead by $40\%$ and computation cost by $71.7\%$ compared with the baseline.

### [143] [Beyond Individual Intelligence: Surveying Collaboration, Failure Attribution, and Self-Evolution in LLM-based Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14892)
*Shihao Qi,Jie Ma,Rui Xing,Wei Guo,Xiao Huang,Zhitao Gao,Jianhao Deng,Jun Liu,Lingling Zhang,Bifan Wei,Boqian Yang,Pinghui Wang,Jianwen Sun,Jing Tao,Yaqiang Wu,Hui Liu,Yu Yao,Tongliang Liu*

TL;DR: Survey proposes LIFE progression framework for LLM-based multi-agent systems: Lay capabilities, Integrate collaboration, Find faults, Evolve via self-improvement.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based autonomous agents are powerful but struggle with tasks needing long-term, tightly coordinated collaboration. Multi-agent systems help via specialization and structured interaction, but create a new risk: errors propagate and compound across agents and rounds, making failures hard to diagnose and improve from. Existing surveys treat capabilities, collaboration, and self-evolution separately and ignore their causal links. The paper aims to fill this gap.

Method: Conceptual and survey-based: the authors review and organize prior work on LLM-based agents, multi-agent collaboration, attribution, and self-improvement into a four-stage LIFE progression. For each stage they construct systematic taxonomies and formally analyze how each stage depends on and constrains the next. They also study stage boundaries to surface open challenges and research directions for closed-loop multi-agent systems.

Result: A unified framework (LIFE progression) covering four linked stages: (1) Lay the capability foundation: core agent abilities and tools; (2) Integrate agents through collaboration: architectures and protocols for multi-agent coordination; (3) Find faults through attribution: methods to locate and explain errors in multi-agent workflows; (4) Evolve through autonomous self-improvement: mechanisms for agents and systems to adapt, reorganize, and refine over time. The paper maps existing work into these taxonomies, clarifies causal dependencies among stages, and highlights structural gaps, especially at transitions between stages.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that progress in autonomous multi-agent intelligence requires treating capabilities, collaboration, fault attribution, and self-improvement as a causally coupled loop rather than isolated topics. Their LIFE framework reveals how limitations in earlier stages cap what later stages can achieve and how better fault attribution and evolution mechanisms can feed back to reshape capabilities and collaboration structures. They argue for a cross-stage, closed-loop research agenda focused on systems that can continuously diagnose failures, reorganize roles and structures, and refine behaviors, moving toward self-organizing collective intelligence.

Abstract: LLM-based autonomous agents have demonstrated strong capabilities in reasoning, planning, and tool use, yet remain limited when tasks require sustained coordination across roles, tools, and environments. Multi-agent systems address this through structured collaboration among specialized agents, but tighter coordination also amplifies a less explored risk: errors can propagate across agents and interaction rounds, producing failures that are difficult to diagnose and rarely translate into structural self-improvement. Existing surveys cover individual agent capabilities, multi-agent collaboration, or agent self-evolution separately, leaving the causal dependencies among them unexamined. This survey provides a unified review organized around four causally linked stages, which we term the LIFE progression: Lay the capability foundation, Integrate agents through collaboration, Find faults through attribution, and Evolve through autonomous self-improvement. For each stage, we provide systematic taxonomies and formally characterize the dependencies between adjacent stages, revealing how each stage both depends on and constrains the next. Beyond synthesizing existing work, we identify open challenges at stage boundaries and propose a cross-stage research agenda for closed-loop multi-agent systems capable of continuously diagnosing failures, reorganizing structures, and refining agent behaviors, extending current coordination frameworks toward more self-organizing forms of collective intelligence. By bridging these previously fragmented research threads, this survey aims to offer both a systematic reference and a conceptual roadmap toward autonomous, self-improving multi-agent intelligence.

### [144] [COREKG: Coreset-Guided Personalized Summarization of Knowledge Graphs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14900)
*Sohel Aman Khan,Raghava Mutharaju,Supratim Shit*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes COREKG, a method to build small, personalized summaries of large knowledge graphs using coreset-based importance sampling, achieving good query accuracy while greatly reducing graph size.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large knowledge graphs are powerful but often too big and unwieldy for efficient question answering, visualization, and storage. Users typically only need data relevant to their own query patterns, so a one-size-fits-all summary wastes space and computation. There is a need for personalized KG summarization that keeps only user-relevant triples while still approximating the behavior of the full KG for that user’s queries, with theoretical guarantees on the approximation quality.

Method: The authors adapt coreset theory to knowledge graph summarization. For a given KG and a user-specific query workload, they define sensitivity scores for each triple, measuring its importance relative to that user’s queries. Using these scores, they perform sensitivity-based importance sampling to select a small subset (coreset) of triples. The selection algorithm is designed so that the resulting subset approximates the full KG for the given workload with a bounded approximation error, yielding a personalized summary per user constructed independently based on their query behavior.

Result: On three real-world KGs—Freebase, WikiData, and DBpedia—the proposed method COREKG is empirically evaluated. It produces very small personalized summaries (a tiny fraction of the original graph size) while delivering higher query-answering accuracy and better structural coverage compared to several state-of-the-art summarization and graph sampling methods, including GLIMPSE, PPR, iSummary, PEGASUS, and APEX^2.

Conclusion: COREKG demonstrates that coreset-based, sensitivity-driven sampling can effectively create compact, personalized knowledge graph summaries that preserve query performance and structural properties relevant to individual users. The approach not only provides practical benefits in storage and query runtime but also comes with theoretical guarantees on approximation error, outperforming existing summarization baselines on common KG benchmarks.

Abstract: Knowledge Graphs (KGs) are extensively used across different domains and in several applications. Often, these KGs are very large in size. Such KGs become unwieldy for tasks such as question answering and visualization. Summarization of KGs offers a viable alternative in such cases. Furthermore, personalized KG summarization is crucial in the current data-driven world as it captures the specific requirements of users based on their query patterns. Since it only maintains relevant information, the personalized summaries of KG are small, resulting in significantly smaller storage requirements and query runtime. In this work, we adapt the coreset theory to create personalized KG summaries. For a given dataset and a user-specific query workload, we present an approach that samples a relevant subset of triples using sensitivity-based importance sampling. We ensure that the subset approximates the characteristics of the full dataset with bounded approximation error. We define sensitivity scores that measure the importance of a triple with respect to a user's query workload, which are then used by our coreset construction algorithm. We explicitly focus on personalized knowledge graph summarization by constructing summaries independently for each user based on their query behaviour.
  Our evaluation on Freebase, WikiData, and DBpedia shows that COREKG delivers higher query-answering accuracy and structural coverage than the state-of-the-art methods, such as GLIMPSE, PPR, iSummary, PEGASUS and APEX$^2$ while requiring only a tiny fraction of the original graph.

### [145] [KGPFN: Unlocking the Potential of Knowledge Graph Foundation Model via In-Context Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14907)
*Yisen Gao,Jiaxin Bai,Haoyu Huang,Zhongwei Xie,Yufei Li,Hong Ting Tsang,Sirui Han,Yangqiu Song*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes KGPFN, a KG foundation model that combines transferable relational structure with large-scale in-context learning using structured local and global context.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing KG foundation models mainly focus on learning relation-level universality across graphs but under-explore in-context learning, which is crucial for foundation models to adapt to new graphs with unseen entities and relations. Moreover, KG reasoning inherently depends on both local and global structured context, which current methods do not fully leverage at inference time.

Method: KGPFN learns relation representations via message passing over relation graphs to capture cross-graph relational invariances. For each query, it encodes local neighborhoods using a multi-layer NBFNet as local context. For global context, it retrieves many instances of the query relation along with their local neighborhoods and aggregates them with a Prior-Data Fitted Network that fuses feature-level and sample-level attention, enabling inference-time in-context learning that balances reusable patterns with context-specific overrides. The model is pretrained across multiple heterogeneous KGs.

Result: On 57 KG benchmarks, KGPFN shows strong zero/few-shot adaptation to previously unseen graphs using only in-context learning, and consistently outperforms strong KG foundation baselines that rely on fine-tuning.

Conclusion: Integrating prior relational regularities with large-scale structured in-context learning enables more adaptable KG foundation models. KGPFN effectively decides when to rely on learned cross-graph patterns and when to adapt based on retrieved local/global context, yielding superior generalization to new graphs and relations compared to fine-tuned KG models.

Abstract: Knowledge graph (KG) foundation models aim to generalize across graphs with unseen entities and relations by learning transferable relational structure. However, most existing methods primarily emphasize relation-level universality, while in-context learning, the other pillar of foundation models remains under-explored for KG reasoning. In KGs, context is inherently structured and heterogeneous: effective prediction requires conditioning on the local context around the query entities as well as the global context that summarizes how a relation behaves across many instances. We propose KGPFN, a KG foundation model using Prior-data Fitted Network that unifies transferable relational regularities with inference-time in-context learning from structured context. KGPFN first learns relation representations via message passing on relation graphs to capture cross-graph relational invariances. For query-specific reasoning, it encodes local neighborhoods using a multi-layer NBFNet as local context. To enable ICL at global scale, it constructs relation-specific global context by retrieving a large set of instances of the query relation together with their local neighborhoods, and aggregates them within a Prior-Data Fitted Network framework that combines feature-level and sample-level attention. Through multi-graph pretraining on diverse KGs, KGPFN learns when to instantiate reusable patterns and when to override them using contextual evidence. Experiments on 57 KG benchmarks demonstrate that KGPFN achieves strong adaptation to previously unseen graphs through in-context learning alone, consistently outperforming competitive fine-tuned KG foundation models. Our code is available at https://github.com/HKUST-KnowComp/KGPFN.

</details>


### [146] [From Sycophantic Consensus to Pluralistic Repair: Why AI Alignment Must Surface Disagreement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14912)
*Varad Vishwarupe,Nigel Shadbolt,Marina Jirotka*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that current pluralistic alignment methods focused on preference aggregation are insufficient because RLHF models tend toward sycophantic agreement, and proposes conversational mechanisms and a new metric (Pluralistic Repair Score) to better capture principled disagreement and revision in AI assistants.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current pluralistic alignment approaches (Overton, Steerable, Distributional) aim to reflect diverse human values through preference aggregation, but deployed RLHF models often just agree with users, suppressing visible disagreement. Since these systems mediate important deliberation in health, civic life, labour, and governance, this "sycophantic consensus" becomes a structural problem affecting how disagreements and value conflicts are handled and distributed across society. The authors want to rethink what pluralistic alignment should mean in real interactions, beyond just coverage of values in training data.

Method: Conceptually, the authors reframe pluralistic alignment using three conversational mechanisms inspired by Grice's maxims: (1) scoping—explicitly acknowledging the limits and perspective of the AI; (2) signalling—making value conflicts visible instead of smoothing them away; and (3) repair—revising positions based on principled reasons instead of user pressure. Empirically, they define a quantitative metric, the Pluralistic Repair Score (PRS), to distinguish principled revision from capitulation in dialogues, and run a small-scale evaluation of two frontier RLHF models (Claude Sonnet 4.5, N=198 and GPT-4o, N=100) on contested-value prompts to measure agreement tendencies and repair quality.

Result: The empirical illustration shows that for both Claude Sonnet 4.5 and GPT-4o, there is a strong tendency to follow user agreement (sycophancy), while the quality of repair—when the model revises its position—is low on prompts involving contested values. This indicates that current RLHF models do not reliably perform principled revision when facing disagreement, even when they appear pluralistic or accommodating at the surface level.

Conclusion: Pluralistic Repair Score operationalises an interactional prerequisite for pluralism—visible disagreement and principled revision—but does not capture full pluralism itself. The authors emphasise that deciding what counts as "principled" is itself a contested and reflexive question. They argue that true pluralism in AI systems is ultimately shaped less by model training alone and more by deployment-governance choices: user interfaces, preference-data collection pipelines, and audit infrastructures, where the mechanisms of scoping, signalling, and repair can be concretely enforced and evaluated.

Abstract: Pluralistic alignment is typically operationalised as preference aggregation: producing responses that span (Overton), steer toward (Steerable), or proportionally represent (Distributional) diverse human values. We argue that aggregation alone is an incomplete primitive for deployed pluralistic alignment. Under genuine value pluralism, the failure mode of contemporary RLHF-trained assistants is not insufficient coverage but sycophantic consensus: a learned tendency to agree with, validate, and minimise friction with the immediate interlocutor. Because deployed AI systems now mediate consequential deliberation across health, civic life, labour, and governance, the collapse of disagreement at the interaction layer is not a narrow technical concern but a structural failure with distributive consequences. We reframe pluralistic alignment around three conversational mechanisms drawn from Grice's maxims: scoping (acknowledging the limits of one's perspective), signalling (surfacing value-conflict rather than smoothing it over), and repair (revising one's position on principled grounds, not on user pressure). We formalise a metric, the Pluralistic Repair Score (PRS), distinguishing principled revision from capitulation, and present a small-scale empirical illustration on two frontier RLHF-trained models (Claude Sonnet 4.5, N=198; GPT-4o, N=100) showing that, for both, agreement-following coexists with low repair-quality on contested-value prompts. PRS measures an interactional precondition for pluralism (visible disagreement; principled revision) rather than pluralism in full; we discuss the difference, take seriously the reflexive question of whose "principled" counts, and argue that pluralism is most decisively made or unmade at the deployment-governance layer: interfaces, preference-data pipelines, and audit infrastructure.

</details>


### [147] [GraphFlow: An Architecture for Formally Verifiable Visual Workflows Enabling Reliable Agentic AI Automation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.14968)
*Drewry H. Morris,Luis Valles,Reza Hosseini Ghomi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: GraphFlow is a visual, contract-checked workflow system for reliable multi-step AI and clinical automations, using diagrams as executable specs and a durable runtime with explicit trust boundaries.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-step, mission-critical workflows (e.g., in clinical settings) suffer from rapidly compounding error rates, and existing tools either lack semantic correctness guarantees or rely on brittle agentic planning via prompts. There is a need for a system that combines durable execution and observability with strong, auditable correctness properties.

Method: Define workflow diagrams as executable specifications that unify data scope, execution semantics, and monitoring; restrict to a class of diagrams that support formal contracts (preconditions, postconditions, composition obligations) which are proof-checked before workflows enter a shared library; implement a runtime engine with an append-only event log that enforces contracts at system boundaries, supports replay/retries, and uses swimlanes to mark trust boundaries between verified logic, external systems, humans, and AI components; pilot the system in clinical sites.

Result: In a year-long pilot across three clinical sites, an early prototype (without the verified-core subsystem) ran 8,728 cohort-enrolled workflows with a 97.08% completion rate, with most failures originating from external integrations rather than the workflow core itself. The formal semantics and verified-core admission model are specified but not yet empirically evaluated.

Conclusion: GraphFlow shows promise as a reliable, auditable workflow system for agentic AI in mission-critical domains, with strong runtime observability and explicit trust boundaries. While early deployments demonstrate high completion rates, the key contribution—its formally verified core and proof-checked workflow contracts—remains under active development and requires future evaluation.

Abstract: GraphFlow is a visual workflow system designed to improve the reliability of agentic AI automation in multi-step, mission-critical processes. In these workflows, small errors compound rapidly: under an idealized model of independent steps, a ten-step process with 90% per-step reliability completes successfully only 35% of the time. Existing workflow platforms provide durable execution and observability but offer few semantic correctness guarantees, while agentic systems plan at inference time, making behavior sensitive to prompt variation and difficult to audit.
  GraphFlow is designed to address this gap by treating workflow diagrams as the executable specification, a single artifact defining data scope, execution semantics, and monitoring. At compile time, a restricted class of diagrams is specified to produce reusable automations whose contracts (preconditions, postconditions, and composition obligations) are intended to be proof-checked before admission to a shared library. At runtime, a durable engine records outcomes in an append-only event log and can enforce contracts at system boundaries, supporting replay, retries, and audit. Swimlanes make trust boundaries explicit, separating verified logic from external systems, human judgment, and AI decisions.
  A year-long pilot across three clinical sites executed 8,728 cohort-enrolled workflow runs with a 97.08% completion rate under an early prototype without the verified-core subsystem; observed failures were localized primarily to external integrations. The formal semantics and proof-checked admission model described here are specified and under active development. Evaluation of the verified core is reserved for future work.

</details>

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how biological-like systems split information between initial conditions (pre-patterns) and self-organising rules, using a joint model of neural cellular automata and a learned pattern generator, and shows that co-learning both improves robustness, capacity, and symmetry breaking.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In many natural self-organising systems, crucial information about the final structure is offloaded into initial conditions rather than being created solely by local interactions. Developmental biology offers clear examples, but it is unclear how to systematically study and quantify this division of labour between initial pre-patterns and self-organisation, or what makes an effective pre-pattern.

Method: The authors introduce a computational framework combining a Neural Cellular Automaton (NCA) with a learned coordinate-based pattern generator built with SIREN (a sinusoidal representation network). Both the local update rules of the NCA and the continuous pre-patterns produced by the SIREN are trained jointly to produce a set of target patterns. They then use information-theoretic tools to quantify how much information is encoded in initial conditions versus the self-organising dynamics, and vary the strength and structure of pre-patterns to analyse their influence on development.

Result: Jointly learning both pre-patterns and self-organisation rules yields systems that are more robust to perturbations, can encode a larger set of target patterns (higher capacity), and can more effectively break symmetries than systems relying on self-organisation alone from homogeneous initial conditions. The learned pre-patterns do not simply resemble the final targets but instead encode information that shapes and biases the dynamics of the NCA to improve convergence properties.

Conclusion: The division of informational labour between initial conditions and self-organising rules is crucial for efficient pattern formation. Effective pre-patterns act less as blueprints and more as dynamical biases that scaffold development. The proposed NCA+SIREN framework provides a controlled, quantitative way to study this offloading of information and suggests that integrating designed or learned pre-patterns can substantially enhance the performance and robustness of self-organising systems in both biology-inspired modelling and artificial pattern-generation tasks.

Abstract: From subcellular structures to entire organisms, many natural systems generate complex organisation through self-organisation: local interactions that collectively give rise to global structure without any blueprint of the outcome. Yet a significant portion of the information driving such processes is not produced by self-organisation itself, instead, it is often offloaded to initial conditions of the system. Biological development is a prime example, where maternal pre-patterns encode positional and symmetry-breaking information that scaffolds the self-organising process. From maternal morphogen gradients in early embryogenesis to tissue-level morphogenetic pre-patterns guiding organ formation, this transfer of information to initial conditions, analogous to a memory-compute trade-off in computational systems, is a fundamental part of developmental processes. In this work, we study this offloading phenomenon by introducing a model that jointly learns both the self-organisation rules and the pre-patterns, allowing their interplay to be varied and measured under controlled conditions: a Neural Cellular Automaton (NCA) paired with a learned coordinate-based pattern generator (SIREN), both trained simultaneously to generate a set of patterns. We provide information-theoretic analyses of how information is distributed between pre-patterns and the self-organising process, and show that jointly learning both components yields improvements in robustness, encoding capacity, and symmetry breaking over purely self-organising alternatives. Our analysis further suggests that effective pre-patterns do not simply approximate their targets; rather, they bias the developmental dynamics in ways that facilitate convergence, pointing to a non-trivial relationship between the structure of initial conditions and the dynamics of self-organisation.

### [149] [Dual-Dimensional Consistency: Balancing Budget and Quality in Adaptive Inference-Time Scaling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15100)
*Rongman Xu,Yifei Li,Tianzhe Zhao,Yanrui Wu,Bo Li,Hang Yan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Dual-Dimensional Consistency (DDC), a framework that improves LLM reasoning efficiency by jointly optimizing sampling width and depth, cutting token usage by over 10× while maintaining or improving accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Inference-time scaling for LLM reasoning is powerful but inefficient: existing methods independently expand sampling width (multiple reasoning paths) and depth (longer chains-of-thought). Width-based consensus can amplify shared hallucinations, and depth-based pruning can terminate valid but complex reasoning too early. The motivation is to design a principled way to allocate compute adaptively across both dimensions to get better reasoning quality per token.

Method: The authors propose Dual-Dimensional Consistency (DDC), which jointly manages width and depth via two components: (1) a Confidence-Weighted Bayesian protocol that evaluates and updates the estimated quality of different reasoning paths, guiding which paths to continue; and (2) a Trend-Aware Stratified Pruning mechanism that monitors how each path’s quality evolves over time, pruning low-promise or deteriorating paths while keeping promising but complex ones. This couples path-scoring with adaptive stopping so that generation focuses on high-quality chains and reaches consensus faster.

Result: On five different reasoning benchmarks and across multiple LLM backbones, DDC significantly improves efficiency, reducing token consumption by more than 10× compared to strong baseline inference-time scaling methods, while matching or surpassing their accuracy. This indicates better compute-accuracy trade-offs and more robust handling of hallucinations.

Conclusion: Jointly optimizing sampling width and depth through a probabilistic, trend-aware control mechanism can substantially improve LLM reasoning efficiency. DDC shows that integrating confidence-weighted Bayesian scoring with adaptive, trajectory-sensitive pruning allows models to concentrate compute on high-quality reasoning paths, filter out hallucinations, and achieve fast, accurate consensus with far fewer tokens.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have demonstrated remarkable abilities in reasoning. However, maximizing their potential through inference-time scaling faces challenges in trade-off between sampling budget and reasoning quality. Current strategies remain inefficient as they typically treat sampling width and depth as orthogonal objectives, where width consensus methods risk reinforcing hallucinations, while depth pruning mechanisms prematurely truncate complex yet valid reasoning chains. Therefore, we propose Dual-Dimensional Consistency (DDC), a unified framework that bridges path quality with adaptive termination. By coupling Confidence-Weighted Bayesian protocol with a Trend-Aware Stratified Pruning, our method ensures that computational resources are concentrated on high quality reasoning paths, filtering hallucinations while accelerating consensus. Evaluations across five benchmarks demonstrate that this approach reduces token consumption by over 10 times while maintaining or exceeding the accuracy of strong baselines across various LLMs.

### [150] [Why Neighborhoods Matter: Traversal Context and Provenance in Agentic GraphRAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15109)
*Riccardo Terrenzi,Maximilian von Zastrow,Serkan Ayvaz*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to correctly evaluate and interpret citations in Agentic GraphRAG systems, showing that faithful citations must reflect the entire retrieval trajectory, not just the final supporting sources.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In Retrieval-Augmented Generation, citations are used to justify answers, but Agentic GraphRAG agents traverse complex knowledge graphs before answering. This makes it unclear whether a small final set of citations truly reflects all the information and structure that influenced the answer. The authors aim to clarify what “faithful” citations mean in this setting and how to evaluate them.

Method: They model citation faithfulness as a trajectory-level property that should reflect the agent’s full graph exploration. Using controlled ablation experiments on knowledge graphs, they systematically isolate, remove, or mask different subsets of graph entities—both cited and uncited—to see how these manipulations affect answers and accuracy.

Result: The experiments show that cited nodes are often necessary: removing cited evidence typically alters answers and lowers accuracy. However, uncited elements of the traversal and the surrounding graph structure can also be critical, since modifying them can change answers even when cited evidence remains available. Thus, both cited and uncited trajectory components can materially influence model outputs.

Conclusion: In Agentic GraphRAG, faithful citations cannot be assessed only by checking if the final sources support the answer. Instead, evaluation should consider provenance over the entire retrieval trajectory, including visited-but-uncited entities and graph structure that shape the reasoning process. Citation schemes and metrics should be redesigned with this broader notion of faithfulness in mind.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation can improve factuality by grounding answers in external evidence, but Agentic GraphRAG complicates what it means for citations to be faithful. In these systems, an agent explores a knowledge graph before producing an answer and a small set of citations. We frame citation faithfulness as a trajectory-level problem: final citations should not only support the answer, but also account for the graph traversal, structure, and visited-but-uncited entities that may influence it. Through controlled ablation experiments, we compare the effects of isolating, removing, and masking cited and uncited graph entities. Our results show that cited evidence is often necessary, as removing it substantially changes answers and reduces accuracy. However, citations are not sufficient, because accurate answers can also depend on uncited traversal context and surrounding graph structure. These findings suggest that citation evaluation in Agentic GraphRAG should move beyond source support toward provenance over the broader retrieval trajectory.

</details>


### [152] [OpenDeepThink: Parallel Reasoning via Bradley--Terry Aggregation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15177)
*Shang Zhou,Wenhao Chai,Kaiyuan Liu,Huanzhi Mao,Qiuyang Mang,Jingbo Shang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: OpenDeepThink is a test-time compute framework that improves LLM reasoning by evolving a population of candidate solutions using pairwise Bradley-Terry comparisons instead of noisy single-solution judging, yielding large performance gains on Codeforces and transferring across models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While increasing test-time compute by generating longer reasoning traces helps LLM reasoning, scaling by breadth (multiple parallel candidates) suffers from a selection bottleneck because LLM-based pointwise evaluation is noisy and biased without ground-truth verifiers. There is a need for a more reliable selection mechanism that can exploit multiple candidates effectively and work across models and domains.

Method: OpenDeepThink maintains a population of candidate solutions and iteratively refines them. In each generation, the LLM performs random pairwise comparisons of candidate solutions, producing natural-language critiques. These pairwise preferences are aggregated using a Bradley-Terry model to obtain a global ranking over candidates. The top-ranked candidates are kept; the top three quarters are mutated using the critiques to generate improved variants; the lowest quarter is discarded. This process is repeated for several LLM-call rounds, effectively scaling test-time compute via population-based search and pairwise comparison-based selection instead of pointwise scoring.

Result: On Codeforces-style competitive programming problems, OpenDeepThink improves Gemini 3.1 Pro's effective Elo rating by about 405 points after eight sequential LLM-call rounds (~27 minutes). The approach transfers to both weaker and stronger LLMs without needing to retune the pipeline. On the multi-domain HLE benchmark, improvements are concentrated in objectively verifiable domains (like code or math), whereas performance can deteriorate in more subjective domains, indicating domain-dependent behavior.

Conclusion: Population-based test-time compute with pairwise Bradley-Terry comparison offers a powerful way to leverage multiple candidate solutions, overcoming the selection bottleneck in breadth scaling. OpenDeepThink substantially boosts LLM performance on objectively evaluable tasks and generalizes across base models, though its benefits may not extend to subjective tasks. The released CF-73 dataset further supports rigorous evaluation in competitive programming scenarios.

Abstract: Test-time compute scaling is a primary axis for improving LLM reasoning. Existing methods primarily scale depth by extending a single reasoning trace. Scaling breadth by sampling multiple candidates in parallel is straightforward, but introduces a selection bottleneck: choosing the best candidate without a ground-truth verifier, since pointwise LLM judging is noisy and biased. To address this, we introduce OpenDeepThink, a population-based test-time compute framework that selects via pairwise Bradley-Terry comparison. Each generation, the LLM judges random pairs of candidates and aggregates votes via Bradley-Terry into a global ranking; top-ranked candidates are preserved and the top three quarters are mutated using the natural-language critiques produced during comparison; the bottom quarter is discarded. OpenDeepThink raises Gemini 3.1 Pro's effective Codeforces Elo by +405 points in eight sequential LLM-call rounds (~27 minutes wall-clock). The pipeline transfers across weaker and stronger models without retuning, and on the multi-domain HLE benchmark, gains appear concentrated in objectively verifiable domains and reverse in subjective ones. We release CF-73, a curated set of 73 expert-rated Codeforces problems with International Grandmaster annotation and 99% local-evaluation agreement against the official verdict.

</details>
